The First Reductio: Collapsing Events into One Day
Presents the first reductio: the day of flood example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how assuming the eternity of time leads to the absurd conclusion that distinct historical events collapse into the same moment.

Core Idea
Eternal time forces all events into a single eternal present.

The first argument against the reality of time as an eternal substance uses a concrete example: if a specific day today were identical to the day of the flood, then any event occurring today would have occurred then. This shows the absurdity of treating time as an independent entity. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Notice the starting assumption: if this day is the day of the flood. What follows from that?
The author uses a consequence that is contradictory.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that this leads to a contradiction (خلف), so the assumption is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجب أن يكون هذا اليوم بعينه هو يوم الطوفان، فيلزم على هذا التقدير أن يكون الحادث في هذا اليوم حادثاً في يوم الطوفان، بل قبله بل أزلاً. هذا خلف. محال. وأيضاً: فبتقدير كونه دائم الوجود بعينه، فلا بد وأن يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل، وسيبقى موجوداً بعد. فلو كان هذا المفهوم يقتضي إثبات مدة وزمان لذلك الشيء، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار المدة والزمان إلى مدة أخرى، وزمان آخر. ويلزم التسلسل وهو محال. والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأنه يقتضي أن تكون الأجزاء المفترضة فيه متعاقبة متوالية، وحينئذ يصح في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء أن يقال: إنه حدث الآن، لا قبل ولا بعد. فلو كان الحكم على الشيء بأنه حدث الآن، أو كان حادثاً قبل ذلك، أو سيحدث بعد ذلك، يقتضي شيئاً آخر يكون ظرفاً له، ويكون لأجل وقوع هذا الشيء في ذلك الظرف، يصدق عليه بأنه حصل الآن، أو في الماضي، أو سيحصل في المستقبل، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر إلى غير النهاية، وذلك محال.
It would be necessary that this very day be the day of the flood, so upon this supposition it would follow that the event occurring on this day occurred on the flood day, rather before it, rather eternally. This is a contradiction. Impossible. And also: upon the supposition that it is perpetually existent identically, it must be true of it that it existed before and will continue to exist after. If this concept entails establishing a duration and time for that thing, then it would necessitate that the duration and time depend on another duration and another time, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The second [division] is also false, because it requires that the parts posited in it be successive and consecutive, and then it is valid for each one of those parts to be said: it occurs now, not before nor after. If the judgment upon a thing that it occurs now, or was occurring before, or will occur after, requires another thing that is its container, and because of the occurrence of this thing in that container it is described as having occurred now, or in the past, or will occur in the future, then it would necessitate that time has another time ad infinitum, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents two reductio arguments against the reality of time as an eternal entity. The first argument begins from a conditional: if a particular day were the very day of the flood, then any event occurring today would have occurred on the flood day, or even before that, or eternally — which is a contradiction (خلف). Then it introduces a second argument (وأيضاً): if time is perpetual existence and that concept implies a duration (مدة) and time (زمان) for that thing, then that duration and time would themselves require another duration and time, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). The author then considers a second division (والثاني): if time is composed of successive parts, each part can be said to occur 'now'. But if the judgment 'now' requires a container time, then that container also requires another time, again leading to infinite regress. Both arguments aim to show that the very concept of time as an independent entity leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
زمان
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first argument conclude from the assumption that this day is the day of the flood?
A) Time is real.
B) It leads to a contradiction.
C) The flood never happened.
D) Days are distinct.

Key Takeaway
Assuming time as an eternal entity collapses distinct historical moments into one, which is contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that assuming eternal time would make today the same as the day of the flood, did you feel the logical absurdity? That contradiction is meant to protect the distinctness of every moment. How will you ensure your understanding of time really acknowledges that every moment is a unique creation of Allah, not just a part of an eternal flow?
?
The author uses a reductio to expose a hidden contradiction in a widespread assumption. What is one assumption you hold about Allah that you have never tested with such logical rigor? Commit today to tracing its implications to see if it leads to a contradiction.
?
This argument shows that a wrong belief about something as abstract as time can collapse the reality of distinct historical events. Consider a belief you hold about your own past—does it allow each moment its unique relationship to Allah? Are you willing to let this argument push you to a more precise and humble understanding?

The Second Reductio: Infinite Regress of Time
Presents two sub-arguments against eternal time leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of time as an independent container leads to an infinite regress of times.

Core Idea
If time requires a container, that container would also need a container ad infinitum.

The second argument tackles the idea of time as a duration or container. If we say something exists perpetually, and that existence implies a duration and time, then that time itself would require another duration, and so on, leading to an infinite regress. Similarly, if time is composed of successive parts, each part's 'now' would require another time. This lesson unpacks these two sub-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second argument.
Notice it has two parts: the perpetual existence argument and the successive parts argument.
2
Understand the first sub-argument
If time is perpetual existence, then it itself would need a duration, leading to regress.
3
Understand the second sub-argument
If time has parts, each part's 'now' requires a container time, again leading to regress.
Both point to infinite regress (تسلسل) which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجب أن يكون هذا اليوم بعينه هو يوم الطوفان، فيلزم على هذا التقدير أن يكون الحادث في هذا اليوم حادثاً في يوم الطوفان، بل قبله بل أزلاً. هذا خلف. محال. وأيضاً: فبتقدير كونه دائم الوجود بعينه، فلا بد وأن يصدق عليه أنه كان موجوداً قبل، وسيبقى موجوداً بعد. فلو كان هذا المفهوم يقتضي إثبات مدة وزمان لذلك الشيء، فحينئذ يلزم افتقار المدة والزمان إلى مدة أخرى، وزمان آخر. ويلزم التسلسل وهو محال. والثاني أيضاً باطل، لأنه يقتضي أن تكون الأجزاء المفترضة فيه متعاقبة متوالية، وحينئذ يصح في كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء أن يقال: إنه حدث الآن، لا قبل ولا بعد. فلو كان الحكم على الشيء بأنه حدث الآن، أو كان حادثاً قبل ذلك، أو سيحدث بعد ذلك، يقتضي شيئاً آخر يكون ظرفاً له، ويكون لأجل وقوع هذا الشيء في ذلك الظرف، يصدق عليه بأنه حصل الآن، أو في الماضي، أو سيحصل في المستقبل، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر إلى غير النهاية، وذلك محال.
It would be necessary that this very day be the day of the flood, so upon this supposition it would follow that the event occurring on this day occurred on the flood day, rather before it, rather eternally. This is a contradiction. Impossible. And also: upon the supposition that it is perpetually existent identically, it must be true of it that it existed before and will continue to exist after. If this concept entails establishing a duration and time for that thing, then it would necessitate that the duration and time depend on another duration and another time, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. The second [division] is also false, because it requires that the parts posited in it be successive and consecutive, and then it is valid for each one of those parts to be said: it occurs now, not before nor after. If the judgment upon a thing that it occurs now, or was occurring before, or will occur after, requires another thing that is its container, and because of the occurrence of this thing in that container it is described as having occurred now, or in the past, or will occur in the future, then it would necessitate that time has another time ad infinitum, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage presents two reductio arguments against the reality of time as an eternal entity. The first argument begins from a conditional: if a particular day were the very day of the flood, then any event occurring today would have occurred on the flood day, or even before that, or eternally — which is a contradiction (خلف). Then it introduces a second argument (وأيضاً): if time is perpetual existence and that concept implies a duration (مدة) and time (زمان) for that thing, then that duration and time would themselves require another duration and time, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). The author then considers a second division (والثاني): if time is composed of successive parts, each part can be said to occur 'now'. But if the judgment 'now' requires a container time, then that container also requires another time, again leading to infinite regress. Both arguments aim to show that the very concept of time as an independent entity leads to absurdity.

Key Terms
مدة
تسلسل
ظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an independent, eternal container in which events occur.
Correction: The arguments show that such a concept leads to infinite regress, so it cannot be real.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of assuming that the concept of perpetual existence requires a time for that existence?
A) It proves time is real.
B) It leads to infinite regress.
C) It shows time has parts.
D) It proves time is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Assuming time as an independent substance leads inevitably to an infinite regress of times, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if time is a separate force that controls you—rushing you, slowing you down, or stealing moments? After reading this argument, if time is not an independent entity but a measure of change, how might that reshape your relationship with deadlines and waiting? What would change today if you truly stopped treating time as a real container?
?
The passage shows that assuming time as a container leads to an infinite regress. What 'containers' do you rely on in your own thinking—categories like past, present, future—that might be just mental constructs? Are you willing to examine one such container today and see it as a mental label rather than reality?
?
If time cannot be an independent substance, then the idea of 'losing time' or 'running out of time' loses its absolute grip. Think of one regret you hold about wasted time. Can you let go of that regret now, knowing that time is not a thing to be wasted but a relation between events? Will you commit to living this day with that freedom?

The Indivisible Present: Time as Successive Moments
Introduces the dichotomy and deduces the indivisibility of the present moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dichotomy of the second argument and the reasoning that leads to time being composed of indivisible moments.

Core Idea
If time's parts are successive, each present moment must be indivisible.

This lesson begins the second proof against real time. The author starts with the necessary fact that time's parts are successive, then poses a crucial either-or. By examining the first option, he shows that any present moment must be indivisible, leading to the conclusion that time is a series of dimensionless nows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the author's style of proof and the key terms: آن, تعاقب, انقسام.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the logic.
2
Identify the dichotomy
The author presents a dichotomy: either some part of time has presence or none does. Focus on the first horn.
The second horn (none present) is not discussed here but implies time is non-existent.
3
Understand the indivisibility argument
If a part has presence, it must be indivisible. Otherwise it would have halves, the first half would precede the second, and the present would not be present—a contradiction.
This shows that 'presence' requires indivisibility.
4
Grasp the conclusion about time
Since each present part is indivisible and time extends by their succession, time is composed of successive indivisible moments (آنات).
This conclusion is the basis for the next lesson's argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الأجزاء المفترضة في الزمان لا تحصل معاً، بل حصولها لا يعقل إلا على سبيل التعاقب والتقضي. فنقول: إما أن يكون لشيء من تلك الأجزاء حضور وحصول، أو لا يكون، فإن كان الأول كان واحد من تلك الأجزاء حال حضوره غير منقسم وإلا لحصل له نصفان، ويكون النصف الأول سابقاً على النصف الثاني وحينئذ لا يكون الحاضر حاضراً. هذا خلف. وإذا ثبت أن كل واحد من تلك الأشياء غير قابل للقسمة - ولا شك أن الزمان إنما يمتد بسبب تعاقب تلك الأشياء الحاضرة - لزم كون الزمان مركباً من الآنات المتتالية التي يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة ولو كان الأمر كذلك لزم كون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، لأن القدر الذي يتحرك عليه المتحرك من المسافة في الآن الواحد، الذي لا يقبل القسمة - إن كان منقسماً - كانت الحركة إلى نصفه واقعة في نصف ذلك الآن، والحركة الواقعة في النصف الثاني من ذلك الآن، فحينئذ ينتصف ذلك الآن وكنا قد فرضنا أنه لا ينتصف. هذا خلف. فثبت أن القول بكون الزمان مركباً من أشياء حاضرة، كل واحد منها لا ينقسم، يفضي إلى إثبات الجوهر الفرد، وهو باطل. فكان القول بوجود الزمان باطلا.
The second proof: We know necessarily that the parts posited in time do not occur together, rather their occurrence is not conceivable except in the manner of succession and passing away. So we say: Either some of those parts have presence and occurrence, or not. If the first, then one of those parts at the moment of its presence is indivisible—otherwise it would have two halves, and the first half would be prior to the second half, and then the present would not be present. This is a contradiction. And if it is established that each one of those things is indivisible—and there is no doubt that time extends only because of the succession of those present things—then time must be composed of successive moments (ānāt) each of which is indivisible. If that were the case, then body must be composed of indivisible parts, because the distance that the moving body traverses in a single indivisible moment—if it were divisible, then the motion to its half would occur in half of that moment, and the motion occurring in the second half of that moment would then divide that moment, whereas we had assumed it does not divide. This is a contradiction. So it is established that the statement that time is composed of present things, each indivisible, leads to the affirmation of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is false. Thus, the statement of the existence of time is false.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a second proof (الحجة الثانية) against the existence of time as an independent reality. It begins by stating a necessary truth: the parts of time do not exist simultaneously but only successively. The argument then poses a dichotomy: either some of these parts have actual presence (حضور وحصول) or none do. If some do, then such a part must be indivisible—otherwise it would have halves and the present moment would not be truly present. If it is indivisible, then time is composed of indivisible moments (آنات). The author then shows that this leads to a further conclusion: if time is made of indivisible moments, then motion and space must also be composed of indivisible parts (the atom). This is demonstrated by considering a moving object: if the distance traversed in an indivisible moment were divisible, the moment itself would be divided—contradicting the assumption. Since the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) is considered false in this tradition, the original assumption that time exists is also false. The second horn of the dichotomy (that no part has presence) outright denies time, which is also unacceptable. Thus the proof refutes the reality of time.

Key Terms
آن
تعاقب
الجوهر الفرد
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the psychological experience of time.
Correction: It is about logical consequences of positing time as a real continuum.

Checkpoint
What is the first conclusion if a part of time has presence?
A) It is divisible.
B) It is indivisible.
C) It does not exist.
D) It is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The first part of the argument establishes that if time has any present part, that part must be indivisible, making time a series of moments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the present moment is indivisible and you are only ever truly present in this instant, then how much of your prayer—your standing, bowing, prostrating—are you actually present for? Will you take a single rakat today and fight to keep your mind from wandering, from dividing the moment?
?
You plan your days, your years, your life—but each plan rests on a chain of moments that you do not own. This argument says time is nothing but the passing of indivisible presents. Are you living as if each moment is a gift you will have to account for, or as if it is a resource you can waste? What will you change today to treat each moment as if it were your last?

From Moments to Atoms: The Refutation of Time
Extends the argument to motion, derives atomism, and concludes the falsity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the composition of time from moments leads to the false doctrine of atomism, thereby refuting time's reality.

Core Idea
If time is moments, then motion forces matter into atoms, which is false.

The second part of the proof shows the fatal consequence of accepting that time is made of indivisible moments. By analyzing motion through space, the author demonstrates that it leads to atomism, which is rejected. Thus the original assumption that time exists collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the chain of reasoning from time as moments to atomism.
This builds on the conclusion of the previous lesson.
2
Follow the motion argument
Consider a moving body traversing a distance in an indivisible moment. If the distance were divisible, the moment would be divided, contradicting its indivisibility.
This shows that the distance must be indivisible as well.
3
Understand the link to atomism
If every distance traversed in an indivisible moment is indivisible, then all bodies are composed of such indivisible parts (الجوهر الفرد).
This is the atomism of the kalam tradition.
4
Grasp the final refutation
Atomism is considered false (باطل) in this context, so the assumption that time exists must also be false.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إنا نعلم بالضرورة أن الأجزاء المفترضة في الزمان لا تحصل معاً، بل حصولها لا يعقل إلا على سبيل التعاقب والتقضي. فنقول: إما أن يكون لشيء من تلك الأجزاء حضور وحصول، أو لا يكون، فإن كان الأول كان واحد من تلك الأجزاء حال حضوره غير منقسم وإلا لحصل له نصفان، ويكون النصف الأول سابقاً على النصف الثاني وحينئذ لا يكون الحاضر حاضراً. هذا خلف. وإذا ثبت أن كل واحد من تلك الأشياء غير قابل للقسمة - ولا شك أن الزمان إنما يمتد بسبب تعاقب تلك الأشياء الحاضرة - لزم كون الزمان مركباً من الآنات المتتالية التي يكون كل واحد منها غير قابل للقسمة ولو كان الأمر كذلك لزم كون الجسم مركباً من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ، لأن القدر الذي يتحرك عليه المتحرك من المسافة في الآن الواحد، الذي لا يقبل القسمة - إن كان منقسماً - كانت الحركة إلى نصفه واقعة في نصف ذلك الآن، والحركة الواقعة في النصف الثاني من ذلك الآن، فحينئذ ينتصف ذلك الآن وكنا قد فرضنا أنه لا ينتصف. هذا خلف. فثبت أن القول بكون الزمان مركباً من أشياء حاضرة، كل واحد منها لا ينقسم، يفضي إلى إثبات الجوهر الفرد، وهو باطل. فكان القول بوجود الزمان باطلا.
The second proof: We know necessarily that the parts posited in time do not occur together, rather their occurrence is not conceivable except in the manner of succession and passing away. So we say: Either some of those parts have presence and occurrence, or not. If the first, then one of those parts at the moment of its presence is indivisible—otherwise it would have two halves, and the first half would be prior to the second half, and then the present would not be present. This is a contradiction. And if it is established that each one of those things is indivisible—and there is no doubt that time extends only because of the succession of those present things—then time must be composed of successive moments (ānāt) each of which is indivisible. If that were the case, then body must be composed of indivisible parts, because the distance that the moving body traverses in a single indivisible moment—if it were divisible, then the motion to its half would occur in half of that moment, and the motion occurring in the second half of that moment would then divide that moment, whereas we had assumed it does not divide. This is a contradiction. So it is established that the statement that time is composed of present things, each indivisible, leads to the affirmation of the indivisible atom (al-jawhar al-fard), which is false. Thus, the statement of the existence of time is false.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a second proof (الحجة الثانية) against the existence of time as an independent reality. It begins by stating a necessary truth: the parts of time do not exist simultaneously but only successively. The argument then poses a dichotomy: either some of these parts have actual presence (حضور وحصول) or none do. If some do, then such a part must be indivisible—otherwise it would have halves and the present moment would not be truly present. If it is indivisible, then time is composed of indivisible moments (آنات). The author then shows that this leads to a further conclusion: if time is made of indivisible moments, then motion and space must also be composed of indivisible parts (the atom). This is demonstrated by considering a moving object: if the distance traversed in an indivisible moment were divisible, the moment itself would be divided—contradicting the assumption. Since the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد) is considered false in this tradition, the original assumption that time exists is also false. The second horn of the dichotomy (that no part has presence) outright denies time, which is also unacceptable. Thus the proof refutes the reality of time.

Key Terms

تعاقب
الجوهر الفرد
انقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that time does not exist in any sense.
Correction: It only proves that a particular conception of time (as a real continuum) fails.

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the indivisible atom (الجوهر الفرد)?
A) It is necessary for time to exist.
B) It is proven to exist.
C) It is false.
D) It is the basis of motion.

Key Takeaway
The proof concludes that positing time as composed of moments entails atomism, which is false, so time does not exist as a real entity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that assuming time is a real thing leads to an absurd conclusion. How often do you treat time as an objective reality without questioning? Are you willing to reconsider your own assumptions about time, even if it feels unsettling?
?
The author uses logical reasoning to overturn a common belief. What is one belief you hold that you have never subjected to rigorous logical scrutiny? Would you be prepared to abandon it if it led to a contradiction?

The Third Argument: Time and Priority
Sanusi presents a dichotomy on time's nature and argues that priority is independent of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the traditional argument refutes the necessity of time for the concept of priority, and why both possibilities lead to the same conclusion.

Core Idea
Priority does not depend on time; it is a rational concept.

This passage presents a third proof against the reality of time. The author examines two possibilities—time is originated or eternal—and shows that in either case, priority (قبلية) can exist without time. This challenges the assumption that time is a necessary framework for sequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the dichotomy
Read the Arabic passage carefully with its translation. Notice that the argument begins by dividing the nature of time into two possibilities: originated (حادث) or eternal (قديم).
The passage is incomplete, but the core argument is present.
2
Analyze the first branch: time originated
If time is originated, then before its existence there was non-existence. The priority of that non-existence over time cannot be temporal because time did not yet exist. Therefore priority exists without time.
This shows that priority is a relationship of non-being to being, not a temporal measurement.
3
Consider the second branch: time eternal
The author states that if time is eternal, it is false (باطل) because time's existence depends on the succession of priorities and posteriorities—which is change. However, even if we grant eternity, the desired conclusion (that priority is independent) is still achieved.
The passage ends abruptly; the full refutation of eternal time is not given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: إن هذا الزمان. إما أن يكون حادثاً، أو قديماً. فإن كان حادثاً كان عدمه قبل وجوده، وهذه القبلية لا تكون بالزمان، لأن هذا الكلام إنما وقع في العدم الذي هو متقدم على وجود كل الزمان، وعند حصول هذا العدم لا يكون للزمان وجود فقد حصل معنى القبلية والتقدم من غير حصول الزمان، فعلمنا أن حصول القبلية والتقدم، لا يتوقف على وجود الزمان. وأما إن قلنا: الزمان قديم. فنقول: هذا باطل. وبتقدير تسليمه، فالمقصود حاصل. أما أنه باطل فلان وجود الزمان لا يتقرر إلا عند توالي القبليات والبعديات وهذا التوالي والتعاقب هو التغير والتغير ماهيته،
The third proof: This time is either originated or eternal. If it is originated, then its non-existence is before its existence, and this priority (al-qabliyya) is not by time, because this discourse concerns the non-existence that is prior to the existence of all time, and at the occurrence of this non-existence time does not exist. So the meaning of priority and anteriority occurs without the occurrence of time. Thus we know that the occurrence of priority and anteriority does not depend on the existence of time. But if we say: time is eternal, then we say: this is false. And even if we grant it, the purpose is achieved. As for its being false, it is because the existence of time is not established except upon the succession of priorities and posteriorities, and this succession and sequence is change, and change is its quiddity.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a third proof (الحجة الثالثة) against the reality of time. It presents a dichotomy: time is either originated (حادث) or eternal (قديم). If time is originated, then prior to its existence there was non-existence, and that priority (قبلية) cannot be temporal because time did not yet exist. Hence priority can exist without time. If time is eternal, the author says that is false (باطل), because the existence of time is defined by the succession of priorities and posteriorities, and succession itself is change (تغير). The argument seems to imply that if time is eternal, its essence is change, which may lead to further problems. The passage ends abruptly, but the point is that in either case, time is not a necessary condition for priority and posteriority. This refutes the idea that time is a real, independent entity.

Key Terms
حادث
قديم
قبلية
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that priority and posteriority cannot exist without time, or that time is a necessary container for all sequence.
Correction: The passage argues that priority (قبلية) can exist without time, as shown by the non-existence before the creation of time. Time is not an external reality required for the concept of 'before'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the priority of non-existence before time's existence not require time?
A) Because time is eternal.
B) Because non-existence is timeless.
C) Because priority is a concept independent of time.
D) Because time does not exist.

Key Takeaway
Priority (قبلية) is a rational concept that does not require the existence of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you consider your most important life decisions, do you base them on rational necessity or on temporal urgency? What would change if you let reason, not the clock, set your priorities starting today?
?
You habitually say, 'I don't have time,' as if time were a real container that can be full or empty. This passage shows that time may not be real in the way you assume. How does that realization challenge your excuses for neglecting your duties toward Allah? Will you continue to use time as a shield, or will you face the fact that priority exists even where time does not?
?
The author says priority can occur without time. Think of a sin you committed 'in the past.' Does your guilt depend on the time that passed, or on the rational priority of the act itself? Are you using the passage of time to diminish your responsibility? Repentance requires you to acknowledge the priority of the sin, not its temporal distance. Will you act on that now?

Time Cannot Be Quantity: The Ninth Argument
Argues that if time had quiddity, it would be quantity, but it cannot be continuous quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ninth argument denies that time can be a real entity by showing it cannot be classified as quantity.

Core Idea
Time fails to fit into any category of quantity, so its existence is impossible.

In this lesson, we examine a philosophical argument that attempts to disprove the reality of time by analyzing its hypothetical quiddity. The argument proceeds by showing that if time had an essence, it would have to be quantity, but then it would have to be either continuous or discrete — both of which are impossible for time to be. The passage focuses on the rejection of continuous quantity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if time had essence, then it must be quantity...'
The passage is incomplete; the refutation of discrete quantity is missing.
2
Identify why time would be quantity
Look for the properties that make time qualify as quantity: equality, inequality, divisibility, measurability.
These properties (l- musawa, l-tajazzi) are specific to quantity in Aristotelian categories.
3
Examine the two types of quantity
The passage states that quantity is either continuous (muttaṣil) or discrete (munfaṣil). Time cannot be continuous because it divides into past and future, which do not share a common boundary.
Continuous quantity, like a line, splits at a shared boundary (e.g., a point). Time's present is not a shared boundary but a fleeting instant.
4
Understand the incomplete conclusion
The passage cuts off after rejecting continuous quantity. It would next reject discrete quantity, then conclude that time cannot be quantity, so its existence is false.
This argument is one of several in the chapter; later sections may complete it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة في هذا الباب : لو كان للزمان والمدة ماهية وحقيقة لوجب أن تكون ماهيته هو الكم. وذلك لأنه قابل للمساواة ، واللامساواة والتجزى ويمكن فرض جزء فيه ، ويقدره وهذا هو خاصية الكم ، ولو كان كماً، لكان إما أن يكون كماً متصلاً ، أو كماً منفصلاً ، والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بوجوده. وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يمكن أن يكون كما متصلاً ، وذلك لأن الكم المتصل هو الذي ينقسم إلى جزءين مشتركين في حد واحد بعينه لكن الزمان منقسم إلى الماضي ، وإلى المستقبل ، وهما
The ninth argument in this chapter: If time and duration had an essence and reality, it would be necessary that their essence be quantity. That is because they admit of equality, inequality, and divisibility, and a part can be assumed in it, and it can be measured, and this is the property of quantity. And if it were quantity, it would either be continuous quantity or discrete quantity. And both divisions are false, so the claim of its existence is false. And we only say that it cannot be continuous quantity, because continuous quantity is that which divides into two parts sharing one and the same boundary, but time divides into past and future, and they are...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation argument attempting to disprove the existence of time by analyzing its hypothetical quiddity. The author argues that if time had an essence, it would have to be quantity (al-kamm), because time exhibits properties of quantity: equality, inequality, divisibility, and measurability. Then it would fall into either continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) or discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil). Both are impossible for time: it cannot be continuous because continuous quantity splits at a common boundary, but time divides into past and future which share no boundary. The argument is incomplete at page's end, but the intended conclusion is that time cannot be quantity, so its existence is impossible.

Key Terms
كم (quantity)
كم متصل (continuous quantity)
كم منفصل (discrete quantity)
مساواة (equality)
تجزى (divisibility)
ماهية (quiddity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is self-evidently existent and does not need a quiddity.
Correction: The argument assumes that for something to be real, it must have an essence that fits a category. By showing time cannot be quantity, it challenges its reality.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth argument, why can time not be continuous quantity?
A) Because continuous quantity is divisible into parts sharing a boundary, but time divides into past and future which do not share a boundary.
B) Because time is not measurable.
C) Because time is discrete quantity.
D) Because time does not admit equality or inequality.

Key Takeaway
The ninth argument attempts to disprove time's existence by showing that its hypothetical quiddity cannot be quantity, but the argument is incomplete here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time has no real essence, what does that imply for how you should view the present moment? Are you living in constant preoccupation with past and future, treating them as real entities? How can you shift your focus to the eternal?
?
The argument shows that the past and future do not share a boundary. How does this challenge your tendency to regret the past or worry about the future? What would it mean for you to live in the present as the only real moment?
?
If time were a real entity, it would have to be quantity, but it fails. Does your attachment to time as a resource reflect a deeper attachment to something that may not even exist? How can you detach from the illusion of time and increase your reliance on the Timeless?

The Priority of Non-Existence and the Nature of Time
The twelfth proof based on the intuitive axiom that non-existence precedes existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the self-evident priority of non-existence leads to the conclusion that time and duration are not necessarily existent entities.

Core Idea
Priority of non-existence is self-evident; time is just a mental construct.

This lesson examines the twelfth proof, which begins with an axiomatic truth: the non-existence of every created thing precedes its existence. From this, the author builds a chain of reasoning to challenge the necessary existence of time. As you read the passage, pay attention to how each step follows logically from the initial axiom.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the self-evident axiom
The proof starts with a claim that the non-existence of every originated thing is prior to its existence. This is presented as intuitively evident, and anyone who denies it disputes the most evident of intuitive sciences.
This axiom is the foundation of the entire proof.
2
Understand the first implication
From the axiom, the author argues that priority is an attribute of non-existence. Since non-existence itself is non-existent, its attribute is also non-existent. Therefore, priority, antecedence, and posteriority have no external existence.
This step shows that temporal relations are not real entities.
3
Apply the logic to time
The author then defines time as that which is characterized by priority, antecedence, and posteriority. Since these relations are not existent, time and duration need not be existent matters.
This challenges the notion that time is a real, objective entity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that time and duration are not necessarily existent. This has implications for understanding creation and the temporal order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : إن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن عدم كل محدث سابق على وجوده ، ومن نازع فيه فقد نازع في أجلى العلوم البديهية ، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : السبق صفة للعدم وصفة المعدوم لا تكون موجودة بل تكون معدومة ، فهذا السبق والتقدم والتأخر ، أمور لا وجود لها في الأعيان البنة والعدم المحض لا يستدعي محلاً موجوداً ، فهذا السبق والتقدم والتأخر لا يحتاج شيء منها إلى موصوف موجود والزمان لا حقيقة له إلا الأمر الذي يعرض له هذا السبق وهذا التقدم، وهذا التأخر . وذلك ينتج : أن الزمان والمدة لا يجب أن يكون أمراً موجوداً .
The twelfth proof: The intuition of reason testifies that the non-existence of every originated thing is prior to its existence. Whoever disputes this has disputed the most evident of intuitive sciences. And when this is established, we say: Priority is an attribute of non-existence, and the attribute of the non-existent cannot be existent but is non-existent. Thus this priority, antecedence, and posteriority are matters that have no existence in external entities at all. Pure non-existence does not demand an existent locus. So none of this priority, antecedence, and posteriority require an existent subject. And time has no reality except the matter to which this priority, this antecedence, and this posteriority occur. That yields: that time and duration are not necessarily existent matters.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth proof, which is based on an axiomatic principle: it is self-evident that the non-existence of every originated thing precedes its existence. The author states that anyone who denies this is disputing the most evident of intuitive truths. From this principle, the argument proceeds: priority (al-sabq) is an attribute of non-existence (al-'adam). Since non-existence itself is non-existent, its attribute is also non-existent. Therefore, priority, antecedence, and posteriority have no external existence. Pure non-existence does not require an existent locus. Consequently, these temporal relations do not need an existent subject. Time is nothing but that which is characterized by these relations. Hence, time and duration need not be existent entities. The proof shows that the reality of time is merely a mental construct based on the priority and posteriority of non-existence.

عدم
سبق
محدث
صفة المعدوم
الزمان
المدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority requires time to exist as a real entity.
Correction: The proof shows priority is an attribute of non-existence itself, so it does not require time to be an existent entity.

Checkpoint

A) Time is a measure of motion
B) Non-existence of every originated thing precedes its existence
C) Existence is prior to non-existence
D) Time is an attribute of substance

Key Takeaway
The twelfth proof demonstrates that time and duration are not necessarily existent; they are mental constructs based on the priority of non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your sense of time—urgency, waiting, aging—feels so real. Yet this proof says time is not an existent entity. If time is just a mental construct based on the priority of non-existence, how much of its dominion over your emotions is actually an illusion? Will you let this truth loosen time's grip on your anxiety today?
?
The proof begins with a self-evident truth: you did not exist before you were born. Do you live as though that non-existence is real and that your current existence is a gift entirely from God, or do you act as if your existence is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly felt the weight of that prior non-existence every morning?
?
Priority is an attribute of non-existence itself. Nothing temporal preceded your acts; all priority is purely relational. So when you regret the past or fear the future, you are giving reality to something that is not real. Are you willing to release your attachments to a past that has no existence and a future that is not yet? What step will you take tonight to live fully in the present moment as a sign of God's creative act?

The Self-Evident Knowledge of Time: The First Proof
Classifies proponents of time and presents the first intuitive proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a thought experiment to argue that knowledge of time is self-evident and independent of motion.

Core Idea
Even in total stillness and darkness, we directly experience time flowing.

This passage classifies those who affirm the reality of time into two camps: one claiming intuitive knowledge, the other seeking demonstrative proof. The author then refines the first group's argument with a vivid thought experiment. You will see how a blind person in a dark room, stilling all motion, still perceives the passage of time—proving that our awareness of time is primary and not derived from external movements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow. Notice how the author sets up the two groups and then focuses on the first group's argument.
The first group claims knowledge of time is self-evident (badihi/daruri).
2
Identify the thought experiment
Find the description of the blind person in a dark room who stills all motions. Understand what the author means by 'still all motions'—even blinking and breathing.
The purpose is to isolate pure duration from any physical motion.
3
Analyze the intuitive comparison
The person can compare intervals (dawn to forenoon vs. dawn to noon) without any reference to celestial motions. This shows that knowledge of time does not depend on external motion.
The ability to compare half and whole intervals is an intuitive operation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that such considerations prove that knowledge of duration/time is primary self-evident knowledge, needing no explanation or proof.
This is the first proof for the self-evident existence of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المثبتين للمدة فريقان : منهم من يدعى أن العلم بوجوده علم بديهي ضروري، غني عن البيان والبرهان. ومنهم من حاول إثباته بالبينة والبرهان . أما الفريق الأول فمنهم : محمد بن زكريا الرازي ، وقوم آخرون أنا وإن كنت ما وجدت لهم إلا ادعاء البديهة والضرورة ، إلا أني أقرر تقريراً أحسن وأكمل مما ذكروه . وأقول : لهم أن يحتجوا على صحة قولهم بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : إنا لو فرضنا شخصاً غافلاً عن وجود الأفلاك والكواكب وعن طلوعها وغروبها ، بأن كان أعمى، كان جالساً في بيت مظلم ، وقدرنا أنه قصد تسكين الحركات بأسرها حتى الطرف والنفس ، فإن هذا الإنسان يجد المدة أمراً سيالا يحدث ، ويمر دائماً بلا وقوف ولا انقضاء . والعلم بذلك ضروري حتى أنه إذا اعتبر هذه الحالة من البكرة إلى الضحوة، ثم اعتبرها من الضحوة إلى وقت الظهر، فإنه مع غفلته عن حركة الشمس والقمر وسائر الكواكب والافلاك ، يعلم بالبديهة : أن الذي انقضى من البكرة إلى الضحوة ، نصف ما انقضى من البكرة إلى الظهر. ويعلم بالبديهة : أن علمه بهذا الاعتبار لا يتوقف على علمه بأن فلكاً تحرك ، أو كوكباً تحرك . وهذه الاعتبارات تدل على العلم بوجود المدة والزمان : علم بديهي أولى ، غني عن البيان والبرهان .
Know that those who affirm duration are two groups: some claim that knowledge of its existence is self-evident (badihi daruri) knowledge, independent of explanation and proof. And some attempt to prove it with evidence and demonstration. As for the first group, among them are Muhammad ibn Zakariyya al-Razi and others. I, though I have not found from them except a claim of self-evidence and necessity, yet I will present a better and more complete formulation than what they mentioned. And I say: They can argue for the soundness of their statement through proofs: The first proof: If we suppose a person oblivious to the existence of the celestial spheres and stars and their rising and setting, such as a blind person sitting in a dark room, and we assume that he intends to still all motions entirely, even blinking and breathing, then this person finds duration (muddah) as something flowing, occurring, and passing continuously without stopping or ending. And knowledge of that is necessary, such that if he considers this state from dawn to forenoon, then considers it from forenoon to noon, then despite his obliviousness to the motion of the sun, moon, and all stars and spheres, he knows intuitively that what passed from dawn to forenoon is half of what passed from dawn to noon. And he knows intuitively that his knowledge of this estimation does not depend on his knowledge that a sphere moved or a star moved. And these considerations indicate the knowledge of the existence of duration and time: a primary self-evident knowledge, independent of explanation and proof.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage classifies those who affirm the existence of time (muddah/zaman) into two groups: one claiming intuitive knowledge, the other seeking demonstration. The author then focuses on the first group, including al-Razi, and presents a more refined version of their argument. The first proof uses a thought experiment: a person blind in a dark room, stilling all motions, still experiences duration as flowing. They can compare intervals (e.g., dawn to forenoon vs. dawn to noon) without any reference to celestial movements. This demonstrates that knowledge of time is self-evident and prior to any external motion. The author emphasizes that this knowledge is primary and needs no proof.

Correction: The argument does not deny that time is perceived through motion; it shows that even without motion, time is intuitively known.

Second Argument: Time as Necessary for Conception
Proves time's intuitive necessity through its role in conceiving occurrence and permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mind's need for time to conceive occurrence and permanence proves that time is an intuitive first principle.

Core Idea
Time is the mental container for all change and endurance.

This second argument demonstrates that time is not a mere physical measure but a necessary condition for rational thought itself. Before we can think of anything happening or continuing, we must already have the concept of time. By examining how the mind inevitably uses time when conceiving occurrence and permanence, the author shows that time is an intuitive first principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow before you interpret.
Notice the argument's structure: two cases (occurrence and permanence) and a conditional.
2
Identify the two mental states
The passage considers every object of thought in two states: occurrence (change) and permanence (continuity). Find how time is involved in each.
For occurrence, time is a container; for permanence, time extends through past and present.
3
Examine the conditional
The author says: if we remove time from mental consideration, the mind becomes incapable of conceiving both occurrence and permanence. Ask yourself why this is significant.
This shows that time is not optional but necessary for basic concepts.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since occurrence and permanence are intuitively known, and their conception depends on time, time itself must be an intuitive first principle. Reflect on how this argument proves time is a priori.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن كل أمر يشير العقل إليه ، سواء كان موجوداً أو [معدوماً] ، فإن ذلك الأمر إما أن يعتبر حال حدوثه [وتبدله] أو حال دوامه واستمراره . فإن اعتبرناه حال حدوثه فههنا العقل حكم بإثبات حيز وزمان ، جعله ظرفاً لحدوثه ، [فإن العقل لا بد وأن يقول : إنه إنما حدث في ذلك الزمان الفلاني] وإن اعتبرناه حال دوامه ، فهذا الدوام لا يعقل منه إلا أنه كان موجوداً في الأزمنة المتقدمة مع أنه موجود في الزمان الحاضر . [فأما إذا] رفعنا اعتبار الزمان والمدة من العقل فههنا يعجز العقل عن تصور معنى الحدوث ، [وتصور معنى] الدوام ولما كان هذان المعنيان متصوران تصوراً بديهياً وثبت أن تصورهما لا يتقرر إلا عند الاعتراف بوجود الزمان ثبت أن الاعتراف بوجود الزمان من العلوم البديهية الأولية .
The second proof: Every matter that the mind considers, whether it is existent or [non-existent], that matter is either considered in its state of occurrence [and change] or its state of permanence and continuity. If we consider it in its state of occurrence, then here the mind judges the affirmation of a place and a time, making it a container for its occurrence, [for the mind must say: it only occurred in that particular time]. And if we consider it in its state of permanence, then this permanence is not understood except that it was existent in past times while it is existent in the present time. [But if] we remove the consideration of time and duration from the mind, then here the mind is incapable of conceiving the meaning of occurrence, [and the conception of the meaning] of permanence. And since these two meanings are conceived with an intuitive conception, and it is established that their conception is not determined except upon the acknowledgment of the existence of time, it is established that the acknowledgment of the existence of time is among the primary intuitive sciences.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the intuitive knowledge of time. The author argues that the mind always considers any object either as occurring (hadith) or as enduring (da'im). For occurrence, the mind necessarily posits a time (and place) as its container. For endurance, the mind understands it as existing in past and present times. If we try to remove time from mental consideration, we cannot conceive occurrence or endurance at all. Since these two concepts are intuitively grasped, and they depend on time, time itself must be an intuitive first principle. The proof shows that time is a necessary condition for fundamental mental categories.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is only a physical measure of motion.
Correction: The argument shows time is a necessary mental precondition for conceiving change, regardless of motion.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what happens to the mind's ability to conceive occurrence and permanence if time is removed?
A) It still conceives them clearly
B) It becomes incapable of conceiving them
C) It conceives occurrence but not permanence
D) It conceives permanence but not occurrence

Key Takeaway
The mind's inability to conceive occurrence and permanence without time proves that time is an intuitive first principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that your very ability to think about change—like your daily habits, your age, your mistakes—requires time as a mental container. But do you live as if time is a resource you own, or as a sign of Allah's constant creation? What would it mean for you to treat each moment as a divine gift that could be taken away at any instant? Will you commit to asking yourself, before every action, 'Is this how I want to spend the time Allah has given me?'
?
You conceive of your own permanence—your existence continuing from past to present—only through time. If time were removed from your mind, you could not even grasp that you are the same person who woke up yesterday. Does this realization humble you? Do you take for granted that you will exist tomorrow, forgetting that your continuing existence is entirely dependent on Allah's will? What one change can you make today to live in light of this dependence?
?
The argument proves that time is not an illusion but a necessary framework for rational thought. Yet how often do you waste time as if it has no value, or despair over lost time as if it can be reclaimed? Since time is a primary intuitive reality, your accountability for its use is inescapable. If you were to stand before Allah tonight and account for your last 24 hours, what would you say? Will you resolve to use the next hour consciously for something that pleases Him?

How the Mutakallimun's Definitions Confirm Intuitive Time
Cites the mutakallimun's definitions to confirm intuitive time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun's definitions of accident and persistence presuppose time, confirming its intuitive nature.

Core Idea
Even technical kalam definitions depend on time, showing time is self-evident.

The author strengthens his earlier argument for the intuitive nature of time by appealing to the definitions used by the mutakallimun themselves. These definitions, which describe accidents as not persisting for two moments, rely on the concept of time. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.

2

These definitions are quoted from the mutakallimun.
3
Trace the logical implication
The author argues that the mutakallimun only conceive occurrence and persistence by means of time. This shows time is presupposed.
The conclusion is that knowledge of time is self-evident.
4
Avoid a common misconception
The author does not endorse the mutakallimun's theory of accidents. He uses their terminology as evidence.
This is a key nuance to avoid overinterpreting.

والذي يزيد هذا الكلام تقريراً أن المتكلمين قالوا : العرض لا يبقى زمانين ، والباقي هو الذي استمر وجوده زمانين وأكثر ، فهم لم يعقلوا البتة معنى الحدوث، ومعنى البقاء إلا بسبب المدة والزمان. وهذا يدل على أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي غني عن الحجة والبرهان

Commentary
This passage provides additional support for the intuitive nature of time. The author references the mutakallimun's (Islamic theologians) technical definitions: an accident ('arad) does not last for two moments (zamanayn), while a persistent entity (baqi) is defined as continuing for two or more moments. These definitions presuppose time. Therefore, even the theologians rely on the concept of time to define basic ontological categories. This confirms that knowledge of time is self-evident and requires no proof.

Time is a Self-Evident Concept
Razi argues that time is presupposed by the intellect for conceiving change and persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Fakhr al-Din al-Razi argues that the concept of time is self-evident and necessary for understanding occurrence and permanence.

Core Idea
Time is not inferred from change; it is already presupposed by the intellect.

Razi presents his second argument for the self-evidence of time. He shows that the intellect naturally conceives of time when thinking about occurrence or permanence. Without time, these concepts become impossible. This lesson unpacks Razi's reasoning and its significance in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two states of consideration
The intellect considers any matter either in its state of occurrence and change, or in its state of permanence and continuity. This sets up the argument.
Notice that these are the only two ways the intellect can consider a thing.
2
See how the intellect implicitly uses time
When considering occurrence, the intellect must affirm a place and time as a container. When considering permanence, the intellect understands that the thing existed in past times and continues in the present.
In both cases, time is already involved.
3
Consider the removal of time
Razi asks us to imagine removing the notion of time and duration. Without it, the intellect becomes incapable of conceiving either occurrence or permanence.
This shows that time is not an add-on but essential.
4
Conclude the self-evidence of time
Since occurrence and permanence are self-evident and their conception depends on time, the intellect must already know time self-evidently. The theologians' definitions of accident and enduring being also rely on time.
This confirms that knowledge of time is primary and needs no proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن كل أمر يشير العقل إليه ، سواء كان موجوداً أو معدومًا ، فإن ذلك الأمر إما أن يعتبر حال حدوثه وتبدله أو حال دوامه واستمراره . فإن اعتبرناه حال حدوثه فههنا العقل حكم بإثبات حيز وزمان ، جعله ظرفاً لحدوثه ، فإن العقل لا بد وأن يقول : إنه إنما حدث في ذلك الزمان الفلاني وإن اعتبرناه حال دوامه ، فهذا الدوام لا يعقل منه إلا أنه كان موجوداً في الأزمنة المتقدمة مع أنه موجود في الزمان الحاضر . فأما إذا رفعنا اعتبار الزمان والمدة من العقل فههنا يعجز العقل عن تصور معنى الحدوث ، وتصور معنى الدوام ولما كان هذان المعنيان متصوران تصوراً بديهياً وثبت أن تصورهما لا يتقرر إلا عند الاعتراف بوجود الزمان ثبت أن الاعتراف بوجود الزمان من العلوم البديهية الأولية . والذي يزيد هذا الكلام تقريراً أن المتكلمين قالوا : العرض لا يبقى زمانين ، والباقي هو الذي استمر وجوده زمانين وأكثر ، فهم لم يعقلوا البتة معنى الحدوث، ومعنى البقاء إلا بسبب المدة والزمان. وهذا يسدل على أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي غني عن الحجة والبرهان
The second proof: Every matter that the intellect points to, whether existent or non-existent, that matter is either considered in the state of its occurrence and change, or in the state of its permanence and continuity. If we consider it in the state of its occurrence, then here the intellect judges by affirming a place and a time, making it a container for its occurrence – for the intellect must say: it only occurred in that particular time. And if we consider it in the state of its permanence, then this permanence is not understood except as it having existed in past times while being existent in the present time. But if we remove the consideration of time and duration from the intellect, then here the intellect is incapable of conceiving the meaning of occurrence and conceiving the meaning of permanence. And since these two meanings are conceived as self-evident conception, and it is established that their conception is not settled except upon acknowledging the existence of time, it is established that acknowledging the existence of time is among the primary self-evident sciences. And what strengthens this statement is that the theologians said: the accident does not persist for two moments, and the enduring is that whose existence continued for two moments or more – they did not understand at all the meaning of occurrence and the meaning of persistence except by means of duration and time. This indicates that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident, needless of proof and demonstration.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the self-evident nature of time and duration. The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, frames it as follows: The intellect considers any matter – whether existent or non-existent – in one of two states: either in its state of occurrence and change, or in its state of permanence and continuity. If we consider it in its state of occurrence, the intellect necessarily affirms a place and time as a container for that occurrence; it must say that the event happened in that particular time. If we consider it in the state of permanence, the intellect understands that the thing existed in past times and continues to exist in the present. If we remove the notion of time and duration from the intellect, then the intellect becomes incapable of conceiving either occurrence or permanence at all. Since these two concepts are self-evidently conceived, and their conception depends on acknowledging time, it follows that acknowledging time is itself a self-evident primary science. Strengthening this, al-Razi notes that theologians (mutakallimun) define an accident as not remaining for two moments, and an enduring entity as that whose existence continues for two moments or more. They could not conceive of occurrence or persistence except by reference to duration and time. This confirms that knowledge of duration and time is self-evident, requiring no proof.

Misconception: Time is inferred from observing change in the world.
Correction: The argument shows that time is already presupposed in the very conception of change; without it, we cannot even conceive of change.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of time is self-evident because the intellect cannot conceive occurrence or permanence without it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is self-evident to your intellect, then every moment you experience is not an illusion but a reality directly perceived. When you waste time, are you wasting something Allah has made foundational to your very ability to think? How will you change your view of time from today?
?
You cannot conceive of anything happening without time. This means your entire life story is woven into time. Are you living as if time is just a line on a clock, or as a gift from Allah that allows you to exist and act? What would it look like to honor time as a self-evident reality given by Him?
?
The intellect must posit time to understand change and persistence. Your own mind testifies to time. If you ignore this testimony and act as if time is meaningless, are you contradicting the very nature Allah gave you? What step will you take today to align your actions with the truth that time is real and precious?

The Third Argument: Precedence and Simultaneity
Razi uses precedence and simultaneity to prove time is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the concepts of precedence and simultaneity to prove that the existence of time is self-evident.

Core Idea
Precedence and simultaneity necessarily imply time, known instinctively.

Razi provides a third argument for the self-evidence of time. He appeals to common understanding: when we say one person was before another, or that two twins existed together, everyone understands that time is involved. Even if someone objects that these terms might refer to the essences themselves, Razi shows that the relation of precedence or simultaneity is necessarily distinct from the subjects. This lesson unpacks the argument and its response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the two examples used.
2
Identify the main claim and examples
Razi claims that precedence (before) and simultaneity (together) are understood only through time. He gives the examples of Adam and Muhammad, and twin brothers.
3
Examine the objection and response
The objector suggests that precedence and simultaneity might refer to essences themselves, not time. Razi responds by distinguishing the subject of division from the relation itself.
This is a key subtle point.
4
Reflect on self-evidence
Consider why Razi considers this knowledge to be instinctive and why the argument is strong.
Think about whether you find it convincing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : على أن العلم بوجود المدة علم بديهي هي : أن نقول : إنا إذا قلنا : إن آدم عليه السلام كان قبل محمد عليه السلام . فإنا لا نعقل من هذه القبلية إلا أن بينهما مدة مخصوصة وزماناً مخصوصاً . وإذا قلنا : الأخوان التوءمان وجدا معاً ، لم نعقل من هذه المعية إلا أنها حصلا في زمان واحد ولو رجعت إلى جميع العقلاء الذين بقوا على فطرتهم الأصلية وسلامة عقولهم الغريزية لم يفهموا من هذه القبلية ولا من هذه المعية إلا ما ذكرناه . فعلمنا : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان : علم مقرر في بدائه العقول وغرائز الأذهان . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من هذه المعية والقبلية نفس ذاتيهما ؟ قلنا : لانا جعلنا ذاتيهما ووجوديها مورداً للتقسيم لهذه المعية ولهذه القبلية. ومورد التقسيم لما به حصل ذلك التقسيم ، وذلك معلوم بالضرورة .
The third proof that knowledge of the existence of duration is self-evident is that we say: When we say that Adam, peace be upon him, was before Muhammad, peace be upon him, we do not understand from this precedence except that there is between them a specific duration and a specific time. And when we say that the twin brothers existed together, we do not understand from this simultaneity except that they occurred in a single time. If you were to return to all rational people who remain on their original disposition and the soundness of their instinctive intellects, they would not understand from this precedence nor from this simultaneity except what we have mentioned. Thus we know that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is a knowledge established in the primaries of intellects and the instincts of minds. If it is said: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning of this simultaneity and precedence is their very essences? We answer: Because we have made their essences and existences the subject of division for this simultaneity and this precedence. The subject of division is distinct from that by which the division occurs, and that is known necessarily.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the self-evidence of time. Al-Razi states: When we say 'Adam was before Muhammad', we understand from this precedence a specific duration and time between them. Similarly, when we say 'the twin brothers existed together', we understand from this simultaneity that they occurred in a single time. If one consulted all rational people who remain on their original sound disposition and instinctive intellects, they would interpret these expressions no differently. Therefore, knowledge of duration and time is firmly established in the primaries of intellects and instincts. An objection is then raised: Why can 'simultaneity' and 'precedence' not refer to the essences themselves, without time? Al-Razi answers: We have made the essences and existences of the individuals the subject of division for this simultaneity and precedence. That is, 'Adam' and 'Muhammad' are the subjects being related, and the relation itself (precedence) is distinct from the subjects. The subject of division is one thing, and the relation by which division occurs (precedence/simultaneity) is another; one cannot collapse them. This distinction is known necessarily.

معية
مدة
زمان
بديهي
فطرة
غرائز الأذهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think precedence and simultaneity are purely relational without involving time.
Correction: The argument shows that these relations necessarily imply time, as understood by all rational people.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised in the third argument?
A) Precedence and simultaneity are not self-evident.
B) The expressions 'before' and 'together' might refer to the essences themselves, not time.
C) Common people do not understand these terms.
D) Time is not needed for simultaneity.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of time is rooted in the innate human understanding of precedence and simultaneity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'before' or 'after' in your daily conversations, do you ever realize that you are implicitly affirming the existence of time? How would your awareness of time as a created dimension change the way you speak about events?
?
Razi says all rational people with sound fitra understand precedence and simultaneity as implying time. Has your fitra been clouded by modern philosophies or habits that treat time as merely a human concept? If so, how can you realign your thinking to see time as a real, self-evident sign of the Creator?
?
The objection Razi refutes tries to reduce relationships to essences—denying the reality of time. Is there any area of your life where you reduce a real, God-given reality (like time, causality, or morality) to mere conceptual labels? What would it take for you to treat them as the self-evident truths they are?

The Fourth Argument: Motion and Rest
Razi argues that motion and rest require time, proving it self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses the self-evident concepts of motion and rest to prove that time (zaman) is self-evident.

Core Idea
Motion and rest logically presuppose time.

Al-Razi presents a concise proof that time is not merely a derived concept but is known self-evidently. He begins by analyzing our basic experience of motion and rest, showing that they cannot be understood without assuming duration. This argument aims to establish time as a primary, undeniable reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that al-Razi starts with a self-evident observation about bodies.
2
Identify the two examples: motion and rest
The passage uses motion and rest as two cases to show that each implies time. Underline the definitions of motion (حركة) and rest (سكون) in the text.
Motion implies 'afterness' (بعدية); rest implies continuation (استمرار).
3
Follow the logical chain to the conclusion
Observe how al-Razi argues: since knowledge of motion/rest is self-evident and depends on time, time must be even more self-evident. Trace this reasoning step by step.
The final sentence states the conclusion: knowledge of time is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة عقله أن الجسم إما أن يكون متحركاً، وإما أن يكون ساكناً، والمعقول من كونه متحركاً إنما يكون متحركاً إذا حصل في مكان ، بعد أن كان حاصلا في غيره ، وهذه البعدية إشارة إلى أن هذا الجسم كان حاصلا في غيره، ثم حصل في زمان آخر في حيز آخر ، وهذا يدل على أنه لا يمكن تعقل معنى الحركة والتغير إلا بعد الاعتراف بوجود المدة والزمان . وأما السكون فالمعقول منه هو استمرار الجسم في الحيز الواحد زماناً طويلاً . وهذا أيضاً إشارة إلى وجود المدة والزمان ، ولما كان العلم بحقيقة الحركة وبحقيقة السكون علماً بديهياً أولياً وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يتقرر إلا عند التسليم بوجود المدة والزمان ، وثبت أن الذي يتوقف عليه التصديق أولى أن يكون أولياً ، ثبت : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي
The fourth proof: Every rational person knows by the self-evidence of his intellect that the body is either moving or at rest. What is understood from its being moving is that it is moving only when it occurs in a place after having occurred in another, and this 'afterness' indicates that the body was in another place and then occurred in a different time and a different location. This shows that the meaning of motion and change cannot be conceived except after acknowledging the existence of duration and time. As for rest, what is understood from it is the continuation of the body in a single location for a long time. This too is an indication of the existence of duration and time. Since knowledge of the reality of motion and the reality of rest is a self-evident primary knowledge, and it is established that knowledge of them is not settled except upon acknowledging the existence of duration and time, and it is established that that upon which assent depends is more worthy of being primary, it is established that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument for the self-evidence of time. Al-Razi argues: Every rational person knows self-evidently that a body is either moving or at rest. The concept of motion requires that a body be in one place after having been in another; this 'afterness' implies a previous location and a subsequent time and location. Thus, motion and change cannot be conceived without acknowledging duration and time. Similarly, rest is understood as the body's continuation in a single place for a long time, which also implies time. Now, since our knowledge of the reality of motion and rest is self-evident and primary, and since that knowledge depends on acknowledging time, the thing upon which it depends (time) must be even more primary and self-evident. Hence, knowledge of duration and time is self-evident.

Key Terms
متحرك
ساكن
حركة
سكون
بعدية
زمان
مدة
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion is directly perceived without time; time is an abstraction derived from motion.
Correction: The argument shows that the very concept of motion includes temporal succession (afterness), so motion cannot even be conceived without time. Thus time is more fundamental, not derived.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does the concept of 'rest' (sukūn) imply?
A) Absence of motion
B) A body continuing in one place for a long time
C) A body being in the same place at different times
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Time is not a derived concept; it is self-evidently known through our experience of motion and rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that time is self-evident through motion and rest. Think about a moment today when you moved from one place to another. Did you acknowledge the time that passed? How does your awareness of time affect your awareness of your own existence? This lesson claims that time is as evident as your own movement. If time is so obvious, why do you often live as if you have infinite time? Today, commit to truly feeling the weight of each moment.
?
Razi says that rest implies continuation over time. When you are still, do you realize that you are still 'in time'? Your stillness is not timeless. Consider your periods of inactivity—do they feel like wasted time? This lesson shows that rest itself requires time. How can you use your rest time to reflect on the Creator of time itself? Resolve to use even your moments of stillness to remember God.
?
The argument builds from something you know innately (motion/rest) to something you might not have considered (time as self-evident). What other things do you take for granted that actually point to deeper truths about reality? This lesson challenges you to think from the obvious to the necessary. What in your daily life, if you ponder it, reveals a necessary truth about Allah? Commit to one such reflection today.
