The Intuitive Knowledge of Time from Priority and Simultaneity
Third proof that knowledge of time is intuitive from priority and simultaneity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concepts of priority and simultaneity necessarily imply the existence of time, and how this is known intuitively.

Core Idea
Priority and simultaneity only make sense if time exists.

This lesson presents the third proof for the intuitive knowledge of time. The proof draws on our everyday understanding of 'before' and 'together' to show that time is a fundamental concept embedded in our rational nature. It then addresses a subtle objection and refutes it, solidifying the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and identify key terms
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: 'priority' (qabliyya), 'simultaneity' (ma'iyya), and 'time' (mudda/zaman). Understand the claim that knowledge of time is intuitive.
2
Understand the universal appeal
Notice how the proof appeals to all rational people on their natural disposition. What does this say about the universality of time as a concept?
This strengthens the argument that time is not a mere convention.
3
Analyze the objection and its refutation
Read the objection: perhaps priority and simultaneity refer directly to essences. Then study the refutation: the essences are made the subject of division, and the division itself requires time. Ask yourself why this refutation is effective.
The refutation uses the principle that the locus of division is distinct from the basis of division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : على أن العلم بوجود المدة علم بديهي هي : أن نقول :
إنا إذا قلنا : إن آدم عليه السلام كان قبل محمد عليه السلام . فإنا لا نعقل من هذه القبلية إلا أن بينهما مدة مخصوصة وزماناً مخصوصاً . وإذا قلنا : الأخوان التوء مان وجدا معاً ، لم نعقل من هذه المعية إلا أنها حصلا في زمان واحد ولو رجعت إلى جميع العقلاء الذين بقوا على فطرتهم الأصلية وسلامة عقولهم الغريزية لم يفهموا من هذه القبلية ولا من هذه المعية إلا ما ذكرناه . فعلمنا : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان : علم مقرر في بدائه العقول وغرائز الأذهان فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد من هذه المعية والقبلية نفس ذاتيهما ؟ قلنا : لانا جعلنا ذاتيهما ووجوديها مورداً للتقسيم لهذه المعية ولهذه القبلية. ومورد التقسيم لما به حصل ذلك التقسيم ، وذلك معلوم بالضرورة .
The third proof: That knowledge of the existence of time (al-mudda) is intuitive (badīhī) is that we say: When we say: 'Ādam, peace be upon him, was before Muḥammad, peace be upon him,' we do not understand from this priority (al-qabliyya) except that there is between them a specific time-period (mudda makhṣūṣa) and a specific time (zamān makhṣūṣ). And when we say: 'The twin brothers were together (maʿan),' we do not understand from this simultaneity (al-maʿiyya) except that they occurred at one time. And if you were to return to all rational people who remain on their original disposition (fiṭra) and soundness of their innate intellects (ʿuqūl ghārizīyya), they would not understand from this priority nor from this simultaneity except what we have mentioned. So we know: that knowledge of the existence of time and duration is a knowledge firmly established in the innate dispositions of intellects (badāʾiʿ al-ʿuqūl) and the natural instincts of minds (gharāʾiz al-adhhān). Then if it is said: Why may it not be that what is meant by this simultaneity and priority is their very essences (dhātayhimā)? We say: Because we have made their essences and existences the locus of division (mawrid al-taqsīm) for this simultaneity and this priority. And the locus of division is through that which gave rise to that division, and that is known necessarily (ḍarūra).
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) that knowledge of the existence of time is intuitive (badīhī). The author begins by stating the conclusion. He then presents an argument based on the concepts of priority (qabliyya) and simultaneity (maʿiyya). When we say one person is before another, we inevitably understand a specific temporal interval between them. Similarly, when we say two things occur together, we understand they happen at one time. This understanding is universal among all sound-minded people, regardless of culture. The author then addresses an objection: perhaps 'priority' and 'simultaneity' refer directly to the essences themselves without implying time. His refutation is that the essences are made the subject of division—they are being categorized as prior or simultaneous—and such categorization requires a framework of time. Therefore, the division itself confirms time's existence. The proof relies on the necessary (ḍarūra) nature of this recognition. The Arabic terms are carefully chosen to show that time (al-mudda wa al-zamān) is a primitive concept, not derivable from anything more basic.

Key Terms

الزمان
القبلية
المعية
بديهي
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and simultaneity are purely logical relations without temporal implication.
Correction: The proof shows that these terms necessarily imply a temporal interval or shared time; otherwise, they are unintelligible.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is the only understanding people have of priority (qabliyya)?
A) The essences are prior
B) There is a specific time interval between them
C) They are in the same location
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Priority and simultaneity are inherently temporal concepts, and their universal understanding proves that time is an intuitive notion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your day, do you think of 'later' as a real period of time that may never come? How does this awareness of time as a limited, real dimension change the urgency you give to obeying Allah today? Specifically, what is one task you will do today that you have been putting off?
?
The proof shows that even the most basic human concepts of 'before' and 'together' force us to acknowledge time. If time is so fundamental to your thinking, how often do you use time without thanking its Creator? Starting now, will you commit to pausing every time you think of 'before' or 'after' to reflect on Allah's power?
?
You have learned that priority and simultaneity only make sense with time. In your spiritual life, you often prioritize some actions over others. Does the order of your priorities reflect that you truly believe each moment is a gift from Allah? What would it look like to treat your daily schedule as an act of worship by deliberately placing the most important deeds first?

The Fourth Proof: Motion and Rest Necessitate Time
The fourth proof argues that the concepts of motion and rest necessarily imply time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intuitive concepts of motion and rest logically require the existence of time, establishing time as intuitive knowledge.

Core Idea
Motion and rest cannot be conceived without time.

This proof begins with something every rational person knows: a body is either moving or at rest. By analyzing these everyday experiences, the author shows that they already contain the idea of time. Let us follow the argument to see why time must be known intuitively.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the author's own reasoning unfold before diving into analysis.
Notice how the author uses a clear structure: statement, definitions, logical dependence, conclusion.
2
Understand the definition of motion
Motion is defined as being in one place after being in another. The 'afterness' (baʿdiyya) directly implies temporal succession.
This is not just spatial change; it is temporal change.
3
Understand the definition of rest
Rest is defined as persistence in one location for a long time. This also implies duration, hence time.
Both motion and rest involve an element of time.
4
Follow the logical dependence argument
Since knowledge of motion and rest is intuitive, and that knowledge depends on time, time must also be intuitive. The author uses a principle: if A depends on B and A is intuitive, then B is intuitive.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that time is known intuitively. Think about why this matters for establishing time as a reality.
This is a foundational argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة عقله أن الجسم إما أن يكون متحركاً، وإما أن يكون ساكناً، والمعقول من كونه متحركاً إنما يكون متحركاً إذا حصل في مكان، بعد أن كان حاصلاً في غيره، وهذه البعدية إشارة إلى أن هذا الجسم كان حاصلاً في غيره، ثم حصل في زمان آخر في حيز آخر، وهذا يدل على أنه لا يمكن تعقل معنى الحركة والتغير إلا بعد الاعتراف بوجود المدة والزمان . وأما السكون فالمعقول منه هو استمرار الجسم في الحيز الواحد زماناً طويلاً . وهذا أيضاً إشارة إلى وجود المدة والزمان، ولما كان العلم بحقيقة الحركة وبحقيقة السكون علماً بديهياً أولياً وثبت أن العلم بهما لا يتقرر إلا عند التسليم بوجود المدة والزمان، وثبت أن الذي يتوقف عليه التصديق أولى أن يكون أولياً، ثبت : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي.
The fourth proof: Every rational person knows by the intuition (badīha) of his intellect that a body is either moving or at rest. And what is understood of it being moving is that it is moving only when it is in one place after having been in another. This afterness indicates that this body was in another place, then it occurred in another time in another location. This demonstrates that the meaning of motion and change cannot be conceived except after acknowledging the existence of time and duration. As for rest, what is understood from it is the persistence of the body in one location for a long time. This also indicates the existence of time and duration. And since knowledge of the reality of motion and the reality of rest is primary intuitive knowledge, and it is established that knowledge of them is not established except upon the admission of the existence of time and duration, and it is established that that upon which affirmation (al-taṣdīq) depends is more worthy of being primary, it is established that knowledge of the existence of time and duration is intuitive knowledge.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) based on the universally known concepts of motion and rest. The author argues that every rational person intuitively knows what motion and rest are. Motion is defined as being in one place after being in another—this 'afterness' (baʿdiyya) directly implies temporal succession. Rest is defined as persistence in one place for a long time, which also implies duration. Since the knowledge of motion and rest is intuitive (badīhī awwalī), and since this knowledge depends on the existence of time, it follows that time itself must be known intuitively. The logic is: if A (motion/rest) is intuitive and A cannot be conceived without B (time), then B is also intuitive. This is a classic kalam argument from dependence.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
المدة
الزمان
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion is purely spatial displacement without temporal dimension.
Correction: The proof shows that motion necessarily involves temporal succession (the 'afterness' of positions), so it cannot be understood without time.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what does the concept of motion necessarily involve?
A) Change in space
B) Being after another position in time
C) Persistence in one place
D) Simultaneity of events

Key Takeaway
The intuitive concepts of motion and rest cannot be understood without presupposing the existence of time, proving that time itself is intuitive knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is an intuitive reality that you cannot deny, how do you justify wasting it? What would it mean to truly honor the time that you know exists and is passing right now?
?
The proof shows that your own understanding of motion and rest depends on time. Where in your life are you 'resting' (stagnating) while time is passing? What change are you avoiding?
?
Since time is self-evident, your consciousness of it is built into your nature. Why then do you often live as if time is irrelevant? What would it take to live each moment with the awareness that it is the only moment you have?

The Fifth Proof: Time as Intuitive Knowledge from Eternity and Origination
The author argues that the meanings of eternal and originated require time, making time intuitively known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary division of existents into eternal and originated demonstrates that time is known intuitively.

Core Idea
The very concepts of 'eternal' and 'originated' require time.

The fifth proof in al-Matalib al-'Aliyah takes an epistemological approach: it begins with what every rational person knows intuitively—that everything is either eternal or originated. Then it shows that to even understand these categories, we must have the concept of time. Therefore, time is not a mere convention but an intuitive certainty.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the intuitive division
Read the opening sentence: 'Every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect that the existent is either eternal or originated.' This is presented as self-evident.
Notice the claim is about intuitive, not inferential, knowledge.
2
Define the terms
Examine how the author defines 'eternal' (no beginning) and 'originated' (has a beginning). These definitions are unpacked with temporal language.
Pay attention to the phrases 'no first' and 'has a first'.
3
Analyze the temporal implications
See that the definitions inherently refer to time: for 'eternal', we consider past times and never reach a time before it; for 'originated', we reach a time when it began.
The author uses 'time' (zamān) and 'duration' (mudda) explicitly.
4
Draw the conclusion
The argument concludes that since the division is intuitive and the concepts require time, time itself must be intuitively known.
This establishes time as a self-evident reality, not a construct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة عقله أن الموجود، إما أن يكون قديماً أو محدثاً، ثم إنه لا نعقل من القديم إلا أنه الذي لا أول لوجوده، ولا نعقل من الحادث إلا أنه الذي يحصل لوجوده أول، ثم إذا فسرنا قولنا : بأنه لا أول لوجوده : وهو أنا إذا اعتبرنا حالة في الأزمنة السالفة، فإنا لا نصل عقولنا إلى زمان إلا وقد كان موجوداً قبله. وإذا فسرنا الحادث : بأنه الذي لوجوده أول لم نفهم منه، إلا أن عقولنا تنتهي إلى وقت، يحكم عقلنا بأنه حدث فيه . فثبت : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الشيء إما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون محدثاً، وثبت أنه لا يمكن تصور معنى القديم ومعنى المحدث إلا عند الحكم بوجود المدة والزمان، وذلك يفيد أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي.
The fifth proof: Every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect that the existent is either eternal (qadīm) or originated (muḥdath). Then we do not understand from the eternal except that which has no beginning (lā awwal li-wujūdihi), and we do not understand from the originated except that which has a beginning (yaḥṣulu li-wujūdihi awwal). Then when we interpret our saying 'it has no beginning' as that if we consider a state in past times, we do not reach a time except that it existed before it. And when we interpret the originated as that which has a beginning, we do not understand from it except that our intellects reach a time in which our intellect judges that it occurred. So it is established that pure intellect (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) judges that a thing is either eternal or originated, and it is established that the meaning of the eternal and the meaning of the originated cannot be conceived except upon the judgment of the existence of time (al-mudda) and duration (al-zamān). And that yields that knowledge of the existence of time and duration is intuitive knowledge.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) that knowledge of time is intuitive, drawn from the necessary division of existents into eternal (qadīm) and originated (muḥdath). The author notes that every rational person intuitively recognizes this division. Defining the eternal as 'that which has no beginning' and the originated as 'that which has a beginning' directly involves temporal concepts. To explain 'no beginning', he says that in past times we never reach a time before which the eternal was not; to explain 'having a beginning', he says our intellect reaches a time when it came to be. Thus, the very act of conceiving these categories requires time. Since the division is intuitively known, time must also be intuitively known. This argument ties the intuitive knowledge of time to the fundamental classification of beings in Islamic theology.

Key Terms
قديم
محدث
المدة
الزمان
بديهي
صريح العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'eternal' is a purely logical or essential attribute without temporal implication.
Correction: The proof shows that 'eternal' necessarily involves the absence of temporal beginning, thus requiring time.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, what does the definition of 'originated' (ḥādith) involve?
A) Having a cause
B) Having a beginning in time
C) Being non-existent
D) Being contingent

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that time is intuitively known because the concepts of eternal and originated, which are intuitively divided, cannot be understood without time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the fact that you will eventually die, do you intuitively sense that your existence has a beginning? How does this proof make you more aware that your own existence in time is a sign of your createdness?
?
The proof argues that your intellect cannot conceive 'eternal' without time. When you try to imagine God's eternity, do you notice yourself relying on a sense of time? How does that humblen you in realising that your understanding of God is limited by your innate concepts?
?
If knowledge of time is intuitive, then you already possess a fundamental premise that points to a Creator. How can you actively use this innate knowledge to strengthen your conviction in times of doubt, especially when you feel disconnected from rational proofs?

The Sixth Proof: Intuitive Knowledge of Time from Its Divisions
The sixth proof argues that the necessary divisions of time prove its existence intuitively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the necessary divisions of time (years, months, days, hours) lead to intuitive knowledge of time's existence.

Core Idea
Time's necessary divisions prove its existence intuitively.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from the text, which argues that the very fact we can divide time into years, months, days, and hours demonstrates that time is real and known intuitively. The author appeals to pure reason and necessary knowledge to establish that time exists externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from division to existence.
The Arabic uses the term al-muddatan and al-zaman; both refer to time.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the argument: divisions are possible → these divisions are known necessarily → the subject of divisions is distinct from physical objects → for divisions to be real in external things, time must exist externally.
3
Reflect on necessary knowledge
Consider why the author calls knowledge of these divisions 'necessary'. Is it self-evident?
4
Consider the misconception
Think about the possibility that divisions are merely conceptual. How does the author refute this?
The proof concludes that time must be actual in external things for the divisions to be real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن صريح العقل حاكم بأنه يمكن قسمة الزمان إلى السنين، وقسمة السنين إلى الشهور، وقسمة الشهور إلى الأيام، وقسمة الأيام إلى الساعات، ويعلم بالضرورة أن الساعة جزء من اليوم الذي هو جزء من الشهر، الذي هو جزء من السنة، التي هي جزء من المدة . والعلم بحصول هذه التقديرات والتقسيمات، علم ضروري . والعلم يكون بعضها أقل من بعض، أو أكثر من بعض علم ضروري . ومن المعلوم بالضرورة : أن المحكوم عليه بقبول هذه التقسيمات مغاير للسواد والبياض والحجر والمثلث، وأن ذلك الأمر ما لم يكن متحققاً في الأعيان، امتنع كونه مورداً لهذه التقسيمات في الأعيان، فثبت : أن العلم بوجود الزمان والمدة علم بديهي.
The sixth proof: Pure intellect (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) judges that it is possible to divide duration (al-zamān) into years, years into months, months into days, days into hours. And it is known necessarily that an hour is a part of a day which is a part of a month which is a part of a year which is a part of time (al-mudda). And the knowledge of the occurrence of these estimations and divisions is necessary knowledge. And the knowledge that some of them are less than others or more than others is necessary knowledge. And it is known necessarily that that which is judged to admit these divisions is other than blackness, whiteness, stone, and triangle. And that this thing, if it were not actualized in external things (al-aʿyān), it would be impossible for it to be the subject of these divisions in external things. So it is established that knowledge of the existence of duration (al-zamān) and time (al-mudda) is intuitive knowledge.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a proof (ḥujja) that knowledge of time is intuitive, based on the universally recognized divisions of time. The author states that pure intellect (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) affirms that time can be divided into years, months, days, and hours. These divisions are known necessarily (ḍarūra), and it is also known necessarily that some parts are larger or smaller. That which receives these divisions—time itself—is distinct from physical objects like colors, stone, or shapes. Furthermore, for time to be the subject of such divisions in the real world, it must itself be real (mutḥaqqiq fī al-aʿyān). Since we necessarily know these divisions, and they presuppose a real subject, time must exist and its knowledge is intuitive. This proof appeals to the common human practice of measuring time.

Key Terms
المدة
الزمان
التقسيم
السنين
الشهور
الأيام
الساعات
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the divisions of time are merely conceptual or conventional without external reality.
Correction: The proof argues that if time were not actualized in external things, it could not be the subject of these divisions in the real world, so time must exist externally.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what does the fact that time can be divided into parts indicate?
A) Time is a mental construct
B) Time exists in external reality
C) Time is infinite
D) Time is relative

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that time's existence is intuitively known through its necessary divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you divide your day into hours, have you ever paused to consider that time itself is a real existence distinct from your activities? How would your daily planning change if you truly internalized that time is a created entity that will eventually end?
?
The proof shows that knowledge of time is intuitive. Yet many people live as if time is infinite. Which decisions in your life this week revealed that you actually treat time as unlimited? What one change can you make today to live with the awareness that time is a finite gift?
?
If time is a real entity that admits divisions, then every moment is a distinct part of a larger whole. How does this truth affect your understanding of the Hereafter, where time as we know it will cease? Are you preparing for that transition, or are you absorbed in the divisions of this world?

The Fifth Proof: Knowledge of Time is Self-Evident
Sanusi presents the fifth proof that knowledge of time is self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves that knowledge of time is self-evident by analyzing the necessary distinction between eternal and originated.

Core Idea
Time is known through the very concepts of 'eternal' and 'originated.'

Sanusi presents his fifth proof, which argues that knowledge of time is self-evident (badihi). He begins from a universal premise: every rational person knows that everything is either eternal (qadim) or originated (muhdath). By unpacking what these terms mean, he shows that they necessarily presuppose duration and time, making time's existence self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Sanusi builds his argument step by step.
2
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the premise, the definitions of eternal and originated, the elaboration on past times, and the final conclusion that time must be self-evident.
The key move is showing that these concepts cannot be understood without time.
3
Reflect on the self-evident nature of time
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi think that knowledge of time is self-evident? Connect it to the intuitive distinction everyone makes between eternal and originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن كل عاقل يعلم ببديهة عقله أن الموجود ، إما أن يكون قديماً أو محدثاً ، ثم إنه لا نعقل من القديم إلا أنه الذي لا أول لوجوده ، ولا نعقل من الحادث إلا أنه الذي يحصل لوجوده أول ، ثم إذا فسرنا قولنا : بأنه لا أول لوجوده : وهو أنا إذا اعتبرنا حالة في الأزمنة السالفة ، فإنا لا نصل عقولنا إلى زمان إلا وقد كان موجوداً قبله. وإذا فسرنا الحادث : بأنه الذي لوجوده أول لم نفهم منه ، إلا أن عقولنا تنتهي إلى وقت ، يحكم عقلنا بأنه حدث فيه . فثبت : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الشيء إما أن يكون قديماً وإما أن يكون محدثاً ، وثبت أنه لا يمكن تصور معنى القديم ومعنى المحدث إلا عند الحكم بوجود المدة والزمان ، وذلك يفيد أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي .
The fifth proof: Every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect that the existent is either eternal or originated. Then we do not conceive of the eternal except as that which has no beginning to its existence, and we do not conceive of the originated except as that which obtains a beginning to its existence. Then if we interpret our saying 'no beginning to its existence'—that is, if we consider a state in past times, we do not reach with our intellects a time except that it existed before it. And if we interpret the originated as that which has a beginning to its existence, we understand from it nothing except that our intellects reach a time which our intellect judges that it occurred in it. So it is established that pure intellect judges that a thing is either eternal or originated, and it is established that it is impossible to conceive the meaning of eternal and the meaning of originated except when judging the existence of duration (al-muddah) and time (al-zaman), and that yields that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident knowledge.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a proof (al-hujjah al-khamisah) that knowledge of time is self-evident (badihi). The author begins by stating a universal premise: every rational person knows by the intuition of his intellect that every existent is either eternal (qadim) or originated (muhdath). He then defines both terms: the eternal is that whose existence has no beginning, and the originated is that which has a beginning. He elaborates on these definitions: for the eternal, when we consider past times, we never come to a time without it already existing before that time; for the originated, our intellect reaches a point in time and judges that it came into existence at that point. The conclusion is that pure intellect (sarih al-aql) rules that everything is either eternal or originated. Then he argues that it is impossible to conceive the meaning of 'eternal' and 'originated' without presupposing the existence of duration (al-muddah) and time (al-zaman). Therefore, knowledge of the existence of duration and time must be self-evident, because its concomitant is self-evident.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
قديم
محدث
المدة
الزمان
علم بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of time before arguing for it.
Correction: The proof does not assume time; it argues that the very distinction between eternal and originated cannot be made without time, so time must be self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth proof, why must knowledge of time be self-evident?
A) Because time is directly perceived by the senses.
B) Because the concepts of 'eternal' and 'originated' cannot be conceived without presupposing time.
C) Because the Quran explicitly states that time exists.
D) Because all philosophers agree on the existence of time.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of time is self-evident because the fundamental distinction between eternal and originated cannot be made without presupposing time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the existence of time is self-evident to your intellect, why do you often live as if time is just a blurry background, ignoring the urgency of preparing for the eternal? What specific change can you make this week to live with more consciousness of time's reality?
?
The proof shows that you cannot conceive of being 'originated' without time. Do you truly ponder that you are a originated being, with a beginning, and that every moment is a gift? How would your priorities shift if you fully internalized that your time is limited?
?
You have just seen that knowledge of time is built into your very rationality. Yet, do you ever use your intellect to reflect on the fact that behind time lies the Eternal Creator? What one step can you take today to align your life with the reality that time is a sign pointing to the Eternal?

The Sixth Proof: Time's Divisions Prove Its Existence
Sanusi presents the sixth proof that time is known through its necessary divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's sixth proof that time is a real external entity based on the necessary knowledge of its divisions.

Core Idea
Time is known to be real because its divisions are necessarily known and cannot belong to physical objects.

Sanusi now presents a sixth proof for the existence of time. The argument is simple: we necessarily know that time can be divided into years, months, days, and hours. These divisions are known with certainty, and the thing that receives these divisions cannot be a physical object like a stone or a color. Therefore, time must be a real entity existing externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: division → necessary knowledge → distinction → conclusion.
Sanusi uses 'pure intellect' as the starting point.
2
Identify the divisions listed
The passage divides time into years, months, days, and hours. Note that each part is a part of the next larger unit.
This hierarchical division is key.
3
Recognize the claims of necessary knowledge
Sanusi asserts that knowledge of these divisions and their relative sizes is necessary (daruri). That means it is self-evident and indubitable.
Necessary knowledge does not require proof.
4
Understand the distinction from physical objects
The thing that accepts these divisions (time) is not like blackness, whiteness, stone, or triangle—physical objects.
This shows time is not a physical substance.
5
Grasp the conclusion
If time were not real in external entities, it could not be the subject of such divisions in reality. Since we necessarily know the divisions, time must exist externally.
Thus, knowledge of time's existence is self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إن صريح العقل حاكم بأنه يمكن قسمة الزمان إلى السنين، وقسمة السنين إلى الشهور، وقسمة الشهور إلى الأيام ، وقسمة الأيام إلى الساعات ، ويعلم بالضرورة أن الساعة جزء من اليوم الذي هو جزء من الشهر ، الذي هو جزء من السنة ، التي هي جزء من المدة . والعلم بحصول هذه التقديرات والتقسيمات ، علم ضروري . والعلم يكون بعضها أقل من بعض، أو أكثر من بعض علم ضروري . ومن المعلوم بالضرورة : أن المحكوم عليه بقبول هذه التقسيمات مغاير للسواد والبياض والحجر والمثلث ، وأن ذلك الأمر ما لم يكن متحققاً في الأعيان ، امتنع كونه مورداً لهذه التقسيمات في الأعيان ، فثبت : أن العلم بوجود الزمان والمدة علم بديهي
The sixth proof: Pure intellect rules that it is possible to divide time into years, and the division of years into months, and the division of months into days, and the division of days into hours, and it is known necessarily that the hour is a part of the day which is a part of the month, which is a part of the year, which is a part of duration. And knowledge of the occurrence of these estimations and divisions is necessary knowledge. And knowledge that some of them are less than others or greater than others is necessary knowledge. And it is known necessarily that that which is judged to accept these divisions is other than blackness, whiteness, stone, and triangle, and that that thing, if it were not realized in external entities, it would be impossible for it to be a subject for these divisions in external entities. So it is established that knowledge of the existence of time and duration is self-evident knowledge.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage presents a sixth proof (al-hujjah al-sadisah) for the self-evident nature of time. The author begins by stating that pure intellect rules that time can be divided into years, months, days, and hours. He then notes that it is known necessarily (bi-al-darurah) that the hour is part of the day, which is part of the month, which is part of the year, which is part of duration (al-muddah). The knowledge of these estimations and divisions, as well as the comparative sizes (some smaller, some larger), is also necessary. Next, he argues that the subject which accepts these divisions (i.e., time) is distinct from physical objects like blackness, whiteness, stone, and triangle. If this subject (time) were not real in external entities (fi al-a'yan), it could not be the recipient of these divisions in external entities. Since we necessarily know these divisions, time must be a real existent, and knowledge of its existence is self-evident.

Key Terms
صريح العقل
ضروري
تقديرات
تقسيمات
أعيان
مدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think time is merely a mental concept with no external reality.
Correction: The proof shows that the divisions of time are known necessarily and apply to something that is not a physical object; thus time must be a real entity existing externally.

Checkpoint
What does the sixth proof use to argue that time exists externally?
A) The fact that time is infinitely divisible.
B) The necessary knowledge of time's divisions and that the subject of those divisions cannot be a physical object like a stone.
C) The consensus of astronomers.
D) The impossibility of measuring motion without time.

Key Takeaway
The necessary knowledge of time's divisions demonstrates that time is a real existent, distinct from physical objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that time is a real existent, not an empty measure. If you truly believed this, how would you change the way you spend the hours of today? Will you treat each moment as a valuable, accountable gift from Allah?
?
The proof shows that knowledge of time's reality is self-evident—you already know it. Yet, do you act as if time is merely a concept, putting off meaningful deeds until 'someday'? What specific procrastination will you confront right now?
?
Since time is distinct from physical objects, your soul's journey through time is separate from your body's changes. Are you preparing your soul for the permanent time after death, or are you lost in the temporary divisions of this world?

The Seventh Proof: Time is Known Through Short and Long Durations
Sanusi presents the seventh proof with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof for the self-evident nature of time, including the objection and its refutation.

Core Idea
Time's duration is known directly through everyday experience of short and long periods.

Sanusi presents a proof based on our direct awareness of time's duration. He argues that our knowledge of short and long durations is self-evident, which implies that time itself is known self-evidently. He then addresses an objection that these durations are merely mental constructs. This lesson guides you through the argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's opening
Read the first few sentences where Sanusi states that everyone knows short and long durations. Notice the everyday example about time until noon.
2
Understand the principle
Identify the logical principle: if knowledge of an attribute is self-evident, then knowledge of existence is even more so. See how this connects the attribute of duration to time itself.
3
Consider the objection
Read the objection that shortness and length might be merely relative matters existing only in the mind. Pause and think about this challenge.
4
Examine the answer
Follow the answer: the mental supposition either corresponds to external reality (supporting the proof) or it does not (making it false, like imagining a stone as a ruby).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note that the division of time into parts is not mere supposition; it has external basis. Therefore the objection fails and the proof stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن كل أحد يعلم بأن هذه المدة قصيرة ، وأن تلك المدة طويلة. فإنه يقال بقي إلى وقت الظهر زمان طويل ، ثم يقال بعد ذلك : إنه لم يبق من تلك المدة إلا القليل. والعلم بحصول هذه الأحوال علم بديهي ، ومتى كان العلم بصفة الشيء بديهياً ، كان العلم بأصل وجوده أولى أن يكون بديهياً ، فثبت : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي . ولا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : هذه القلة ، وهذه الكثرة ، أمران اعتباريان لا حصول لهما إلا في الأذهان والخيال ؟ لأنا نقول : هذا الفرض الذهني ، إن كان مطابقاً للأمر الخارجي ، فقد حصل المطلوب ، وإن لم يكن مطابقاً كان فرضاً كاذباً ، وحكماً باطلاً ، وكان جارياً مجرى ما إذا فرضنا هذا الحجر ياقوتاً ، مع أنه في نفسه ليس كذلك ، ومعلوم أن تقسيم الزمان بالأجزاء العظيمة والصغيرة ، ليس من هذا الباب . فبطل قول من قال : إنه محض الفرض والاعتبار
The seventh proof: Everyone knows that this duration is short and that duration is long. For it is said: 'There remains until noon a long time,' then it is said after that: 'Nothing remains of that duration except a little.' And knowledge of the occurrence of these states is self-evident knowledge. And whenever knowledge of an attribute of a thing is self-evident, knowledge of the very existence of it is more fitting to be self-evident. So it is established that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident. And it cannot be said: 'Why is it not permissible to say that this shortness and this abundance are two relative matters that have no existence except in minds and imagination?' Because we say: This mental supposition, if it corresponds to the external matter, then the desired is achieved; and if it does not correspond, it is a false supposition and an invalid judgment, and it runs the course of when we suppose this stone to be a ruby while in itself it is not so. And it is known that the division of time into great and small parts is not of this category. So the saying of those who say that it is mere supposition and consideration is nullified.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is the seventh proof (al-hujjah al-sabi'ah) for the self-evident nature of time, followed by an objection and its answer. The proof begins with a common experience: everyone knows that some durations are short and others long. For example, we say 'a long time remains until noon' and later 'only a little remains.' Knowledge of these states is self-evident (badihi). The author then applies a principle: if knowledge of an attribute of a thing is self-evident, then knowledge of its existence is even more fitting to be self-evident. So knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident. He then raises an objection: why not say that shortness and length are merely relative (i'tibariyan) and exist only in minds and imagination? The answer: such a mental supposition either corresponds to external reality (in which case the proof stands) or it does not (in which case it is false, like supposing a stone is a ruby). The division of time into great and small parts is not of the same category as false suppositions; it is based on real external divisions. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
قصيرة
طويلة
صفة
اعتباري
ذهني
خارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time durations are purely mental constructs with no external reality.
Correction: The argument shows that our mental suppositions must correspond to external reality for them to be true, and time divisions are not merely imaginary.

Checkpoint
What principle does the seventh proof use to argue from the attribute of time to its existence?
A) If an attribute is known necessarily, the existence of its subject is known with greater necessity.
B) All attributes are self-evident.
C) Time cannot be an attribute because it is a substance.
D) The attribute and the subject are identical.

Key Takeaway
The seventh proof shows that our knowledge of time's existence is self-evident, grounded in our direct experience of durations, and the objection that it is merely mental is invalid because true mental suppositions correspond to external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know without thinking that a wait is long or short. How does this simple, undeniable knowledge expose the lie in your claim that you 'don't have time' for prayer or charity? What specific excuse will you discard today?
?
The objection says time's length is just in the mind. Have you ever acted as if your sins have no real weight because 'time is relative'? How does Sanusi's refutation call you to acknowledge that your actions over time have objective reality before Allah?
?
If knowledge of time's existence is self-evident, why do you still postpone repentance as if time is not real? What is one small change you will make before noon tomorrow to prove you truly believe time is real and fleeting?

The Eighth Proof: Time Known Through Dating and Succession
Eighth proof and concluding remark on the necessity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof for the necessary existence of duration based on universal human experience of time.

Core Idea
Human dating and distinguishing past/future/present proves time is self-evident.

The author now presents the eighth and final proof, which draws on the most universal of human practices: dating and the distinction between past, present, and future. He then concludes by noting that all the proofs converge on the same conclusion — that knowledge of duration is necessary. Let's see how this ordinary human experience grounds a necessary truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and then the translation carefully. Notice the two parts: the proof itself and the concluding remark.
2
Identify the core claim of the proof
What universal practice does Sanusi point to? How does he argue from that practice to the necessary knowledge of duration?
3
Analyze the logical step from attribute to subject
Sanusi says if an attribute is known necessarily, the subject (the described) is even more fitting to be known necessarily. Think about why that is a valid inference.
This principle appears in earlier proofs as well.
4
Understand the concluding remark
The author says all the proofs (5th to 8th) are close and all indicate that knowledge of duration is necessary. Why does he emphasize that they all converge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إن جميع الجهال والعوالم ، يؤرخون ويعلمون أن السنين تتوالى وتتعاقب ، ويميزون ببدائه عقولهم بين الماضي والمستقبل والحال ، وهذه الصفات لا شك أنها صفات المدة والصفة إذا كانت معلومة بالضرورة ، كان الموصوف أولى أن يكون كذلك . واعلم أن هذه الوجوه التي ذكرناها متقاربة ، وهي بأسرها دالة على أن العلم بوجود المدة ضروري
The eighth proof: All the ignorant and the learned date events and know that the years follow one another and succeed each other, and they distinguish by the intuitions of their intellects between the past, the future, and the present. And these attributes are undoubtedly attributes of duration. And if an attribute is known necessarily, then the described is more fitting to be so. And know that these proofs that we have mentioned are close to each other, and all of them indicate that knowledge of the existence of duration is necessary.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage consists of two parts: the eighth proof (al-hujjah al-thaminah) and a concluding remark. The eighth proof argues that even all people, both ignorant and learned, use dating (ta'rikh) and know that years follow each other in succession. They distinguish by the intuitions of their intellects between past, future, and present. These attributes (succession, pastness, etc.) are undoubtedly attributes of duration (al-muddah). The author applies the same principle as before: if an attribute is known necessarily, then the subject (the described) is even more fitting to be known necessarily. Therefore, knowledge of the existence of duration is necessary. The second part is a remark (i'lam) that all the proofs mentioned (from the fifth to the eighth) are close to each other and all indicate that knowledge of the existence of duration is necessary (daruri).

عوالم
تتوالى
تتعاقب
ماضي

حال
صفة
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only learned people can grasp time.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that even ignorant people date events and distinguish temporal states, showing time is known to all.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth proof, what universal human practice shows knowledge of time is self-evident?
A) The construction of clocks.
B) Dating events and distinguishing past, present, and future.
C) The scientific study of time.
D) The philosophical debate about time.

Key Takeaway
The universal human practice of dating and distinguishing past, present, and future proves that knowledge of duration is self-evident and necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You instinctively distinguish between past, present, and future—yet do you live as if the present is slipping away, or do you waste it on distractions? What would change today if you truly internalized that your knowledge of time’s passage is a self-evident reality from which there is no escape?
?
If even the ignorant understand the succession of years, what is your excuse for ignoring the inevitable end? When you look at your calendar, do you see merely dates, or do you see the moments that will account for your very soul?
?
The proof shows that time is not an illusion but a real necessary existence that all humans recognize. How would you live differently if you stopped pretending you had unlimited time? What one concrete action will you take this week to align your life with the reality of time’s passing?

The Ninth Proof: Time Known Through Motions
Sanusi presents the ninth proof: time is known through necessary judgments about motions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses our necessary judgments about motions being together or apart to prove that time is self-evidently known.

Core Idea
Our necessary knowledge of motion relations proves time is real.

This passage presents the ninth proof for the self-evident nature of time. Sanusi begins with everyday observations: we inevitably judge two motions as starting together or one before the other. These judgments are known necessarily, without reflection. By analyzing what these judgments imply, he shows that time must be a real container in which motions occur, and that our knowledge of time is itself self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read through the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own words and argument structure come through before you analyze.
Notice that Sanusi uses a simple example to lead to a profound conclusion.
2
Identify the two types of motion judgments
The passage presents two cases: simultaneity (both motions begin and end together) and priority/posteriority (one motion starts before the other). In both cases, the knowledge is described as necessary (ḍarūrī).
What makes this knowledge 'necessary'? It is immediate and cannot be doubted in everyday experience.
3
Trace the implication to time
Sanusi then argues that we cannot conceive of simultaneity except as motions occurring in one time, and priority as one motion in a preceding time. Since these judgments are necessary and entail time, the existence of time must itself be known necessarily (self-evident).
This is a key move: showing that a necessary judgment about motions requires time as a real entity, not just a relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنا قد نحكم على حركتين بأنهما ابتدأنا معاً ، وانقطعتا معاً ، ويكون علمنا بهذه المعية في الابتداء وفي الانقطاع : علماً ضرورياً . وقد نقول في حركتين أخريين : إن إحداهما ابتدأت قبل الأخرى ، وانقطعت قبلها وبعدها . وعلمنا بهذا التقدم والتأخر علم ضروري ، ثم لا تعقل من هذه المعية إلا أنها حصلتا في زمان واحد ، ولا نعقل من هذا التقدم والتأخر إلا أن أحدهما حصل في الزمان السابق على زمان حدوث هذا المتأخر . وكل ذلك يدل على أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي
The ninth proof: We may judge of two motions that they began together and ceased together, and our knowledge of this simultaneity in beginning and in cessation is necessary knowledge. And we may say of two other motions that one of them began before the other and ceased before it and after it. And our knowledge of this precedence and posteriority is necessary knowledge. Then we do not conceive of this simultaneity except that they occurred in one time, and we do not conceive of this precedence and posteriority except that one of them occurred in the time preceding the time of occurrence of this posterior one. And all of that indicates that knowledge of the existence of duration and time is self-evident knowledge.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the ninth proof (al-hujjah al-tasi'ah) for the self-evident nature of time. The author starts by observing that we sometimes judge two motions to have begun together and ended together; our knowledge of this simultaneity (al-ma'iyyah) is necessary (daruri). Similarly, we judge that one motion began before another and ended before or after it; our knowledge of this precedence (taqaddum) and posteriority (ta'akhkhur) is also necessary. The author then analyzes these judgments: we cannot conceive of simultaneity except as the two motions occurring in one time, and we cannot conceive of precedence and posteriority except as one occurring in a time before the time of the other. Since these judgments are necessarily known and they entail the existence of time, knowledge of time's existence must itself be self-evident (badihi).

Key Terms
حركتين
معاً
ابتدأ
انقطع
معية
تقدم
تأخر
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that simultaneity and priority are purely relations between motions, without requiring a real time.
Correction: Sanusi argues that we cannot conceive of these relations without positing a single time or preceding time, showing time is a real entity, not a mental construct.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what does our necessary judgment about two motions being simultaneous imply?
A) The motions are the same length
B) The motions occur in one time
C) The motions are causally connected
D) The motions are unreal

Key Takeaway
Our necessary knowledge about motions being together or sequential proves that time is a real entity known by self-evident intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe time is real, but do you act as if every moment is a gift from your Creator that you will be asked about? How would your day change if you truly felt the weight of time's reality?
?
If knowledge of time is self-evident, why do you often procrastinate in obeying Allah? What does that reveal about the sincerity of your faith in the unseen?
?
When you compare your life to others, do you feel you are ahead or behind? How does this necessary knowledge of precedence in time humble you and make you turn to the One who is not bound by time?

Knowledge of Time is Intuitive
Proof that knowledge of time is intuitive with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theologians argue that knowledge of time is intuitive and how they respond to the objection that it is merely a mental construct.

Core Idea
Our awareness of time passing is immediate, not inferred.

This passage presents a key argument about the nature of time in Islamic theology. The author argues that our knowledge of time is intuitive—it does not require reasoning. He then anticipates an objection that concepts like 'little time' are purely mental, and decisively refutes it. This lesson walks through the proof and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the author's argument in his own words.
Notice the concise style typical of theological proofs.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Break the passage into three parts: the initial proof, the objection, and the author's refutation. Look for the general principle used.
The principle is: if knowledge of an attribute is intuitive, knowledge of its existence is even more so.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the objection fails according to the author. Understand the difference between a mental supposition that matches reality and one that is false fantasy.
The division of time is not like imagining a stone to be a ruby.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبق من تلك المدة إلا القليل. والعلم بحصول هذه الأحوال علم بديهي، ومتى كان العلم بصفة الشيء بديهياً، كان العلم بأصل وجوده أولى أن يكون بديهياً، فثبت: أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي. ولا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: هذه القلة، وهذه الكثرة، أمران اعتباريان لا حصول لهما إلا في الأذهان والخيال؟ لأنا نقول: هذا الفرض الذهني، إن كان مطابقاً للأمر الخارجي، فقد حصل المطلوب، وإن لم يكن مطابقاً كان فرضاً كاذباً، وحكماً باطلاً، وكان جارياً مجرى ما إذا فرضنا هذا الحجر ياقوتاً، مع أنه في نفسه ليس كذلك، ومعلوم أن تقسيم الزمان بالأجزاء العظيمة والصغيرة، ليس من هذا الباب. فبطل قول من قال: إنه محض الفرض والاعتبار.
There remains from that duration except a little. And knowledge of the occurrence of these states is intuitive knowledge. Whenever knowledge of a thing's attribute is intuitive, knowledge of its very existence is more deserving of being intuitive. So it is established that knowledge of the existence of matter and time is intuitive. And it is not said: Why is it not permissible to say: this littleness and this muchness are two relational matters that have no existence except in minds and imagination? Because we say: This mental supposition, if it corresponds to external reality, then the desired result is obtained; and if it does not correspond, it is a false supposition and an invalid judgment, and it runs the course of when we suppose this stone to be ruby while it is not so in itself. And it is known that the division of time into large and small parts is not of this type. So the statement of whoever says that it is pure supposition and consideration is invalidated.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) that knowledge of time is intuitive, followed by an objection and its refutation. It begins by stating that our awareness of the passing of a remaining duration is intuitive knowledge. The argument then applies a general principle: if knowledge of a thing's attribute is intuitive, then knowledge of its existence is even more obviously intuitive. Thus, the existence of time is known intuitively. An objector might claim that terms like 'little' and 'much' are merely mental constructs with no external reality. The author refutes this: if the mental supposition matches external reality, the point is conceded; if not, it becomes a falsehood, like imagining a stone to be a ruby. Since dividing time into large and small parts is not a mere fantasy, the objection fails. The passage concludes that the claim that time is pure mental construction is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time and its divisions (little/much) are merely subjective mental impressions with no basis in reality.
Correction: The author argues that if such mental impressions correspond to external reality, they are valid; if not, they are false. Since our division of time is not arbitrary fantasy, it reflects real duration, and knowledge of time is intuitive.

Checkpoint

A) Time is a mental construct.
B) Knowledge of time is intuitive.
C) Time is divisible into parts.
D) Duration cannot be perceived.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of time is intuitive, not derived from reasoning, and the objection that it is purely mental is refuted by the correspondence argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author argues that dividing time into small and large parts corresponds to external reality. Look at your own schedule: do you distinguish between 'a little time' and 'a lot of time' based on genuine perception, or do you deceive yourself about how much time you truly have? How will you use the next 'little bit' of your day differently starting now?
?
Someone might claim 'long' and 'short' are just mental categories. But in your heart, you know the difference between a fleeting moment of sin and a long night of prayer. That knowledge is intuitive—you cannot deny it. Are you using that undeniable awareness to bring yourself closer to Allah, or are you numbing it with distractions? What is one concrete step you will take today to honor the reality of time?

The Eighth Proof: Time is Intuitively Known Through Universal Dating
All people date events and distinguish past, present, future intuitively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal human practice of dating events and distinguishing past, present, and future proves that knowledge of time is intuitive and necessary.

Core Idea
Dating events is intuitive proof that time is known necessarily.

In this lesson, we examine the eighth proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which appeals to a simple yet powerful observation: all people, regardless of education, engage in dating events and naturally differentiate between past, present, and future. This universal behavior reveals that our awareness of time is not learned but innate. The author argues that since the attributes of time (past, present, future) are known intuitively, the existence of time itself is even more evidently intuitive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the logical structure: observation, attributes, principle.
2
Identify the universal experience
Find the phrase describing what all people do: 'dating events' and 'distinguishing past, present, future'. Why is this evidence?
3
Understand the logical principle
The passage ends with a principle: if an attribute is known necessarily, the described subject is even more certainly known. Apply this to time.
This connects the universal experience to the necessary knowledge of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة: إن جميع الجهال والعوالم، يؤرخون ويعلمون أن السنين تتوالى وتتعاقب، ويميزون ببدائه عقولهم بين الماضي والمستقبل والحال، وهذه الصفات لا شك أنها صفات المدة والصفة إذا كانت معلومة بالضرورة، كان الموصوف أولى أن يكون كذلك.
The eighth proof: All the ignorant and the learned date [events] and know that years follow one another and succeed, and they distinguish by the intuitions of their minds between the past, the future, and the present. These attributes are without doubt attributes of duration. And when the attribute is known necessarily, the described is more deserving of being so.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the eighth proof that knowledge of time is intuitive. It appeals to universal human experience: all people, regardless of education, engage in dating events and recognize the succession of years. They naturally differentiate between past, future, and present using their innate reasoning. These three temporal categories are attributes of duration (muddah). The author applies a logical principle: if an attribute (like being past) is known necessarily, then the subject possessing that attribute (time itself) is even more certainly known necessarily. Thus, the existence of time is intuitive.

Correction: The proof shows that all people, including the uneducated, engage in dating and temporal distinction, indicating it relies on innate awareness of time.

The Ninth Proof: Time from Motion Relations
Ninth proof: necessary knowledge of motion simultaneity implies time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how our intuitive judgments about motions—their simultaneity and priority—necessarily imply the existence of time as an entity distinct from motion.

Core Idea
Our perception of motions starting or ending together secretly demands time.

This lesson presents the ninth proof from the text, which grounds the existence of time in our most basic, intuitive judgments about motion. You will see that when we judge two motions as simultaneous or sequential, we cannot make sense of those judgments unless time exists as a container. The author argues that this knowledge is necessary and immediate.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its structure
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the author contrasts two scenarios: motions that begin and end together, and motions where one precedes the other.
The proof is built on a pair of contrasting examples.
2
Identify the necessary judgments
The author says our knowledge of simultaneity (ma‘iyya) and priority (taqaddum)/posteriority (ta’akhkhur) is necessary (ḍarūrī). Ask yourself: why can't these be ordinary inductive knowledge?
These judgments are intuitive, not derived from observation.
3
Understand the inference to time
The passage states that simultaneity can only be conceived as occurring in one time, and priority/posteriority as occurring in earlier and later times. This shows time is implicit in our concepts.
We cannot think these relations without positing time.
4
Connect to the broader argument
The passage concludes that knowledge of time (and duration) is intuitive and that all the proofs are consistent. Reflect on how this proof differs from the earlier ones based on motion's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة: إنا قد نحكم على حركتين بأنها ابتدأنا معاً، وانقطعتا معاً، ويكون علمنا بهذه المعية في الابتداء وفي الانقطاع: علماً ضرورياً. وقد نقول في حركتين أخريين: إن إحداهما ابتدأت قبل الأخرى، وانقطعت قبلها وبعدها. وعلمنا بهذا التقدم والتأخر علم ضروري، ثم لا تعقل من هذه المعية إلا أنها حصلا في زمان واحد، ولا نعقل من هذا التقدم والتأخر إلا أن أحدهما حصل في الزمان السابق على زمان حدوث هذا المتأخر. وكل ذلك يدل على أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي. واعلم أن هذه الوجوه التي ذكرناها متقاربة، وهي بأسرها دالة على أن العلم بوجود المدة ضروري.
The ninth proof: We may judge concerning two motions that they began together and stopped together, and our knowledge of this simultaneity in beginning and in stopping is necessary knowledge. And we may say concerning two other motions that one of them began before the other and stopped before it and after it. And our knowledge of this priority and posteriority is necessary knowledge. Then we do not conceive of this simultaneity except that they occurred in one time, and we do not conceive of this priority and posteriority except that one of them occurred in the time preceding the time of occurrence of this latter. And all of that indicates that knowledge of the existence of matter and time is intuitive. And know that these proofs that we have mentioned are close [to each other], and they all indicate that knowledge of the existence of duration is necessary.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage consists of the ninth proof and a concluding summary. The ninth proof examines our intuitive judgments about motions. We naturally know when two motions are simultaneous—begining and ending together—and we also know when one precedes another. These judgments are necessary (daruri). In understanding simultaneity, we cannot avoid positing a single time in which both occur. Similarly, priority and posteriority require a concept of earlier and later times. Thus, the existence of time is presupposed by these basic perceptions. After this proof, the author notes that all the proofs mentioned so far are closely related and collectively establish that knowledge of time is necessary.

تقدم
تأخر
مدة

ضروري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simultaneity is a relation that can exist without time.
Correction: The proof shows that we cannot conceive of simultaneity except as occurring in one time, so time is necessary for simultaneity.

Checkpoint

A) Simultaneity is merely conceptual.
B) Simultaneity implies a single time.
C) Time is a product of motion.
D) Priority and posteriority are illusions.

Key Takeaway
Our intuitive judgments about motion simultaneity and priority only make sense if time exists; therefore, knowledge of time is necessary and immediate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You make judgments about motion simultaneity every day without thinking. After reading this proof, do you now see that those judgments secretly depend on a concept of time that is necessary and intuitive? How does that realization affect your trust in your own mind's ability to grasp fundamental truths?

The author says our knowledge of time is 'necessary knowledge' (ḍarūrī). Reflect on a time when you felt absolutely certain about something without being able to prove it. How does that feeling compare to the certainty you have about time? What does this tell you about your own soul's connection to reality?
?
If time is indeed a necessary precondition for judging motion, then your very ability to notice change in the world is a sign of a deeper order. How does this reshape your awareness of the moment you are in right now? What one change in your perception can you make today to live more consciously of this truth?

Tenth Proof: Understanding Fast and Slow Motion Necessitates Time
Understanding fast and slow motion necessitates positing time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intuitive judgment of relative speed logically requires the existence of time as a necessary concept.

Core Idea
Fast and slow motions can only be defined relative to time and distance.

Al-Shahrastani presents a powerful intuitive argument for the existence of time. He begins with a simple observation: we all know that some motions are slower than others, like an ant's crawl versus a bird's flight. But can we explain what 'slower' means without invoking time? This lesson unpacks the proof that our very ability to compare speeds forces us to acknowledge time as a necessary concept.

Guided Path
1
Start with an intuitive observation
Read the passage and note the claim that we make an intuitive judgment that one motion is slower than another, e.g., ant vs bird. Reflect on this: do you need any prior knowledge to make that comparison?
This judgment is presented as self-evident.
2
Examine the definition of fast and slow
The proof defines fast motion in two ways: covering the same distance in less time, or covering more distance in the same time. Write down these definitions in your own words.
Both definitions explicitly depend on time.
3
Link definitions to the intuitive judgment
Observe that without the concept of time, we cannot explain what makes one motion faster than another. The definition requires time. So the intuitive judgment implicitly relies on time.
4
Draw the conclusion about time's existence
Since our intuitive knowledge of speed comparison is sound, and it logically depends on time, the existence of time must also be known intuitively. The passage concludes that knowledge of time's existence is intuitive.
This is the main point of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة: إنا نحكم حكماً بديهياً بأن هذه الحركة أبطأ من تلك الأخرى. مثل قولنا: إن دبيب النملة أبطأ من طيران الطائر، ثم إنه لا يمكننا تفسير الحركة البطيئة، والحركة السريعة، إلا بأنا نقول: السريعة هي التي تقطع مسافة مساوية لمسافة البطيئة في زمان أقل من زمانها، أو التي تقطع مسافة أطول من مسافة البطيئة في زمان مساوي لزمانها، فلما ثبت أن العلم يكون الحركة بطيئة وسريعة علم بديهي، وثبت أنه لا يمكن فهم تصور ماهية الحركة السريعة وماهية الحركة البطيئة، إلا بعد الاعتراف بوجود المدة والزمان، علمنا: أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي.
The tenth proof: We judge intuitively that this motion is slower than that other one, like our saying: The crawling of an ant is slower than the flying of a bird. Then it is not possible for us to explain slow motion and fast motion except by saying: The fast one is that which covers a distance equal to the distance of the slow one in a time less than its time, or that which covers a distance longer than the distance of the slow one in a time equal to its time. Since it is established that knowledge of motion being slow and fast is intuitive, and it is established that understanding the essence of fast motion and the essence of slow motion is not possible except after acknowledging the existence of matter and time, we know that knowledge of the existence of matter and time is intuitive.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is the tenth proof for the intuitive knowledge of time. It begins with an intuitive judgment: we know that one motion is slower than another, such as an ant's crawl versus a bird's flight. The author then defines fast and slow in terms of time and distance: a fast motion covers the same distance in less time, or a longer distance in the same time. This definition is necessary to explain the concepts. Since our ability to conceive of fast and slow motions depends on the notion of time, and since our initial judgment of slowness is intuitive, it follows that our knowledge of time's existence is also intuitive.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think speed is perceived directly without any need for time.
Correction: The proof shows that the very definition of fast and slow necessarily involves time; thus, speed comparison is not direct but depends on the concept of time.

C) Motion that feels quick.
D) Motion that has high velocity.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive comparison of speeds proves that the concept of time is necessary and self-evident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Time is Not Motion: The Blind Man's Awareness
First proof that time is not motion using blind man's awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of a blind man's awareness of time demonstrates that time is not dependent on celestial motion.

Core Idea
Time is perceived intuitively, not derived from motion.

This lesson introduces a key argument from the classical theological tradition that time is not identical to the motion of the spheres. The author uses the example of a blind man in darkness to show that our awareness of time is independent of any visual perception of celestial movements. This intuitive awareness suggests that time is a separate, self-evident reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a statement that time knowledge is intuitive, then the claim that time cannot be motion, then the first proof.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the conclusion is not fully stated.
2
Analyze the blind man example
Focus on the blind man sitting in a dark house. How does he perceive the passage of time without any visual cues? What does this show about the nature of time?
The river analogy emphasizes the continuous flow of time.
3
Consider the implication
If time were motion, could a blind person still perceive time? What does this example imply about time's relationship to physical movement?
Think about what the missing conclusion might be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما ثبت أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي أولى. نقول: هذه المدة لا يجوز أن تكون عبارة عن حركة الفلك، ولا عبارة عن صفة من صفات شيء من حركات الفلك، ويدل عليه وجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن الإنسان الأعمى الجالس في البيت المظلم، المراقب لكيفية انقضاء المدة والزمان، يعلم أن مقدار ما مضى من المدة من الوقت الفلاني، كم هو بالتقريب؟ من غير اعتبار حال حركة الشمس والقمر والكواكب والأفلاك، وهذا العاقل يجد المدة تمضي وتمر مروراً دائماً من غير وقوف البتة. وأنه كالنهر الجاري، والعاقل إذا اعتبر حال نفسه، وجد أن الأمر كما ذكرناه، وجداناً ضرورياً، إلا إذا كان نائماً أو مصروف الفكر إلى غير هذا الاعتبار، ولو أنه توهم الفلك معدوماً، وتوهم الشمس والقمر وجميع الكواكب.
And since it is established that knowledge of the existence of matter and time is intuitive, we say: This duration cannot be an expression of the motion of the sphere, nor an expression of an attribute of any of the motions of the spheres. And evidence for this is [from] proofs: The first proof: A blind man sitting in a dark house, observing the manner of the passing of duration and time, knows approximately how much of the duration has passed from such-and-such a time, without considering the state of the motion of the sun, moon, planets, and spheres. And this rational person finds that duration passes and flows perpetually without stopping at all, and that it is like a flowing river. And the rational person, when he considers his own state, finds the matter as we have described, by necessary finding, unless he is asleep or his thought is diverted to other than this consideration. And if he were to imagine the sphere as non-existent, and were to imagine the sun, moon, and all the planets...
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage begins a new section arguing that time (muddah) is not the same as the motion of the celestial sphere, nor an attribute thereof. The author first reiterates that knowledge of time is intuitive. Then he presents the first proof: a blind man in a dark room, unable to perceive celestial motions, can still apprehend the passage of time approximately. This shows that awareness of time does not depend on observing motion. Furthermore, the rational person experiences time as a continuous flow, like a river, unless distracted by sleep or other thoughts. Even if one imagines the entire celestial sphere and all heavenly bodies as non-existent, the perception of time would still persist (the sentence is cut off, implying the conclusion that time is independent of motion).
