The Tenth Argument: Intuitive Knowledge of Time
The author presents the tenth argument that knowledge of time is intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intuitive judgment of motion speed necessarily presupposes time, establishing that knowledge of time is itself intuitive.

Core Idea
Our everyday judgments of speed implicitly rely on time.

How do we know that time exists? The author presents a straightforward argument: we all make intuitive judgments about which motions are faster or slower. But explaining such judgments forces us to refer to time. If our knowledge of speed is intuitive, and speed requires time, then our knowledge of time must also be intuitive. This lesson traces that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author begins with a concrete example.
2
Understand the example
The example contrasts an ant's crawl with a bird's flight. This illustrates that we instinctively compare speeds.
3
Unpack the explanation
The author claims that the only way to define fast and slow is by referencing time. Fast motion covers equal distance in less time, or more distance in equal time.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since our knowledge of speed is intuitive, and speed cannot be conceived without time, time itself must be known intuitively.
This is a transcendental argument: a necessary condition for a self-evident fact is itself self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إنا نحكم حكماً بديهياً بأن هذه الحركة أبطأ من تلك الأخرى . مثل قولنا : إن دبيب النملة أبطأ من طيران الطائر ، ثم إنه لا يمكننا تفسير الحركة البطيئة ، والحركة السريعة ، إلا بأنا نقول : السريعة هي التي تقطع مسافة مساوية لمسافة البطيئة في زمان أقل من زمانها ، أو التي تقطع مسافة أطول من مسافة البطيئة في زمان مساوي لزمانها ، فلما ثبت أن العلم يكون الحركة بطيئة وسريعة علم بديهي، وثبت أنه لا يمكن فهم تصور ماهية الحركة السريعة وماهية الحركة البطيئة ، إلا بعد الاعتراف بوجود المدة والزمان ، علمنا : أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي .
The tenth argument: We judge by an intuitive judgment that this motion is slower than that other. For example, we say: The crawling of an ant is slower than the flight of a bird. Then we cannot explain slow motion and fast motion except by saying: The fast is that which covers a distance equal to the distance of the slow in a time less than its time, or that which covers a longer distance than the distance of the slow in a time equal to its time. So when it is established that the knowledge of a motion being slow and fast is intuitive, and it is established that it is impossible to understand the conception of the essence of fast motion and the essence of slow motion except after acknowledging the existence of duration and time, we know that the knowledge of the existence of duration and time is intuitive.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument for the intuitive knowledge of time. The author begins by stating that we make an intuitive judgment that one motion is slower than another. For example, the crawling of an ant is slower than the flight of a bird. Then he argues that we cannot explain slow and fast motion except by referencing time: fast motion covers an equal distance in less time, or a greater distance in equal time. Since our knowledge of slowness and speed is intuitive, and understanding these concepts requires acknowledging time, it follows that our knowledge of time's existence is also intuitive. The argument proceeds by showing that the very concept of speed presupposes time, thus time is a necessary presupposition of intuitive knowledge.

Misconception: Some might think time is derived from or inferred from motion.
Correction: The argument shows that motion concepts already presuppose time; time is not derived but is a necessary condition for motion comparisons.

B) It covers equal distance in less time, or more distance in equal time

Key Takeaway
Our intuitive sense of speed reveals that time is a fundamental, self-evident reality.

?

?
If time is self-evident and not derived from anything else, then it is a fundamental gift from Allah. How are you using this gift? Is your time spent in ways that reflect your ultimate purpose, or are you drifting along unconsciously?

Time is Not Motion: The First Proof from Intuitive Perception
First proof that time is distinct from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time cannot be identical to celestial motion, based on the intuitive perception of duration even in the absence of observed motion.

Core Idea
Time is known directly by the mind, not through motion.

This lesson presents the first proof that time is not the motion of the spheres. The author argues that even a blind person in a dark room, or someone imagining all celestial bodies non-existent, still experiences the passage of time. This shows that our knowledge of time is intuitive and independent of motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
Notice the author's use of thought experiments.
2
Identify the author's claim
The author states clearly that duration (al-midda) cannot be an expression of celestial motion. Find this claim in the text.
3
Follow the first thought experiment
Consider the blind man in a dark room. He can still know the passage of time without seeing the sun or stars. This shows time is perceived intuitively.
The author emphasizes 'without considering the state of motion of the sun, moon, planets, and spheres.'
4
Consider the stronger case
The author then asks you to imagine a deaf-blind person from birth, who knows nothing of celestial bodies. Even this person, when stilling his own motion, finds duration constant in his mind.
This removes even internal motion like breath and eyelids.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that knowledge of duration is necessary (daruri) and that duration is distinct from all motions. Ask yourself: why does this matter for the nature of time?
This supports the overall goal of separating time from motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا قد نبهنا بهذه الوجوه العشرة التي ذكرناها على كثير من الوجوه المغايرة لها، مع أن في الواحد منها كفاية في إثبات هذا المطلوب . ولما ثبت أن العلم بوجود المدة والزمان علم بديهي أولى . نقول : هذه المدة لا يجوز أن تكون عبارة عن حركة الفلك ، ولا عبارة عن صفة من صفات شيء من حركات الفلك، ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن الإنسان الأعمى الجالس في البيت المظلم ، المراقب لكيفية انقضاء المدة والزمان، يعلم أن مقدار ما مضى من المدة من الوقت الفلاني، كم هو بالتقريب ؟ من غير اعتبار حال حركة الشمس والقمر والكواكب والأفلاك، وهذا العاقل يجد المدة تمضي وتمر مروراً دائماً من غير وقوف البتة . وأنه كالنهر الجاري، والعاقل إذا اعتبر حال نفسه، وجد أن الأمر كما ذكرناه، وجدانا ضرورياً، إلا إذا كان نائماً أو مصروف الفكر إلى غير هذا الاعتبار، ولو أنه توهم الفلك معدوماً، وتوهم الشمس والقمر وجميع الكواكب معدومة، وتوهم جميع المتحركات ساكنة، وفرضنا أن هذا الإنسان غفل عن جميع المتحركات، لوجد عقله جازماً بمرور هذا الشيء الذي سميناه بالمدة، بل نقول : لو فرضنا رجلاً أصم أعمى من أول الخلقة، بحيث ما أبصر شيئاً من الكواكب والأفلاك ولم يسمع أن الله خلق هذه الأشياء البتة . ثم فرضنا أن هذا الإنسان تكلف تسكين النفس والطرف والحدقة فإنه عند هذه الأحوال يجد هذه المدة أمراً ثابتاً مستمراً في الذهن والعقل، وكل ذلك يدل على أن العلم بوجود هذه المدة : علم ضروري وأن العلم بأن هذه المدة موجودة أمر مغاير لحركات الافلاك والكواكب أيضاً : علم ضروري .
And know that we have drawn attention by these ten aspects which we have mentioned to many aspects different from them, even though one of them is sufficient to establish this demand. And when it is established that the knowledge of the existence of duration and time is an intuitive knowledge, we say: This duration cannot be an expression of the motion of the sphere, nor an expression of an attribute of any of the motions of the sphere. Evidence for this is indicated by aspects: The first proof: A blind person sitting in a dark house, observing how duration and time pass, knows approximately how much duration has passed from a certain time, without considering the state of the motion of the sun, moon, planets, and spheres. This rational person finds duration passing and flowing continuously without stopping at all, and it is like a flowing river. When a rational person considers his own state, he finds the matter as we have described, by necessary sensation, unless he is asleep or his thought is distracted to other considerations. If he imagined the sphere non-existent, imagined the sun, moon, and all planets non-existent, imagined all moving things stationary, and we assumed that this person is oblivious to all moving things, he would find his mind firmly convinced of the passage of that thing which we call duration. Rather, we say: If we assume a man who is deaf and blind from the beginning of creation, such that he never saw anything of the planets and spheres and never heard that God created these things at all, then we assume that this person undertakes to still his breath, his eyelids, and his pupil, then in these states he finds this duration as a constant, continuous thing in the mind and intellect. All of this indicates that the knowledge of the existence of this duration is necessary knowledge, and that the knowledge that this duration exists as something different from the motions of the spheres and planets is also necessary knowledge.

Commentary
This passage first notes that the previous ten arguments suffice, then states that time cannot be motion or an attribute of motion. The first proof is presented: a blind person in a dark room can still sense the passage of time without reference to celestial motions. This shows that time is perceived directly. Even if one imagines all celestial bodies non-existent and all motion stopped, one still perceives the passage of time. The author goes further: even a deaf-blind person from birth, who knows nothing of celestial bodies, will still find duration constant in his mind when he stills his own movements. This proves that the knowledge of duration is necessary and that duration is distinct from motion.

Time vs Motion: The Reversal Proof
Sanusi argues time ≠ motion via reversibility contrast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the irreversibility of time shows it is not motion.

Core Idea
Time cannot be reversed; motion can. Therefore time is not motion.

Sanusi presents a second argument to prove that time is not identical to motion. He uses a thought experiment: we can imagine reversing the direction of celestial motion, but we cannot imagine reversing the order of time itself. This intuitive difference reveals a fundamental distinction between the two.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author uses a hypothetical example about the sphere's motion.
The passage contrasts what the intellect finds plausible for motion vs. time.
2
Identify the two premises
Find where the author describes the intellect's judgment about motion (reversible) and about time (irreversible). These are the two key premises.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the author concludes that time is not motion, nor an accident or consequent of motion.
This shows time has a fixed directionality that motion lacks.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Ask yourself: why is this a convincing proof? How does it appeal to innate intuition (fitra)?
The proof relies on a self-evident intellectual judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن العقل بحكم الفطرة الأصلية ، لا يستبعد أن ينقلب الجزء المتقدم من الحركة الفلكية متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر متقدماً ، فإن الفلك لما تحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب كانت حركته في الربع الشرقي متقدمة على حركته في الربع الغربي ، ولو أنا فرضنا أن هذا الفلك بعينه ، كان يتحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق ، لكانت حركته في الربع الغربي متقدمة على حركته في الربع الشرقي . فعلمنا : أن العقل بفطرته الأصلية لا يستبعد أن يفرض الجزء المتقدم من الحركة الفلكية متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر متقدماً . وأما الجزء المتقدم من الزمان ، فإن العقل بفطرته الأولية البديهية ، يستبعد أن يفرضه متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر يستبعد أن يفرضه متقدماً . فثبت : أن هذه المدة ليست نفس الحركة، ولا عارضاً من عوارضها ، ولا لاحقاً من لواحقها .
The second proof: The intellect, by original natural disposition, does not deem it improbable that the earlier part of the celestial motion be turned into later, and the later part into earlier. For when the sphere moved from east to west, its motion in the eastern quadrant was earlier than its motion in the western quadrant. If we assume that this same sphere moved from west to east, its motion in the western quadrant would be earlier than its motion in the eastern quadrant. So we know that the intellect, by its original natural disposition, does not deem it improbable to suppose the earlier part of celestial motion as later, and the later part as earlier. As for the earlier part of time, the intellect by its primary intuitive natural disposition deems it improbable to suppose it later, and deems it improbable to suppose the later part earlier. So it is established that this duration is not identical to motion, nor an accident of its accidents, nor a consequent of its consequents.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof that time is not motion. The author argues that the intellect can conceive of reversing the order of parts of celestial motion: if the sphere moved from west to east instead of east to west, the earlier part would become later. However, the intellect cannot conceive of reversing the order of parts of time: the earlier part of time cannot be later. This shows that time has a fixed directionality that motion does not, proving that time is not motion nor an attribute of motion.

Correction: The proof shows time has a property (irreversibility) that motion lacks, so time cannot be an accident of motion.

B) That celestial motion can be reversed in direction
C) That time is an accident of motion

Key Takeaway
Time has fixed directionality; motion does not; therefore time is not motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If time were merely a property of motion, you could theoretically undo your sins by changing your actions. But time has its own fixed order. What sin have you been deferring repentance on, assuming you could just 'move differently' later? When will you repent?

Third Proof: Doubting Celestial Motion but Not Time
The author presents the third proof by contrasting doubt about celestial motion with certainty about time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the possibility of doubting celestial motion, but not time itself, supports the distinction between time and motion in proofs for the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
You can doubt whether stars moved long ago, but you cannot doubt time itself.

The author now presents a third proof that helps separate the concept of time from physical motion. The argument invites you to consider a simple thought experiment: can you doubt that celestial motions occurred a hundred thousand years ago? Yes, you can — especially if you accept the world's creation. But can you doubt the passage of that time? The following passage makes this point, though the conclusion is implicit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to understand the literal meaning.
2
Identify the doubt
Notice that the doubt is specifically about celestial motion occurring a hundred thousand years ago, not about time itself.
The proponents of temporal origination are used as an example of those who would doubt.
3
Consider the contrast
Reflect on why one cannot doubt the passage of time even if they doubt the motion. This shows time is more fundamental.
The argument implies that time is not identical to any particular motion.
4
Reflect on the proof's purpose
Understand that this proof is part of a larger argument for the temporal origination of the world, distinguishing time from motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه يمكننا أن نتشكك في أن هذه الحركة الفلكية ، هل كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت بمائة ألف سنة ؟ فإن القائلين بحدوث العالم يمكنهم أن يتشككوا في حصول الحركات الفلكية قبل هذا الوقت بمائة ألف سنة .
The third proof: It is possible for us to doubt whether this celestial motion existed before this time by a hundred thousand years. For the proponents of the temporal origination of the world can doubt the occurrence of celestial motions before this time by a hundred thousand years.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The author points out that one can doubt whether celestial motion existed a hundred thousand years ago. For example, those who believe in the temporal creation of the world can doubt that the celestial motions were occurring before creation. This shows that celestial motion is not essential to the concept of time itself, because time can be conceived without that particular motion. However, the argument as presented here is incomplete; it likely continues to conclude that time itself cannot be doubted, so time is distinct from motion.

The Second Proof: Reason's Innate Judgment on Time and Motion
Ar-Razi presents a rational argument distinguishing time from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to ar-Razi, reason's inability to conceive of time reversal proves that time is not motion.

Core Idea
Reason can imagine reversing motion but not time—so they are distinct.

Ar-Razi presents a second proof that time is not identical to motion. He appeals to a basic rational intuition: we can easily imagine motion happening in the opposite direction, but we cannot imagine the order of time being reversed. This difference shows that time and motion are fundamentally different things. Let's examine the argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
Notice the two distinct scenarios: motion and time.
2
Compare the two scenarios
Identify how reason treats motion reversal vs. time reversal. For motion, reversal is conceivable; for time, it is inconceivable.
This contrast is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the conclusion
The difference in conceivability leads to the conclusion that time (duration) is not motion, nor an accident of motion.
Ask yourself: why does this matter for understanding time?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن العقل بحكم الفطرة الأصلية ، لا يستبعد أن ينقلب الجزء المتقدم من الحركة الفلكية متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر متقدماً ، فإن الفلك لما تحرك من المشرق إلى المغرب كانت حركته في الربع الشرقي متقدمة على حركته في الربع الغربي ، ولو أنا فرضنا أن هذا الفلك بعينه ، كان يتحرك من المغرب إلى المشرق ، لكانت حركته في الربع الغربي متقدمة على حركته في الربع الشرقي . فعلمنا : أن العقل بفطرته الأصلية لا يستبعد أن يفرض الجزء المتقدم من الحركة الفلكية متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر متقدماً وأما الجزء المتقدم من الزمان ، فإن العقل بفطرته الأولية البديهية ، يستبعد أن يفرضه متأخراً ، والجزء المتأخر يستبعد أن يفرضه متقدماً . فثبت : أن هذه المدة ليست نفس الحركة، ولا عارضاً من عوارضها ، ولا لاحقاً من لواحقها .
The second proof: The mind, by virtue of original innate disposition, does not find it improbable that the prior part of celestial motion becomes posterior, and the posterior part becomes prior. For when the celestial sphere moves from east to west, its motion in the eastern quarter is prior to its motion in the western quarter. And if we suppose that this same sphere moved from west to east, its motion in the western quarter would be prior to its motion in the eastern quarter. Thus we know that the mind, by its original innate disposition, does not find it improbable that the prior part of celestial motion be supposed posterior, and the posterior part prior. But as for the prior part of time, the mind, by its primary intuitive disposition, finds it improbable that it be supposed posterior, and the posterior part finds it improbable that it be supposed prior. So it is established that this duration is not motion itself, nor an accident of its accidents, nor a consequence of its consequences.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a proof (حجة) that time (al-mudda/al-zaman) is distinct from motion (al-haraka). The author argues that reason can conceive of reversing the order of parts of motion (e.g., imagining the celestial sphere moving in the opposite direction) without absurdity. However, reason cannot conceive of reversing the order of parts of time (e.g., imagining yesterday after today). This difference shows that time is not motion, nor an accident or consequence of motion. The argument appeals to intuitive rational judgments.

Key Terms
العقل
الفطرة
المدة
الزمان
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time and motion are identical or inseparable.
Correction: This proof shows they are distinct because reason can separate them conceptually: motion parts can be reversed in imagination, but time parts cannot.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between motion and time according to this proof?
A) Motion can be fast or slow, time cannot.
B) Reason can imagine reversing motion but not reversing time.
C) Time is created, motion is eternal.
D) Motion is physical, time is mental.

Key Takeaway
Time and motion are distinct because reason can separate them in thought: motion parts can be reversed imaginatively, but time parts cannot.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You can imagine reversing the order of your daily actions—sleeping before waking, eating before cooking—but can you truly imagine a past moment becoming future? What does this impossibility tell you about your dependence on a Creator who alone controls time? How should this change the way you use each present moment?
?
If time is not merely a product of motion but a distinct creation, then every second of your life is a gift directly from Allah, not just a byproduct of the universe's movement. Are you living with this awareness, or do you take time for granted? What will you do today to honor each moment as a divine trust?
?
This proof shows that your own mind, by its innate disposition, knows that time is irreversible. When you sin, do you ever wish to reverse time to undo it? This very wish reveals that you know the past cannot change. How does this knowledge drive you to seek forgiveness now, before the moment passes forever?

The Third Proof: Distinguishing Time from Motion by Doubt
Ar-Razi argues that doubt is possible about motion but not about time, proving their distinctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ar-Razi's argument that time is distinct from motion based on the asymmetry of doubt.

Core Idea
If you can doubt motion but not time, they are not the same.

Ar-Razi presents a concise argument: we can doubt whether a specific motion existed in the distant past, but we cannot doubt that a duration preceded the present. This asymmetry reveals that time is not motion nor any attribute of motion. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the logical structure: doubt is possible for motion, impossible for duration.
2
Identify the two sides of the comparison
The author contrasts motion (celestial motion) with duration (time). Identify the exact phrasing for each.
Motion: 'هل كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت بمائة ألف سنة'. Duration: 'هل حصل قبل هذه الساعة الحاضرة مدة متقدمة عليها'.
3
Understand why doubt is a logical indicator
If two things were identical, they would be equally open to doubt regarding the same question. The difference in doubt shows they are distinct.
This follows the principle of identity: identical things have identical properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنه يمكننا أن نتشكك في أن هذه الحركة الفلكية ، هل كانت حاصلة قبل هذا الوقت بمائة ألف سنة ؟ فإن القائلين بحدوث العالم يمكنهم أن يتشككوا في حصول الحركات الفلكية قبل هذا الوقت بمائة ألف سنة ، أو أقل أو أكثر. وأما المدة فإنه لا يمكننا أن نتشكك في أنه هل حصل قبل هذه الساعة الحاضرة مدة متقدمة عليها بمائة ألف سنة ؟ ولما أمكن التشكك في الحركة، ولم يمكن التشكك في المدة علمنا أن المدة غير الحركة ، وغير جميع الصفات الفلكية
The third proof: It is possible for us to doubt whether this celestial motion existed before this time by a hundred thousand years? For those who affirm the temporal origination of the world can doubt the occurrence of celestial motions before this time by a hundred thousand years, or less or more. But as for duration, it is not possible for us to doubt whether there existed before this present hour a duration preceding it by a hundred thousand years. Since doubt is possible concerning motion, and not possible concerning duration, we know that duration is other than motion and other than all celestial attributes.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This is another proof distinguishing time from motion. The author points out that we can rationally doubt whether motion existed at a specific past moment (e.g., whether the heavens were moving 100,000 years ago), but we cannot doubt that there was a time period (duration) that preceded the present moment. This difference shows that time is not identical to motion or any celestial attribute.

Correction: The doubt here is a logical device to test identity, not a claim about actual uncertainty of existence.

The Fourth Proof: Multiple Celestial Motions Require a Single Time
Ar-Razi argues that different celestial motions share one time, proving time is distinct from any single motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the multiplicity and diversity of celestial motions prove that time is not identical to any motion but is a distinct container for all motions.

Core Idea
Time is not a specific motion but the single container for all motions.

Ar-Razi presents a powerful logical argument based on the observable fact that celestial circles at different distances from the ecliptic move at different speeds. If time were simply motion, we would face an impossible dilemma: either time is one specific motion or it is all motions together — both lead to absurdity. This lesson unpacks that argument and shows why time must be something else: a universal container.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the flow of the argument register before analysing.
Notice the structure: observation → dilemma → elimination → conclusion.
2
Identify the central dilemma
The passage presents two possibilities if time were motion: either time equals one specific motion or it equals all motions together. Why are both impossible?
The first leads to arbitrary preference, the second to contradiction.
3
Examine the strengthening argument
After the dilemma, Ar-Razi adds an intuitive observation: all motions occur in one time. How does this support the conclusion?
It confirms that time is a container (وعاء) for all motions.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect on how this proof establishes time as something distinct from motion. Write down one sentence capturing the conclusion.
This is the key: time measures all motions but is not itself a motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن أعظم الدوائر الفلكية هي المنطقة ، وأما الدوائر الموازية لها فكل ما كان منها أقرب إلى المنطقة ، كانت حركته أسرع ، وما كان منها أبعد من المنطقة كانت حركته أبطأ . فظهر أن الحركات اليومية الحاصلة في الدوائر الفلكية المتوازية ، والموازنة للمنطقة الفلكية كثيرة ومختلفة بالسرعة والبطء ، فلو كان الزمان عبارة عن حركة الفلك ، لما كان جعله عبارة عن حركة بعض تلك الدوائر ، أولى من جعله عبارة عن حركات سائر الدوائر . فإما أن يكون عبارة عن الكل فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون هذا اليوم الواحد لا يكون يوماً واحداً بل أياماً كثيرة حصلت معاً ، بحسب تلك الدوائر المتوازية ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل ، أو يقال : إنه ليس شيء منها نفس المدة والزمان ، بل المدة والزمان أمر مغاير لها ، مقدر لها بأسرها . وذلك هو المطلوب . ومما يقوى هذا الكلام : أن صريح العقل ناطق بأن حركات كل تلك الدوائر واقعة في زمان واحد ، فالعقل حكم بأن تلك الحركات بأسرها واقعة في زمان واحد ، وإن ذلك الزمان ظرف لها بأسرها ، ووعاء لها بأسرها ، وذلك يوجب القطع بأن الزمان مغاير للحركة .
The fourth proof: The greatest of the celestial circles is the ecliptic. As for the circles parallel to it, whichever is closer to the ecliptic, its motion is faster, and whichever is farther from the ecliptic, its motion is slower. Thus it appears that the daily motions occurring in the parallel celestial circles, parallel to the ecliptic, are many and differ in speed and slowness. So if time were identical to the motion of the celestial sphere, then making it identical to the motion of some of those circles would be no more appropriate than making it identical to the motions of the remaining circles. So either it is identical to all of them, in which case it would follow that this single day would not be a single day but many days occurring together, according to those parallel circles—and no rational person would say that—or it is said that none of them is itself duration and time, but duration and time are something other than them, measuring all of them. And that is the desired conclusion. And what strengthens this statement is that clear reason declares that the motions of all those circles occur in a single time. So reason judges that all those motions occur in a single time, and that time is a container for all of them, a receptacle for all of them. That necessitates certainty that time is other than motion.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is a proof from the multiplicity of celestial motions with varying speeds. If time were a specific motion, it would have to be identified with one particular motion among many, but any choice is arbitrary. If time were all motions together, then a single day would be many days simultaneously, which is absurd. The only coherent explanation is that time is something distinct that measures all motions uniformly. The argument is strengthened by the intuitive recognition that all these motions occur in one time, which serves as their container.

Key Terms
دوائر فلكية
منطقة
حركة
زمان
وعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time might be relative to each motion or identified with some specific motion.
Correction: The argument shows that time cannot be identified with any particular motion or with all motions collectively; rather, time is a single container that measures all motions uniformly.

Checkpoint
Why does the multiplicity of celestial motions prove time is not identical to any single motion?
A) Because they all have different speeds.
B) Because if time were a specific motion, choosing which one would be arbitrary.
C) Because the ecliptic is the biggest circle.
D) Because the stars move differently.

Key Takeaway
Time is not identical to any motion but is a single container measuring all celestial motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fifth Proof: Motion is Fast or Slow, Time is Not
Ar-Razi argues that motion is described as fast or slow, but time never is.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a simple argument from Ar-Razi that distinguishes motion from time based on the applicability of speed and slowness.

Core Idea
Motion can be fast or slow; time cannot — therefore they are distinct.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from Ar-Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The argument is concise: because we describe motion but not time as fast or slow, they must be different realities. Pay attention to how a single difference in description can serve as a proof of distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
The passage is very brief — take note of its straightforward structure.
2
Identify the contrast
Notice that motion is associated with speed and slowness, while time is explicitly denied such descriptions.
This contrast is the core of the proof.
3
Reflect on the implication
If two things have different essential predicates, they cannot be identical. Consider why this difference implies distinct realities.
Think about what it means for time to lack a property that motion has.
4
Address a common misunderstanding
Some might think time can be called 'fast' or 'slow' metaphorically. The argument targets literal descriptions, not figurative speech.
Metaphors don't undermine the distinction; the essence is what matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن الحركة توصف بالسرعة والبطء ، والزمان لا يوصف بذلك ، فإنه لا يقال : هذا الزمان أبطأ من ذلك الزمان ولا أسرع منه
The fifth proof: Motion is described as speed and slowness, while time is not described by that, for it is not said: 'This time is slower than that time,' nor 'faster than it.'
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is a short argument distinguishing time from motion by noting that motion can be characterized as fast or slow, but time cannot be so described. The argument is cut off here, but the implication is that since time lacks this descriptive quality inherent to motion, time and motion are distinct.

The Argument from Speed and Slowness
Argues that speed and slowness imply time is separate from motion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concepts of speed and slowness demonstrate that time is a reality distinct from motion.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness prove time is separate from motion.

This argument draws on our everyday experience of faster and slower motions. By examining how we define speed, the author shows that time is needed as a measure, independent of the motion itself. This passage uses a simple logical definition to establish a fundamental point about the reality of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the initial statement to the definitions and the conclusion.
The passage starts with 'And also:' indicating it is part of a larger discussion.
2
Understand the definition of slower and faster
The passage defines slower and faster in terms of distance and time. Slower either covers the same distance in more time, or covers less distance in the same time. Faster is the opposite.
This definition depends on time as a measure.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that all these considerations show that time is other than motion, not identical to it.
The conclusion is that time is a distinct reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً : فإن سرعة الحركة وبطأها ، إنما يعقلان بسبب المدة والزمان ، فإنه يقال : الأبطأ هو الذي يقطع مثل ما قطعه السريع في زمان أطول ، أو يقطع أقل مما قطعه السريع في مثل زمان السريع ، والأسرع ما يكون بالضد منه ، وكل ذلك يدل على أن الزمان غير الحركة

Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that time is distinct from motion by examining how we conceive speed and slowness. The author states that speed and slowness are understood only through duration and time. He gives the definition: the slower motion covers the same distance as the faster in a longer time, or covers less distance in the same time. The faster is the opposite. The conclusion is that these comparative concepts rely on time, not on motion itself, proving that time is not identical to motion.

الحركة (motion)
المدة (duration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The argument shows that speed and slowness are relational concepts that only make sense when measured against time, so time must be a distinct entity.

Multiple Spheres in Void: A Proof Time ≠ Motion
The sixth proof uses a thought experiment to show time is not motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a thought experiment of multiple simultaneous independent motions demonstrates that time is not identical to motion.

Core Idea
Multiple independent motions can coexist, but multiple times cannot.

This proof employs a thought experiment to argue against the identity of time and motion. By imagining many spheres moving independently in an infinite void, we can conceive multiple motions simultaneously. Yet we cannot conceive multiple times. This intuitive impossibility shows that time must be something separate from motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a compact argument with two premises and a conclusion.
The word [معما؟] is unclear; it likely means 'with them' or 'together'.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Imagine many spheres in an infinite empty void, each separate and independent. Then imagine each sphere moving with its own motion, not dependent on the others.
The void is infinite to ensure no constraints on motion.
3
Grasp the key intuition
The proof says we know by intuition that we cannot conceive two times occurring simultaneously. Reflect on why this is self-evident.
If time were motion, multiple motions would require multiple times.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since we can conceive multiple motions but not multiple times, motion and time cannot be identical. The proof establishes a clear distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : إنه يمكننا تعقل أفلاك كثيرة في الخلاء الذي لا نهاية له ، بحيث يكون كل واحد منها خارجاً عن الآخر ، وغير متعلق به . فعلى هذا التقدير يمكننا تصور حركات كثيرة [معما؟] بحيث لا تكون منها تبعاً للأخرى. ونعلم بالبديهة : أنه لا يمكننا تعقل حصول زمانين ، فالحركة غير الزمان .
The sixth proof: It is possible for us to conceive many spheres in the infinite void, such that each one is outside the other and not connected to it. On this assumption, we can imagine many motions [with them?] such that none of them is dependent on the others. And we know by intuition that it is not possible for us to conceive the occurrence of two times. So motion is other than time.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة السادسة) that time is not motion. The author proposes a thought experiment: imagine many spheres in infinite empty space, each separate and independent. Then imagine each sphere moving with its own motion, independent of the others. In such a scenario, we would have multiple motions occurring simultaneously. However, we intuitively know that we cannot conceive of two times occurring at once. If time were identical to motion, multiple motions would imply multiple times. Since we cannot conceive multiple times, motion must be distinct from time.

Key Terms
الخلاء (void)
البديهة (intuition)
الزمان (time)
الحركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is merely the measure or aspect of motion, so time and motion are identical.
Correction: This proof shows that if time were identical to motion, multiple independent motions would create multiple times, which is inconceivable. Therefore, time must be a single dimension distinct from individual motions.

Checkpoint
Why does the sixth proof conclude that motion is other than time?
A) Because motions are infinite
B) Because multiple motions would require multiple times, which is inconceivable
C) Because spheres cannot move
D) Because time is not real

Key Takeaway
Time is not identical to motion because multiple independent motions can be conceived simultaneously, but multiple times cannot.

The seventh proof: motion occurs in time, not in itself
Sanusi argues that motion cannot be self-located and thus occurs in time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion cannot be self-located and how this proof establishes time as a distinct entity from motion.

Core Idea
Motion needs an external container—time—to occur; it cannot locate itself.

This proof addresses a fundamental question: where does motion happen? The author argues that motion cannot reside in itself or in its attributes, and therefore must occur in something else—time. By negating alternative possibilities, the proof isolates time as the indispensable context for motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated phrase 'or in an attribute subsisting in it'.
2
Identify the positive and negative claims
Find the statement about what we can say (motion occurred in this time) and the list of what we cannot say. Mark each one.
The proof builds on a contrast between what is possible and what is impossible.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Reflect on why the negated options are impossible. Then connect them to the final statement: time is other than motion and its attributes.
This conclusion is the direct result of eliminating all self-location possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : يمكننا أن نقول : هذه الحركة بجميع صفاتها ، حصلت في هذا الزمان ، ولم تحصل في الزمان المتقدم. ولا يمكننا أن نقول : حصلت هذه الحركة في نفسها ، أو في صفة من الصفات القائمة بها ، أو في حركة أخرى ، أو في صفة من الصفات القائمة بها ، أو في حركة أخرى ، وكل ذلك يدل على أن الزمان غير الحركة ، وغير جميع الصفات القائمة بالحركة
The seventh proof: We can say: this motion with all its attributes occurred in this time, and did not occur in the previous time. And we cannot say: this motion occurred in itself, or in an attribute subsisting in it, or in another motion, or in an attribute subsisting in it, or in another motion. And all of that indicates that time is other than motion, and other than all attributes subsisting in motion.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة السابعة) that time is distinct from motion and from all attributes of motion. The author states that we can correctly say a particular motion occurred in this time and not in the previous time. However, we cannot say that it occurred in itself, in an attribute of itself, or in another motion. The impossibility of locating motion in itself or its attributes shows that time is a separate reality in which motion occurs. Thus time is not motion nor any quality of motion.

Key Terms
الزمان (time)
الحركة (motion)
الصفات (attributes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion can be located in itself or its attributes.
Correction: The proof shows that motion cannot be in itself or its attributes; it must occur in time.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh proof say we cannot claim about motion?
A) That it occurred in time
B) That it occurred in itself or its attributes
C) That it is fast
D) That it is slow

Key Takeaway
Time is distinct from motion because motion cannot be located in itself or its attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Eighth Proof: Duration is Distinct from Motion
Proof that duration is distinct from motion via a thought experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that duration (al-muddah) is a reality distinct from motion, based on the conceivability of a time without motion.

Core Idea
We can imagine a time without any motion — so time must be something other than motion.

In this lesson, we examine the eighth rational proof from al-Matalib al-aliyah. The author presents a thought experiment that challenges the common assumption that time depends on motion. By considering a scenario where all celestial bodies are annihilated and then restored after a period, we see that the intervening duration is conceivable and quantifiable, even though no motion occurs. This shows that duration is an independent reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the clear structure: a statement, an example, reasoning, and conclusion.
The proof is self-contained; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand the thought experiment
The author imagines God annihilating the heavens and all planets at the end of time, leaving them in non-existence for a period, then restoring them. The interval between annihilation and restoration is a specific duration that the mind can conceive as longer or shorter.
During this interval, all motion is absent because no celestial bodies exist.
3
Analyze the reasoning
The author argues that this supposition is not intuitively impossible; indeed, many religious people accept it (e.g., the period between death and resurrection). Since it is possible to conceive a time without motion, duration must be something other than motion itself.
The proof appeals to both reason and consensus.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is clear: duration is distinct from motion. All motions are attributes that occur within duration. This proof establishes duration as a fundamental reality.
This has implications for understanding time in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنه يمكننا أن نتصور زماناً خالياً عن الحركة . مثلاً : إذا توهمنا أن الله سبحانه أعدم الفلك ، وأعدم جميع الكواكب عند قيام القيامة ، ثم إنه تعالى ترك هذه الأشياء على العدم المحض مدة مديدة إلى أن يعيدها ، ويعيد الخلائق في موقف القيامة. فالمدة المتوسطة بين أول زمان الإعدام ، وأول زمان الإعادة لا شك أنها تكون مدة مخصوصة ، والعقل يمكنه فرض تلك المدة أقل ، أو أكثر ، أو أنقص ، أو أزيد ، ففي هذا الفرض قد فرضنا جميع الحركات معدومة ، وهذا الفرض ليس فرضاً معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة بل هو أمر ممكن بحسب العقل ، ولذلك فإن جمهور أرباب الملل والأديان جازمون به. ومن المعلوم أن الذي يكون معلوم البطلان بالبديهة ، لا يمكن إطباق الخلق العظيم عليه . فثبت : أن رفع كل الحركات عن العقول والأذهان غير ممتنع فوجب الجزم بأن المدة أمر مغاير للحركة ، والجميع صفات الحركة . وهو المطلوب
The eighth proof: It is possible for us to conceive a time empty of motion. For example: if we imagine that God, exalted, annihilates the sphere and all the planets at the establishment of the Hour, then He leaves these things in absolute non-existence for a long period until He restores them and restores the creatures in the station of resurrection. Then the intermediate period between the first time of annihilation and the first time of restoration is undoubtedly a specific period, and the mind can suppose that period to be less or more, or smaller or greater. In this supposition we have supposed all motions non-existent, and this supposition is not a supposition known to be impossible by intuition; rather it is a possible matter according to reason. Therefore, the majority of the people of religions and faiths are certain of it. And it is known that that which is known to be false by intuition cannot have the great number of creatures agree upon it. So it is established that the removal of all motions from minds and intellects is not impossible. Therefore, it is necessary to be certain that duration is something other than motion, and all [these] are attributes of motion. And that is the desired [conclusion].
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة الثامنة) that duration (المدة) is distinct from motion. The author describes a thought experiment: imagine God annihilates the heavens and all celestial bodies at the end of time, leaving them in pure non-existence for a long period, and then restores them. The intermediate period between annihilation and restoration is a specific duration that the mind can conceive as longer or shorter. In this scenario, all motions are absent. The author argues that this supposition is not intuitively impossible; indeed, many religious people accept it (the period between death and resurrection). Since it is possible to conceive a time without any motion, duration must be something other than motion. The proof concludes that duration is a reality distinct from motion and that all motions are attributes that occur within it.

Key Terms
المدة (duration)
الحركة (motion)
العدم (non-existence)
البديهة (intuition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time cannot exist without motion; time is defined by motion.
Correction: This proof shows that we can rationally conceive a duration with no motion (e.g., between annihilation and restoration). Therefore, duration is a separate reality from motion.

Checkpoint

A) It is identical to motion
B) It is impossible without motion
C) It is something other than motion
D) It is an illusion

Key Takeaway
Duration is a reality distinct from motion, as we can conceive a time without any motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Ninth Proof: Time is Prior to Motion
The ninth proof argues time is prior to motion in conception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why time must be conceptually prior to motion, according to the ninth proof in al-Matalib al-Aliyah.

Core Idea
Time is conceptually prior to motion; motion presupposes temporal afterness.

This lesson presents the ninth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that time is not derived from motion but is a necessary concept for understanding motion. The proof uses both a conceptual analysis of motion and a thought experiment to show that time can be conceived without motion. By examining the essence of motion and the innate awareness of duration, the author establishes the priority of time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get an overall sense of the argument.
Notice the two main parts: the conceptual analysis and the evidence.
2
Understand the core argument
The author argues that motion is understood as transition from place to place, and the 'afterness' involved is temporal. Therefore, to conceive motion, we must already have the concept of time.
This shows time is conceptually prior to motion.
3
Examine the thought experiment
The author asks you to imagine God annihilates all motions (celestial bodies). Even then, you cannot judge duration as null; your innate disposition recognizes time must still exist.
This shows time can be conceived without motion.
4
Reflect on inner awareness of duration
Even when oblivious to external motions, we find in our intellect an extended continuous thing, like flowing water. This internal sense proves time is independent of motion.
This completes the proof of the conceptual priority of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنا لا نعقل ماهية الحركة، إلا إذا عقلنا كون المتحرك حاصلاً في حيز ، بعد أن كان حاصلا في حيز آخر . ولذلك فإن جميع العقلاء لا يفهمون من الحركة : إلا الانتقال من حيز إلى حيز آخر فيكون الحصول في الحيز الثاني واقعاً بعد الحصول في الحيز الأول . وهذه البعدية ليست إلا البعدية الزمانية، فثبت : أنه لا يمكن تعقل ماهية الحركة ، إلا بعد تعقل ماهية المدة والزمان . وأما تعقل المدة والزمان فإنه غني عن تعقل ماهية الحركة. بدليل : أنا لما فرضنا أن الله تعالى أعدم الفلك ، وأعدم الكواكب ، وأعدم كل الحركات، ففي هذه الحالة لم يقدر عقلنا وفكرنا أن يحكم ببطلان المدة ، بل حكمت الفطرة الأصلية ، بأنه لا بد وأن يكون وقت الإعدام مغاير لوقت الوجود . وأيضاً : فقد بينا أن حالنا نكون غافلين عن الفلك والشمس والقمر ، وسائر الكواكب فإنا نجد في عقلنا أمراً ممتداً مستمراً ، يحدث منه شيء فشيء ، كأنه الماء السيال الجاري ، أو كأنه خيط ألقي على طرف سيف ، ثم جرّ . فإن الخيط يلاقي حد السيف جزءاً فجزءاً ، فكذا ههنا
The ninth proof: We do not conceive the essence of motion except when we conceive the moving thing being in a place after it had been in another place. Therefore, all rational people understand by motion nothing but transition from one place to another place, so that being in the second place occurs after being in the first place. And this 'afterness' is nothing but temporal afterness. So it is established that it is not possible to conceive the essence of motion except after conceiving the essence of duration and time. As for conceiving duration and time, it is independent of conceiving the essence of motion. Evidence: when we supposed that God annihilated the sphere, the planets, and all motions, in that state our intellect and thought could not judge the nullity of duration; rather, the original innate disposition judged that necessarily the time of annihilation is different from the time of existence. Also, we have shown that when we are heedless of the sphere, the sun, the moon, and all the planets, we find in our intellect an extended continuous thing, from which something arises little by little, like flowing water or like a thread placed on the edge of a sword and then pulled. For the thread meets the sword's edge part by part, and so likewise here.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a proof (الحجة التاسعة) that time (الزمان) is conceptually prior to motion. The author argues that motion is understood as a transition from one place to another, and that 'afterness' involved is temporal afterness. Thus, to conceive motion, we must already conceive time. In contrast, we can conceive time without motion, as shown by the earlier thought experiment (annihilation of all motions). Additionally, even when we are not thinking of celestial motions, we are aware of a continuous extended thing in our minds, analogous to flowing water or a thread being drawn along a sword. This internal sense of duration proves time is independent of external motion.

Key Terms
ماهية الحركة (essence of motion)
البعدية الزمانية (temporal afterness)
المدة (duration)
الفطرة (innate disposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion defines time, and time cannot be conceived without motion.
Correction: The proof demonstrates the opposite: time is conceptually prior to motion; we can conceive time without motion, but not motion without time.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, what is necessary to conceive the essence of motion?
A) The existence of moving objects
B) The concept of time and duration
C) The presence of space
D) The force of motion

Key Takeaway
Time is a necessary concept for understanding motion, not the other way around.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson argues that time is an innate concept in your intellect, independent of external motion. Have you ever paused to reflect on your own inner awareness of time’s passage? How does that constant, unceasing awareness challenge you to use each moment for what truly matters to Allah?
?
The author presents a thought experiment: even if God annihilated all motion, duration would still exist. If you truly believed that every moment is sustained by Allah directly, not by cause and effect, how would your view of worldly pursuits and your dependence on Allah change?

You now know that time is not defined by the movement of planets but is a gift directly placed in your consciousness. In what specific way will you change your daily routine starting today to ensure that this God-given time is spent in obedience, not squandered?

The Tenth Proof: Implicit Affirmation of Time
Sanusi begins the tenth proof by noting a concession from temporalists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opening of Sanusi's tenth proof, which argues that those who affirm temporal origination implicitly acknowledge the existence of time as a necessary precondition.

Core Idea
Temporal origination presupposes time itself.

In this passage, Sanusi begins his tenth proof by appealing to common ground with those who believe the universe is temporally originated. He points out that their own reasoning concedes the necessity of time and duration before any event. Let's examine how this argument opens, even though it remains incomplete in the available text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

The source text has high OCR risk and ends abruptly.
2
Identify the key concession
The passage states that advocates of temporal origination conceded that before the universe existed, its existence was possible. This concession is the foundation of the proof.
3
Reflect on the implication

This is the core of the incomplete proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن القائلين بحدوث الفلك ، بل القائلين بحدوث جميع الأجسام ، سلموا أن حدوثه قبل أن حدث : ممكن . وهذا يدل على شهادة فطرتهم الأصلية بأنه لا يعقل حدوث شيء ، إلا في مدة وزمان وتمام التقرير في هذا الوجه : إن من القائلين بحدوث العالم ،

And the complete articulation of this aspect: that among those who affirm the temporal origination of the world...

They admit that before the universe existed, its existence was possible. This implies that they acknowledge a duration and time in which that possibility existed. The author argues that even those who deny the eternity of motion must accept time as a precondition for origination. However, the argument is incomplete in the scanned text; it breaks off mid-sentence, so the full reasoning and conclusion are missing.

The Parity Argument for Time
Refutation using future motion to deny temporal container

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the denial of time by drawing a parallel between future and past capacities.

Core Idea
Future time implies past time—consistency demands both or neither.

In this lesson, we examine the second refutation against those who deny time as a container. The author challenges their position by showing that the reasoning they use to deny the past applies equally to the future, exposing a contradiction. This argument uses the concept of possibility (imkān) to show that the opponent's logic forces them to accept time's reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author starts with the future possibility and draws an implication about the present existence of future time.
Pay attention to the logical connective 'فَ' indicating implication.
2
Identify the opponent's objection and rebuttal
The opponent says the present is merely a possibility, not a container. The author turns this against them regarding the past.
This is a tuqid al-‘inal: turning the tables.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
This is an argument by parity of reasoning—what holds for the future must hold for the past. Consider why this undermines the denial of time.
The key is consistency: if future possibility doesn't need a container, neither does past.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : إن ما ذكرتموه معارض بجانب المستقبل ، وذلك لأن الساعة الحاضرة إلى الغد ، حصل إمكان يتسع لمقدار من الحركة الموصوفة بمقدار معين من السرعة أو البطء ، ولا يمتلىء بالأقل منه ولا يتسع للأعظم منه ، وإمكان آخر أصغر منه . فهذا يقتضي أن يكون الزمان الذي سيجيء غداً ، حاصلا الآن ، وحاضراً في هذا الوقت. ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فإن قالوا : الحاضر في الحال إنما هو إمكان وجود تلك الحركات المستقبلية ، لا وجود شيء آخر يكون وعاء لها وظرفاً لها. فنقول : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال في الماضي مثل ما ذكرتموه في المستقبل ؟
And the second of them: Indeed what you have mentioned is contradicted with respect to the future. That is because from the present hour until tomorrow, there occurs a possibility (imkān) that accommodates an amount of motion described by a certain measure of speed or slowness, and it is not filled up by less than it nor does it accommodate greater than it, and another possibility smaller than it. This entails that the time (zamān) that will come tomorrow is present now and present at this time. It is known that this is false. If they say: The present at this moment is only the possibility of existence of those future motions, not the existence of something else that would be a vessel (wi‘ā’) and container (ẓarf) for them, then we say: Why may it not be that the situation with the past is like what you mentioned concerning the future?
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (the second of three) of an argument denying time. The author presents an argument by analogy: just as the opponent admits that the present moment has a certain capacity (imkān) that can accommodate a specific amount of future motion—neither more nor less—so too must they admit that this capacity in the present is something real. But if they claim that this capacity is merely the potential existence of those future motions and not an actual container, then the author turns the tables: why not say the same about the past? That is, the possibility in the past is just the potential existence of past motions, not an actual container. This shows the inconsistency of the opponent's position.

Key Terms
إمكان
الزمان
الحركة
الوعاء
الظرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The present possibility for future motion is an actual container for time.
Correction: The author does not claim it is an actual container; rather, he shows that if it is not a container, then the past also is not, forcing the opponent to accept time as a container for both or neither.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue about the present moment's capacity for future motion?
A) It is an actual container for time.
B) It is merely a logical possibility with no real existence.
C) It is exactly the same as the capacity for past motion.
D) It proves that time does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The possibility of future motion implies a container for future time, and by parity, the past must also have a container, undermining the denial of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself treating the past as more 'real' than the future—believing your past mistakes are fixed while your future actions are still open? How does the parity argument in this passage demand that you view them with the same seriousness? What would change in your life if you truly applied this consistency?
?
The opponent argued that future possibility is not a container, yet the author forces the same on the past. In your own life, do you make excuses for future bad habits (they're not yet here) while condemning past ones? How does this lesson expose your inconsistency, and what step will you take today to align your view of past and future?
?
If you truly believed that the future is as 'present' to God as the past, would you treat your upcoming choices with the same urgency as your past accountability? How does this passage challenge you to live now as if both are equally real? What is one decision you will make today based on this parity?

Third Refutation: Space Between Two Ends
Refutation via spatial container analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analogy of a vessel's fixed capacity demonstrates the necessity of real spatial dimensions, and how this refutes the denial of time as a separate container.

Core Idea
Spatial capacity proves real dimensions; time is analogous.

This lesson presents the third refutation against those who deny time as a real container. The author uses a spatial analogy: the fixed capacity between two ends of a vessel implies real dimensions. He then forces consistency: if they accept spatial containers, they must accept temporal containers as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: analogy, conclusion, objection, rebuttal.
2
Identify the analogy
The author describes a vessel that holds a specific amount of bodies. This fixed capacity must be something real, implying real dimensions that serve as places for bodies.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent claims the capacity is merely the potential for bodies to exist in themselves, not a separate container.
They try to avoid admitting real dimensions.
4
Grasp the rebuttal
The author uses parity of reasoning: if the opponent's view about time is that it is just potential motion, then the same applies to space — but they would find that absurd. Consistency demands accepting real containers for both.
This is the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها : أن نقول : إن بين طرفي الطاس ، إمكاناً يتسع لمقدار معين من الأجسام ولا يمتلء بأقل من ذلك القدر، ولا يتسع للأزيد منه ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك الإمكان الذي يتسع لذلك القدر من الجسم أمراً موجوداً . وذلك يوجب عليكم الاعتراف بوجود أبعاد قائمة بذواتها تكون هي أمكنة لهذه الأجسام . فإن قالوا : ذلك الإمكان عبارة عن إمكان وجود تلك الأجسام في أنفسها ، لا عن إمكان وجود شيء يكون ذلك الشيء ظرفاً لتلك الأجسام ووعاء لها . فنقول : فلم لا يجوز أن يكون الحال فيما ذكرتم ، كالحال ههنا ؟ وهو أن يقال : الإمكان الذي ذكرتم إشارة إلى إمكان وجود تلك الحركات المختلفة في أنفسها ، فأما إثبات أمر آخر ظرفاً لتلك الحركات ووعاء لها . فهذا ممنوع .
And the third of them: That we say: Indeed between the two ends of the vessel there is a possibility (imkān) that accommodates a specific amount of bodies and is not filled by less than that amount, nor does it accommodate more than it. So it becomes necessary that this possibility which accommodates that amount of body be an existent thing. And that obliges you to acknowledge the existence of dimensions subsisting by themselves which would be places (amkina) for these bodies. If they say: That possibility is an expression of the possibility of existence of those bodies in themselves, not of the possibility of existence of something that would be a container (ẓarf) and vessel (wi‘ā’) for those bodies. Then we say: Why may it not be that the situation concerning what you mentioned is like the situation here? That is, to say: The possibility you mentioned refers to the possibility of existence of those various motions in themselves. As for establishing something else as a container and vessel for those motions, that is rejected.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is the third refutation. The author uses a spatial analogy: between the two ends of a vessel, there is a capacity (imkān) that can contain a specific quantity of bodies—no more, no less. This capacity must be something real (mawjūd), implying the existence of dimensions (ab‘ād) that are places (amkina) for bodies. The opponent objects that this capacity is merely the potential existence of the bodies themselves, not a separate container. The author retorts that this is exactly analogous to their earlier claim about time: if they reject a separate container for time, they should also reject a separate spatial container here, but they find that absurd. Thus, consistency demands accepting real containers for both space and time.

Key Terms
إمكان
الطاس
الأجسام
الأبعاد
الأمكنة
الظرف
الوعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The capacity between two ends is merely a logical possibility, not a real thing.
Correction: The author argues that this capacity has a specific measure and cannot be more or less, indicating it is something existent, not just a possibility.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the author draw from the capacity between the two ends of a vessel?
A) That the vessel is empty.
B) That the capacity is an existent thing requiring dimensions.
C) That the capacity is merely a logical possibility.
D) That time does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The vessel analogy shows that denying a real container for time leads to inconsistency with accepted spatial containers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Six Proofs That Priority Is an Additional Attribute
The author presents six proofs that priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are additional attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are not identical to the essence but are additional attributes.

Core Idea
Priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are additional attributes, not the essence.

In this passage, the author sets out to prove that temporal relationships like priority are not simply the existence of the prior thing, but are extra attributes. He begins by stating the assumption that priority is either identical to the existence of the father and non-existence of the son, or an additional matter. Then he provides six compelling arguments that conclusively show priority must be an additional attribute, concluding the same for posteriority and simultaneity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the question
Read the author's framing: Is priority identical to the prior's existence and the posterior's non-existence, or something extra?
The author uses the example of a father and son.
2
Follow the six proofs
Work through each proof one by one. Notice that each proof approaches the issue from a different angle: conceivability, relation, temporal change, quiddity sharing, relative predication, and divine immutability.
3
Notice the conclusion
After the six proofs, the author concludes that priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are matters different from the essence itself.
This conclusion applies to all three temporal relations.

واعلم أن القوم ما لخصوا هذه الطريقة تلخيصاً شافياً مرتباً ، وأنا أقررها على الوجه الصحيح المعلوم ، فأقول : لا شك أن الأب موجود قبل وجود الابن ، فهذه القبلية إما أن تكون نفس وجود الأب وعدم الابن، أو أمراً زائداً على ذلك . والأول باطل لوجوه :

وثانيها : إن هذه القبلية والبعدية : نسبة مخصوصة بين هاتين الذاتين . والنسبة بين الشيئين مغايرة لهما .

ورابعها : إن الأشياء المختلفة في الماهية قد تكون متشاركة في كون كل واحد منها قبل أشياء ، فإن والد الفرس قبل ولده ووالد الحمار قبل ولده ، ووالد الإنسان قبل ولده . فالفرس والحمار والإنسان ماهيات مختلفة ، وهي مع اختلافها متشاركة في كون كل واحد منها قبل ولده . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : اختلافها في الماهية ، مع اشتراكها في المفهوم من القبلية ، يدل على أن كونها قبلاً وبعداً أمور مغايرة لتلك الماهيات المخصوصة . وأيضاً : فالأشياء المتشاركة في تمام الماهية قد تكون مختلفة في القبلية والبعدية ، وذلك مثل الأب والابن ، فإنهما مع اشتراكهما في تمام الماهية قد اختلفا بحسب القبلية والبعدية . والتقريب معلوم .
وخامسها : أن القبل من حيث هو قبل ، مضاف إلى البعد من حيث هو بعد ، والشيء المحكوم عليه بهذه القبلية جوهر قائم بنفسه غير معقول الماهية بالقياس إلى غيره ، والشيء الذي يكون محكوماً عليه بأنه مقول بالماهية بالقياس إلى غيره ، مغاير للشيء الذي يكون محكوماً عليه بأنه غير مقول الماهية بالقياس إلى غيره ، وذلك يقتضي أن تكون القبلية والبعدية أموراً مغايرة لتلك الذوات .
وسادسها : أن الإله تعالى كان موجوداً قبل هذا الحادث اليومي ، ويكون أيضاً موجوداً عند دخول هذا الحادث اليومي في الوجود ، وسيبقى أيضاً بعد انقضائه وفنائه. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : ذات واجب الوجود لذاته قد تواردت عليها هذه القبليات والبعديات والمعيات. فلو كانت هذه المفهومات نفس الذات ، لزم وقوع التبدل في ذاته . وذلك محال .

Know that the people did not summarize this method in a sufficient, ordered summary, and I present it in the correct, known way. I say: There is no doubt that the father exists before the existence of the son. This priority (qabliyya) is either identical to the existence of the father and the non-existence of the son, or an additional matter (amr zā'id) beyond that. The first is false for several reasons:
First: It is correct to conceive the essence (dhāt) of each one of them while being heedless of this priority, this posteriority (ba'diyya), and this simultaneity (ma'iyya). The known is different from what is not known.
Second: This priority and posteriority is a specific relation (nisba) between these two essences. The relation between two things is different from them.
Third: That which is judged to be prior existed before that which is judged to be posterior, and the prior insofar as it is prior does not remain after the posterior insofar as it is posterior.
Fourth: Things that differ in quiddity (māhiyya) may share in that each one of them is prior to some things. For example, the father of a horse is prior to his offspring, the father of a donkey is prior to his offspring, and the father of a human is prior to his offspring. The horse, donkey, and human are different quiddities, yet they share in being prior to their offspring. If this is established, we say: Their difference in quiddity with their sharing in the concept of priority indicates that their being prior and posterior are matters different from those specific quiddities. Also, things that share in the complete quiddity may differ in priority and posteriority, such as the father and the son: although they share in complete quiddity, they differ with respect to priority and posteriority. The approach is clear.
Fifth: The prior insofar as it is prior is related (muḍāf) to the posterior insofar as it is posterior. The thing judged to have this priority is a substance (jawhar) subsisting by itself, whose quiddity is not intelligible in relation to another. That which is judged to be predicable of quiddity in relation to another is different from that which is judged not to be predicable of quiddity in relation to another. This necessitates that priority and posteriority are matters different from those essences.
Sixth: God Most High existed before this today-occurring event, and He also exists when this event comes into existence, and He will remain after its expiration and annihilation. If this is established, we say: The essence of the Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd) in itself has been subject to these priorities, posteriorities, and simultaneities. If these concepts were identical to the essence, change (tabaddul) would occur in His essence, which is impossible.
Thus it is established by these six proofs that the concept of priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are matters different from the essence and reality itself.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof demonstrating that priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are additional attributes (amr zā'id) not identical to the essence (dhāt). The author begins by defining the problem: the father's priority to the son is either identical to the father's existence and the son's non-existence, or it is an additional matter. He then rejects the first option through six arguments:
1) We can conceive each essence while ignoring its priority/posteriority, showing the concept is additional.
2) Priority is a relation between two things, and a relation is distinct from the relata.
3) The prior exists before the posterior, and the prior qua prior does not remain after the posterior qua posterior, indicating distinction.
4) Things with different quiddities share in the concept of priority, and things with the same quiddity differ in priority, showing priority is extra.
5) Priority is relative and involves predication in relation to another, which is different from the essence itself.
6) God has priority, simultaneity, and posteriority relative to events; if these were His essence, He would change, which is impossible. The conclusion: priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are distinct from the essence.

Key Terms
قبلية (qabliyya)
بعدية (ba'diyya)
معية (ma'iyya)
ذات (dhāt)
أمر زائد (amr zā'id)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is identical with the existence of the prior thing and the non-existence of the posterior thing.
Correction: Priority is an additional attribute distinct from both the prior's existence and the posterior's non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what would happen if priority were identical to God's essence?
A) God would be dependent on creation.
B) Change would occur in God's essence.
C) Priority would be impossible.
D) God would be prior to all things.

Key Takeaway
Priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are additional attributes distinct from the essence, as proven by six logical arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The proof from God's attributes shows that change in essence is impossible for Him. Are there ways you have unconsciously imagined God as changing or reacting to your actions? How can you correct that in your worship?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you see priority and posteriority as mere mental labels or as real attributes that shape your relationships? How does this lesson challenge you to see time and sequence as part of Allah's creation, not part of Him?

Priority and Posteriority as Real Attributes
The author argues that priority and posteriority are not non-existence but are real existing attributes, both mental and external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority and posteriority are considered real extra-mental attributes, not merely mental constructs.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are not just mental fictions; they exist in reality.

In this passage, the author continues his argument about relational attributes. He first shows that priority and posteriority cannot be pure non-existence; they must be existing matters. Then he distinguishes between purely mental concepts (like 'five is even') and real relations (like a father's priority to his son). The conclusion is that these attributes exist both mentally and externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text ends mid-sentence; the argument is complete up to a point.
2
Identify the two stages of the argument
The argument has two main stages: first proving that priority/posteriority are not pure non-existence, and second proving they are not merely mental concepts. Look for the conditional 'either...or' structures.
3
Understand the analogy of 'five is even'
The author compares a purely mental concept like 'five is even' to the father's priority. The key difference is that 'five is even' has no external reality, while a father's priority is a real relation. Ask yourself why this analogy works.
The author uses this analogy to reject the idea that priority is only mental.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are concepts additional to essences and exist in external reality just as they exist in minds. This is the main teaching point.
The passage ends with a new conditional question, but the core conclusion is already established.
