وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المفهوم الزائد إما أن يكون عدماً محضاً ، وإما أن يكون أمراً موجوداً . والأول باطل لان كونه قبل غيره نقيض لقولنا : إنه ليس قبله ، والمفهوم من عدم القبلية عدم محض ، ونقيض العدم ثبوت. فظهر أن القبلية والبعدية : أمران زائدان على الذات ، وهما وصفان ثابتان موجودان. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إما أن تكون هذه المعاني من الأمور الثابتة التي لا حصول لها إلا في الذهن ، وإما أن يقال : إنها موجودة في الأعيان . والأول باطل ، لأن الذي لا ثبوت له إلا في الذهن يكون جارياً مجرى قولنا الخمسة زوج، فإن هذا المعنى وإن كان يوجد في الفرض الذهني ، والاعتبار الفكري ، إلا أن العقل جازم بأنه لا وجود له في الخارج البتة ، لكن كون الأب قبل الابن ، ليس من هذا الباب . فثبت : أن هذه القبلية والمعية والبعدية مفهومات زائدة على الذوات ، ومعان موجودة في الأعيان ، كما أنها موجودة في الأذهان. ثم نقول : هذه القبليات والبعديات إما أن تكون من الأمور التي توجد مستقلة بأنفسها ، قائمة بذواتها ، غنية عن
And if this is established, we say: This additional concept is either pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ) or an existing matter (amr mawjūd). The first is false because its being prior to another is the contradictory (naqīḍ) of our statement that it is not prior to it, and the concept of absence of priority is pure non-existence, and the contradictory of non-existence is existence (thubūt). Thus it appears that priority and posteriority are two additional matters to the essence, and they are two established, existing attributes. And if this is established, we say: Either these meanings are among the established things that have no occurrence except in the mind (dhihn), or it is said that they exist in external reality (aʿyān). The first is false, because that which has no establishment except in the mind is like our saying 'five is even'—this meaning, although it exists in mental assumption and intellectual consideration, the intellect is certain that it has no existence in the external world at all. But the father being prior to the son is not of this sort. Thus it is established that these priority, simultaneity, and posteriority are concepts additional to the essences, and meanings existing in external reality, just as they exist in minds. Then we say: These priorities and posteriorities are either among matters that exist independently by themselves, subsisting by their essences, independent of...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument proving that priority and posteriority are real attributes. After establishing that they are additional to the essence, the author considers two possibilities: they are either pure non-existence or existing matters. He rejects non-existence because being prior to something is the contradictory of not being prior; the contradictory of non-existence is existence, so they must exist. Next, he considers whether they are merely mental or also external. He rejects the mental-only option by analogy: a mental concept like 'five is even' has no external reality, but the father's priority to the son is not like that—it is a real relation. Thus priority, posteriority, and simultaneity are both mental and external realities. The passage concludes by introducing a further question (unfinished in this window) about whether these attributes exist independently or inhere in subjects.

Correction: The father's priority to his son is a real external relation, not a mere mental construct. The analogy of 'five is even' fails because that concept has no external reality, whereas priority does.

The Objection from Possibility: Challenging the Existence of Priorness
The objector presents the first question of his objection, using possibility (imkan) as a counterexample to the claim that priorness is existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's first argument against the existence of priorness as a real attribute, using the concept of possibility (imkan) as a counterexample.

Core Idea
Possibility (imkan) removes both impossibility and necessity, creating a logical problem for the priorness argument.

In this passage, the objector directly challenges the claim that priorness is an existent attribute. He does not merely assert a different view; he constructs a logical counterexample using the concept of possibility (imkan). By showing that the same reasoning applied to priorness would lead to a contradiction when applied to possibility, he attempts to undermine the original argument. This moves the discussion from simple denial to a sophisticated dialectical engagement.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how the objector structures his first question: he denies the claim and then presents a counterexample.
The passage is incomplete; focus on the logic presented.
2
Identify the original argument being objected to
Recall that the original argument claimed priorness is existent because it is the contradictory of non-priorness (non-existence), and the remover of non-existence must be existence.
The objector summarizes this argument in his opening words.
3
Analyze the counterexample: possibility (imkan)
The objector says that possibility (imkan) also removes both impossibility (non-existence) and necessity (existence). According to the same logic, possibility would have to be both existence and non-existence simultaneously, which is contradictory. Therefore, the reasoning is flawed.
This shows that the original argument is not valid universally.
4
Reflect on the incomplete nature of the passage
Note that the passage cuts off before the objection is fully developed. The answer to this objection is not present here. Consider what a response might need to address.

والاعتراض عليه :
السؤال الأول (۳) ] لا نسلم أن القبلية والبعدية من المعاني الموجودة أما قوله : ( القبلية نقيض اللاقبلية ، ثم إن اللاقبلية عدم ، ورافع العدم ثبوت . . قلنا : يشكل هذا بالإمكان فإنه رافع للامتناع ورافع للوجوب . والامتناع عدم ، والوجوب ثبوت. فيلزم في الإمكان أن يقال : إنه لكونه رافعاً للامتناع الذي هو عدم أن يكون (4) وجوداً ، ولكونه رافعاً للوجوب الذي هو
And the objection against it:
The first question: We do not concede that priorness and posteriority are among the existent meanings. As for his statement: “Priorness is the contradictory of non-priorness, and non-priorness is non-existence, and the remover of non-existence is existence.” We say: this is problematic because of possibility (imkan), for it is a remover of impossibility and a remover of necessity. Impossibility is non-existence, and necessity is existence. So it would follow concerning possibility that it is, because it is a remover of impossibility (which is non-existence), an existence, and because it is a remover of necessity (which is existence), ...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents the beginning of an objection to the earlier argument that priorness and posteriority are existent attributes. The objector's first question disputes this, using the concept of possibility (imkan) as a counterexample. The argument given (by the objector) is that priorness is claimed to be existent because it is the contradictory of non-priorness (which is non-existence), and the contradictory of non-existence must be existence. The objector responds that this reasoning is problematic because possibility (imkan) also removes both impossibility (which is non-existence) and necessity (which is existence). If we apply the same logic, possibility would have to be both existence and non-existence, or something contradictory. The text cuts off before the objection is fully developed or answered. This is an incomplete objection.

Establishing Time as the Substrate of Priority and Posteriority
Author proves time exists as a flowing substrate for priority and posteriority, distinct from motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves the existence of time as a distinct substrate for priority and posteriority, separate from motion.

Core Idea
Time is the unique, irreversible bearer of priority and posteriority.

In this passage, the author presents a rational proof for the existence of time as a real entity underlying temporal relations. He first eliminates the possibility that priority and posteriority are self-subsistent, then argues they must inhere in a subject, and finally distinguishes that subject from motion using the irreversibility of temporal parts. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. This will give you the raw argument before interpretation.
The passage is dense; pause after each sentence to grasp the flow.
2
Understand the initial division
Notice that the author divides possibilities: either priority/posteriority are self-subsistent or they need a subject. He eliminates self-subsistence because they are relations.
Relations cannot stand alone—they require relata.
3
Examine the father example
The author uses the example of a father and son to show that ordinary objects do not bear priority/posteriority by virtue of their substance or accidental properties, but by a specific relation. This points to a need for a special subject.
This example clarifies that the substrate is not just any object.
4
Distinguish time from motion
The key argument: parts of motion can be thought of in reverse order, but parts of time cannot. This proves that time, not motion, is the irreversible bearer of priority and posteriority.
Think about a video played backwards vs. time itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعروض والموصوف، وإما أن لا تكون كذلك، بل تكون محتاجة إلى المعروض وإلى الموصوف والأول باطل بالبديهة لأن القبلية والبعدية من باب النسب والإضافات، وما كان كذلك امتنع كونها جواهر قائمة بأنفسها، مستقلة بذواتها. فبقي القسم الثاني وهو أنه لا بد من أشياء تكون هذه القبليات والبعديات معارضة لها من حيث إنها هي هي. ثم نقول: إن كل شيء فإنه لا يقبل هذا النوع من النسب والإضافات من حيث هو هو، فإن الأب من حيث هو جوهر جسماني، ليس قبل الابن، ولا من حيث إنه طويل وأبيض وعالم وزاهد. فإذن لا بد من الجزم بوجود أمر تلحقه هذه القبليات والبعديات، لحوقاً بالذات. وذلك الشيء ليس هو الحركة للوجوه الكثيرة التي ذكرناها في الفصل المتقدم. ونزيد ههنا كلاماً آخر فنقول: إن الجزء المتقدم من الحركة يمكن تعقله متأخراً وبالعكس. وأما الجزء المتقدم من الزمان فإنه لا يمكن تعقله متأخراً وبالعكس. فثبت: أن هذا الشيء الذي هو المعروض بالذات لهذه القبليات والبعديات موجود، سيال منقضي لذاته، وهو شيء مغاير للحركة. ولا نعني بالزمان إلا ذلك.
The subject and described, and either they are not like that, but rather need the subject and the described, and the first is invalid by intuition because priority and posteriority belong to the category of relations and additions, and whatever is like that is impossible for them to be substances subsisting by themselves, independent in their essences. So the second division remains, which is that there must be things to which these priorities and posteriorities are attributed insofar as they are what they are. Then we say: Every thing does not accept this type of relations and additions insofar as it is what it is, for the father insofar as he is a corporeal substance is not prior to the son, nor insofar as he is tall, white, learned, and ascetic. Therefore, one must be certain of the existence of a thing that these priorities and posteriorities attach to, essentially. And that thing is not motion, for the many reasons we mentioned in the preceding chapter. And we add here another statement and say: The prior part of motion can be conceived as posterior and vice versa. As for the prior part of time, it cannot be conceived as posterior and vice versa. So it is established that this thing which is the essential subject of these priorities and posteriorities exists, flowing and passing away by its essence, and it is something other than motion. And we mean nothing other than that by time.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of time as a distinct substrate for priority and posteriority. The author begins by eliminating the possibility that priority and posteriority are self-subsistent substances (since they are relational), concluding they must inhere in a subject. Then he argues that not just any subject can bear these relations—for example, a father is not prior to his son by virtue of being a body or having accidental properties, but by a specific relation. Therefore, there must be a real thing that essentially underlies priority and posteriority. This thing is not motion, because parts of motion can be thought of in reverse order (imagine the sequence reversed), whereas temporal parts cannot. Hence, time is that flowing, self-annihilating thing that is the bearer of priority and posteriority.

Key Terms
قبلية (priority)
بعدية (posteriority)
زمان (time)
حركة (motion)
معروض (substrate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is identical to motion.
Correction: The author proves they are distinct because parts of motion are reversible in conception, but parts of time are not.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the prior part of time from the prior part of motion according to the author?
A) The prior part of time can be conceived as posterior, unlike motion.
B) The prior part of motion can be conceived as posterior, unlike time.
C) Both are reversible in conception.
D) Neither is reversible in conception.

Key Takeaway
Time is a real, flowing thing that is the essential bearer of priority and posteriority, distinct from motion due to the irreversibility of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You cannot reverse a single moment of your life. The prior part of time cannot be conceived as posterior. How does this absolute irreversibility reshape what you prioritize today, given that you are not the master of time but its subject?
?
The author argues that you are not 'prior' to your children by being tall or learned. What false identities do you cling to that make you forget that your priority before God is purely relational—rooted in your transient position in time, not your attributes?
?
If time is 'flowing and passing away by its essence', what part of your daily routine is anchored to something that does not pass away? What would it cost you to build one such anchor today?

Objection via Possibility: Priority as Existing Attribute
Objection to priority as existing, using possibility reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector uses the concept of possibility (imkan) to challenge the claim that priority is an existing attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility cannot be both existence and non-existence, exposing a flaw in the opponent's logic.

In this passage, the author presents an objection against those who claim that priority and posteriority are real existing attributes. The objector denies this and argues using a parallel with possibility (imkan). By showing that the opponent's reasoning would force possibility to be both existence and non-existence, he exposes a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains only the objection, not the full rebuttal.
2
Identify the objector's main claim and the opponent's argument
The objector first denies that priority and posteriority are existing meanings. Then he paraphrases an opponent's argument: priority is the opposite of non-priority, non-priority is non-existence, and the remover of non-existence is existence, so priority would be existence. Find these elements in the text.
3
Analyze the parallel with possibility
The objector counters by drawing a parallel with possibility (imkan): possibility removes both impossibility (non-existence) and necessity (existence). If we apply the same reasoning, possibility would have to be both existence and non-existence, which is impossible. Understand this logical move.
The objector uses a reductio ad absurdum: the opponent's reasoning leads to a contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this objection matters. If the opponent's reasoning is flawed, then priority cannot be proven to be an existing attribute through that argument. The objector's point is valid regardless of whether priority itself exists; it shows a problem with the proof.
In the larger context, the author maintains that priority and posteriority are real relations, but this objection is a challenge that must be addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه: السؤال الأول: لا نسلم أن القبلية والبعدية من المعاني الموجودة. أما قوله: (القبلية نقيض اللاقبلية، ثم إن اللاقبلية عدم، ورافع العدم ثبوت) قلنا: يشكل هذا بالإمكان فإنه رافع للامتناع ورافع للوجوب. والامتناع عدم، والوجوب ثبوت. فيلزم في الإمكان أن يقال: إنه لكونه رافعاً للامتناع الذي هو عدم أن يكون وجوداً، ولكونه رافعاً للوجوب الذي هو وجود، أن يكون عدماً، فيلزم في الشيء الواحد أن يكون عدماً ووجوداً، وهو محال.
And the objection against it: The first question: We do not grant that priority and posteriority are among existing meanings. As for his statement: (Priority is the opposite of non-priority, then non-priority is non-existence, and the remover of non-existence is existence) we say: This is problematic because of possibility, for it is the remover of impossibility and the remover of necessity. And impossibility is non-existence, and necessity is existence. So it follows concerning possibility that it be said: Because it is the remover of impossibility which is non-existence, it is existence, and because it is the remover of necessity which is existence, it is non-existence, so it follows that one thing is both non-existence and existence, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector first denies that priority and posteriority are existing realities. He then paraphrases an opponent's argument: priority is the opposite of non-priority, non-priority is non-existence, and the remover of non-existence is existence, so priority would be existence. The objector counters by drawing a parallel with possibility (al-imkan): possibility removes both impossibility (non-existence) and necessity (existence), so by the same logic possibility would have to be both existence and non-existence, which is absurd. The objection aims to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to contradiction. The response is implicit in the structure: the objector's parallel is not exact, as priority is not analogous to possibility in both removing opposites. However, the text as given only contains the objection, not a full rebuttal, but the context of the larger argument suggests the author rejects this objection.

Key Terms
قبلية (priority)
بعدية (posteriority)
إمكان (possibility)
عدم (non-existence)
ثبوت (existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection refutes the existence of priority itself.
Correction: The objection only challenges the opponent's argument for priority, not necessarily priority's existence. In the larger framework, priority and posteriority are still considered real relations that inhere in time.

Checkpoint
What does the objector use to challenge the claim that priority is an existing attribute?
A) The concept of motion
B) The concept of possibility (imkan)
C) The concept of time
D) The concept of substance

Key Takeaway
The objector's reductio ad absurdum uses possibility to show that the opponent's reasoning leads to a contradiction, thereby undermining the claim that priority is an existing attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that, when followed to their logical conclusions, contradict each other? Reflect on a time when you had to abandon a comfortable argument because it led to an absurdity. What did that reveal about your reliance on truth versus convenience?
?
This lesson uses a reductio to expose a flaw. In your own understanding of Allah's attributes, are there any positions you hold that, when scrutinized, lead to an impossible conclusion? Are you willing to follow the argument wherever it leads, even if it unsettles you?
?
The objector here denies that priority is an existing attribute. But the larger authorial framework affirms it. Are you sometimes too quick to accept an objection without seeing the larger picture? How can you train yourself to seek the complete context before judging a theological claim?

First Proof: Priority Cannot Be an Existing Attribute
First proof that priority/posteriority are not existing attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that priority (precedence) is not an existing attribute but a relation to non-existence.

Core Idea
Priority describes non-existence, not existence; no existing attribute can inhere in nothing.

This lesson presents the first of three proofs that priority and posteriority are not real, existing attributes. Sanusi begins with a self-evident rational judgment: every originated thing is preceded by its non-existence. From this, he shows that priority must be an attribute of non-existence, and therefore cannot be an existing attribute. Prepare to follow a concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key premise
Find the self-evident judgment: every originated thing (muhdath) is preceded by its non-existence (adam).
This premise is asserted as clear rational judgment.
3
Follow the inference
From the premise, the author concludes that priority (al-qabliyya) is an attribute of non-existence, not of the thing itself.
4
Understand the impossibility
An existing attribute cannot inhere in pure non-existence. Therefore priority cannot be an existing attribute.
This is the core logical move.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The lesson establishes that priority and posteriority are not among existing attributes. Consider why this matters for the broader discussion of divine attributes.
This proof is part of a larger argument about the nature of time and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: الذي يدل على أن القبلية والبعدية لا يمكن أن يكونا من الأمور الموجودة: وجوه: الأول: إن صريح العقل يحكم بأن كل محدث، فإنه مسبوق بعدمه، وأن عدمه سابق على وجوده. وهذا يقتضي كون القبلية والسبق والتقدم وصفاً للعدم، وما كان وصفاً للعدم، امتنع أن يكون صفة موجودة، لامتناع قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم المحض [والنفي الصرف]. فثبت: أن القبلية والبعدية [ليست] من الصفات الموجودة.
Then we say: That which indicates that priority and posteriority cannot be among existing things are aspects: The first: Clear reason judges that every originated thing is preceded by its non-existence, and that its non-existence is prior to its existence. And this necessitates that priority, precedence, and antecedence are attributes of non-existence, and whatever is an attribute of non-existence is impossible to be an existing attribute, because it is impossible for an existing attribute to inhere in pure non-existence [and pure negation]. So it is established that priority and posteriority are not among existing attributes.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is the first of three proofs demonstrating that priority and posteriority are not real, existing attributes. The proof begins with a self-evident principle: every originated thing (muhdath) is preceded by its own non-existence. That non-existence is prior to its existence. Therefore, priority (qabliyya) is actually a description of non-existence, not of something existent. Since an existing attribute cannot inhere in pure non-existence, priority cannot be an existing attribute. The same applies to posteriority (ba'diyya). The author concludes that priority and posteriority are not among the existing attributes.

Key Terms
محدث (originated)
عدم (non-existence)
سبق (precedence)
صفة موجودة (existing attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority is a real, inherent property of the originated thing itself.
Correction: The proof shows priority is actually a relation to prior non-existence, not an existing attribute inhering in the thing.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a substance
B) Because it inheres in non-existence
C) Because it is a relation
D) Because it is eternal

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority are not real attributes; they are relations to non-existence and cannot be existing attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Proof: Priority Leads to Infinite Regress
Second proof that priority cannot be an existing attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority cannot be an existing attribute because it would necessitate an infinite regress of priority relations.

Core Idea
If priority is a real attribute, it needs another priority—an infinite chain.

The author presents the second proof against priority being an existing attribute. It argues that priority itself, if real, would require another priority relative to something else, leading to an infinite regress. An objection that priority's relation might be identical to its essence is refuted through logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional proof
Read the opening conditional statement: if priority were an existing attribute, then relative to something else it would be prior, simultaneous, or posterior. Grasp how this sets up the regress.
Notice the three alternatives—each leads to another relation.
2
Examine the objection
Read the objection that priority's relational property might be identical to its essence, not an additional attribute. Consider why this could seem plausible.
The objection tries to stop the regress by collapsing the relation into the essence.
3
Analyze the refutation
The refutation uses subject-predicate logic: when we say 'priority is prior to something', the predicate is distinct from the subject. Thus the relation cannot be identical to the essence.
This shows the relation is always additional, so the regress is unavoidable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو كانت القبلية صفة موجودة، لكانت تلك الصفة الموجودة بالنسبة إلى غيرها، إما أن تكون قبلاً أو معاً أو بعداً، ويلزم التسلسل. لا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن تكون القبلية بالنسبة إلى غيرها، وإن كانت قبلاً، إلا أن كونها كذلك عين ذاته. لا أنه صفة زائدة على الذات؟ لأنا نقول: إذا حكمنا على القبلية بأنها قبل شيء آخر، فههنا ذات القبلية هي الموضوع. ثم حكمنا عليها بأنها قبل شيء آخر هو [المحمول]، ومحمول القضية غير موضوعها لا محالة. فثبت: أن القبلية لو كانت صفة موجودة لكان كونها قبلاً بالنسبة إلى غيرها، يمتنع أن يكون عين ذاتها، بل لا بد وأن يكون أمراً [مغايراً] لذاتها.
The second: If priority were an existing attribute, then that existing attribute would be, in relation to something else, either prior, simultaneous, or posterior, and an infinite regress would follow. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible that priority is, in relation to something else, although it is prior, except that its being so is identical to its essence, not an additional attribute on the essence? For we say: When we judge that priority is prior to something else, here the essence of priority is the subject. Then we judge that it is prior to something else which is the predicate, and the predicate of the proposition is necessarily other than its subject. So it is established that if priority were an existing attribute, then its being prior in relation to something else would be impossible for it to be identical to its essence, rather it must be something other than its essence.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. It argues that if priority were an existing attribute (say, a real property), then relative to something else it would itself be either prior, simultaneous, or posterior. In each case, another relation of priority would be needed, leading to infinite regress (tasalsul). A possible objection is raised: perhaps priority's being prior to something is identical to its very essence, not an additional attribute. The author refutes this by analyzing the logical structure: when we say 'priority is prior to something', the essence of priority is the subject, and the predicate ('prior to something') is distinct from the subject. Therefore, the relation is not identical to the essence. Hence, priority would require an infinite chain of relations, which is impossible. Consequently, priority cannot be an existing attribute.

Key Terms
قبلية (priority)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
ذات (essence)
محمول (predicate)
موضوع (subject)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority's relational property could be identical to its essence, so no additional attribute is needed.
Correction: The logical structure of the judgment reveals that the predicate ('prior to something') is distinct from the subject (the essence of priority), so the relation cannot be identical to the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence if priority were an existing attribute, according to the second proof?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would be a substance
C) It would require an infinite regress of priority relations
D) It would be identical to motion

Key Takeaway
Priority cannot be an existing attribute because it would require an infinite regress of priority relations, and the objection that its relation is identical to its essence fails due to the subject-predicate distinction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attributes of Allah, do you ever stop to examine whether each can logically be a real attribute without contradiction? If not, why do you shy away from such scrutiny? Allow this proof to challenge you to investigate at least one attribute this week to see if it withstands logical analysis.
?
The proof shows that assuming priority as a real attribute leads to an endless chain. In your own spiritual life, are there thoughts or actions that you keep repeating without addressing their root? What is the 'first cause' you need to confront instead of getting lost in the regress?
?
The objection tried to collapse the relation into the essence to avoid regress, but the subject-predicate distinction exposed this as impossible. Are there beliefs you hold that you try to 'simplify' by avoiding logical distinctions? Are you willing to accept that some distinctions are necessary for truth, even if they feel uncomfortable?

Third Proof: Priority and Posteriority as Relations
Third proof that priority and posteriority are not existing things because they are relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that priority and posteriority are not existing things because they are relations that require simultaneous existence of their relata, and the logical implications of this requirement.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are relations that must coexist—they cannot exist alone.

This lesson presents the third proof in a series arguing that priority and posteriority are not existing attributes. The author begins by asserting that priority and posteriority are relations (idafatan) and that each is defined relative to the other. A key principle is invoked: two correlatives must exist together in both the mind and external reality. From this, the proof deduces that if priority and posteriority were existing, they would have to coexist, leading to a problematic situation that the text begins to explore.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the key terms إضافة and معاً.
The text is incomplete; it cuts off at 'لحصل معروضاهما'.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage asserts that priority and posteriority are relations. Understand what that means: they do not exist independently but are relative to each other.
3
Grasp the principle of correlatives
The passage states it is established that two correlatives (al-mudafan) exist together (ma'an) both externally and mentally. This is a key principle.
Think of examples: father and son, above and below.
4
Follow the deduction
If priority and posteriority exist, they must exist together (by the principle). But if they exist together, their substrates (ma'rudahuma) would be obtained—leading to a contradiction or impossibility that the full proof would conclude.
The text breaks off, but the direction is that their coexistence cannot be sustained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن القبلية والبعدية إضافتان. وكل واحدة منهما مقولة بالقياس إلى الأخرى وقد ثبت: أن المضافين يوجدان معاً في الأعيان وفي الأذهان. فلو كانا موجودين، لوجب أن معاً يوجدا، ولو وجدا معاً، لحصل معروضاهما
The third: Priority and posteriority are two relations. And each one of them is predicated in relation to the other, and it has been established that two correlatives exist together in external things and in minds. So if they both existed, they would necessarily exist together, and if they existed together, their substrates would be obtained.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof. It states that priority and posteriority are relational (idafatan). Each is defined relative to the other. It is a known principle that two correlatives (al-mudafan) exist simultaneously, both in external reality and in the mind. Therefore, if priority and posteriority were existing attributes, they would have to exist together. And if they exist together, then their respective substrates (ma'rudahuma) would be simultaneously present. The text breaks off, but the intended conclusion is presumably that this leads to a contradiction because priority and posteriority cannot inhere in the same subject at the same time, or that their substrates would need to be distinct yet simultaneous, which may be impossible for time. The proof is incomplete, but the logical direction is clear.

Key Terms
إضافة (relation)
مضافان (correlatives)
معاً (together)
معروض (substrate)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority can exist independently as absolute attributes.
Correction: Priority and posteriority are relational—they only make sense in relation to each other. The third proof shows that if they were existing things, they would have to coexist, which leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is a necessary condition for two correlatives (such as priority and posteriority) to exist?
A) They must exist at different times
B) They must exist together
C) They must be substances
D) They must be eternal

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority, as relations, cannot exist independently; they require simultaneous existence of both relata.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of being 'first' or ahead of others. Did you see that priority as something real inside you? How does this proof—that priority is just a relation, not a real attribute—challenge the foundation of that pride? What would change if you genuinely accepted that your 'firstness' depends entirely on the existence of something 'second'?
?
You constantly compare yourself to others—your past to their present, your achievements to theirs. This proof says priority and posteriority are not real things; they only exist as relations. If you truly believed that, would you still compare? Would you still feel inferior or superior? What would it take to stop measuring your worth by such relations?
?
When you make plans, you think of some actions as 'before' and others as 'after'. But this proof suggests these labels are not inherent. How does that affect your sense of control over time? If priority and posteriority are mere relations, not fixed realities, do you ever cling to 'being early' or fear 'being late'? Can you see those as illusions? What would it mean to live free from the tyranny of sequence?

The Fourth Argument: Priority and Posteriority Cannot Be Real Attributes
Sanusi argues that priority and posteriority cannot be real attributes due to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why priority and posteriority cannot be real attributes, based on the dilemma that mutuality either leads to infinite regress or is not an additional attribute.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are relative notions, not real attributes—they would cause infinite regress.

In this fourth argument, Sanusi tackles the question of whether relational concepts like 'priority' and 'posteriority' can be real, objective attributes existing in external entities. Using a clever logical move, he shows that if they were real, they would either lead to an infinite regress or become indistinguishable from the essences themselves. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional dilemma followed by an objection and refutation.
The passage is compact; take your time.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The argument first assumes two relatives exist together. Then it considers whether the mutuality between them is an additional attribute or not. Trace each branch and its consequence.
If additional → infinite regress. If not additional → why not for all relative attributes?
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
The objection says mutuality is just the essences themselves. Sanusi refutes this by noting that in the proposition 'the two relatives exist together,' the predicate is distinct from the subject.
This refutation relies on the principle that predication requires distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن نقول : لما ثبت أن المضافين يوجدان معاً ، فهذه المعية إن كانت وصفاً زائداً عليهما ، كان ذلك الزائد أيضاً معهما ، فتكون هذه المعية الثانية زائدة ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن لم يكن وصفاً زائداً ، فلم لا يجوز مثله في سائر المعيات والقبليات ؟ لا يقال : إن كون المضافين معاً ، نفس ذاتيهما . لأنـا نقول : إذا قلنا : المضافان يوجدان معاً ، فهو قضية موضوعها : قولنا المضافان . ومحمولها قولنا يوجدان معاً. والمحمول [ في كل قضية ] غير الموضوع [ لامتناع حمل الشيء الواحد بالاعتبار الواحد على نفسه ] . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يبطل هذا العذر . فثبت بهذه الوجه : أن القبلية والبعدية لا يمكن أن يكونا من الصفات الموجودة في الأعيان
Fourth: We say: Since it is established that the two relatives exist together, then this mutuality, if it is an additional attribute to them, then that additional would also be with them, so this second mutuality would be additional, and an infinite regress follows. And if it is not an additional attribute, then why is the like of it not permissible in all other mutualities and priorities? It cannot be said: That the two relatives being together is their very essences. Because we say: When we say 'the two relatives exist together,' it is a proposition whose subject is our saying 'the two relatives,' and its predicate is our saying 'exist together.' And the predicate [in every proposition] is other than the subject [because it is impossible to predicate the same thing in the same respect of itself]. On this estimation, this excuse is invalidated. So by this aspect it is established that priority and posteriority cannot be among the attributes existing in external entities.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the reality of priority and posteriority as real attributes. The author begins by noting that if two relative entities (al-mudafayn) exist together, then the mutuality (al-mu'iyya) between them must be either an additional attribute or not. If it is an additional attribute, then that attribute itself must also exist with them, leading to a second mutuality, and so on ad infinitum. If it is not an additional attribute, then why would it be impermissible for other mutualities and priorities to also be non-additional? The author then considers a potential objection: that the mutuality is simply the essences themselves. He refutes this by pointing out that the proposition 'the two relatives exist together' has a subject and predicate that are distinct; the predicate cannot be identical to the subject. Thus the excuse fails. The conclusion is that priority and posteriority cannot be real attributes in external objects.

Key Terms
مضافين (relatives)
معية (mutuality)
قبلية (priority)
بعدية (posteriority)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are real attributes existing in external objects.
Correction: The argument shows that if they were real, they would either lead to infinite regress or not be additional attributes at all, so they cannot be real.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what would happen if the mutuality (المعية) were an additional attribute?
A) It would be identical to the essence
B) It would lead to an infinite regress
C) It would be impossible
D) It would be a separate substance

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority cannot be real attributes because they would either cause an infinite regress or collapse into the essences of the relatives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that even a simple relationship like 'togetherness' cannot be a real attribute without leading to absurdity. Have you ever accepted a theological or philosophical belief without testing its logical consequences? How will you commit to examining your beliefs more rigorously starting today?
?
The argument hinges on the principle that the predicate in a proposition must be distinct from the subject. When you say 'Allah is merciful' or 'Allah is knowing,' do you unconsciously assume that these attributes are exactly like human attributes? What will you do to guard against comparing Allah to His creation in your own mind?
?
This lesson demolishes the notion that priority and posteriority are real. Yet we often live as if 'being first' or 'being last' truly inheres in things. How does this affect your understanding of your own place in the world? Are you willing to see yourself not as inherently prior or posterior, but simply as contingent before your Creator?

Second Objection: The Argument Implies a Time for Time
The objector presents a reductio argument against the necessity of time for priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the argument for temporal priority leads to an infinite regress of time, and how it challenges the necessity of time for priority.

Core Idea
The objector turns the argument against itself: if priority needs time, then time needs time.

This lesson presents a critical objection to the idea that priority and posteriority necessarily imply time. The objector argues that if we accept that all priority requires time, then the priority of parts of time themselves would require another time, leading to an infinite regress. This challenge forces us to reconsider the relationship between time and priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Pay attention to the logical flow: the objector states the consequence, explains why temporal priority is different from causal priority, and draws the absurd conclusion.
2
Identify the structure of the argument
The objection is a reductio ad absurdum. Break it into parts: (1) claim that the original argument implies a time for time, (2) explanation of the difference between temporal and causal priority, (3) the consequence that time would need another time, (4) the challenge to accept the regress or reject the original argument.
3
Understand the distinction between priorities
The objector distinguishes temporal priority from causal/essential priority. In causal priority, the prior can coexist with the posterior (e.g., a cause and its effect can coexist in time). But temporal parts like yesterday and today cannot coexist.
This distinction is crucial for the objection.
4
Reflect on the force of the objection
Consider how this objection undermines the need to posit time as the basis for all priority. If temporal priority itself needs time, we get an infinite regress. The defender must either deny that temporal priority is a real type of priority or find another way to ground it.
The objection is not answered in this passage; it is left as a challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : إن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرتم يقتضي إثبات زمان للزمان وبيانه : وهو أن بعض أجزاء الزمان سابق على البعض ، وظاهر أن ذلك السبق ليس بالعلية ولا بالذات، لأن السبق الذي يكون بالعلية وبالذات يجوز أن يوجد المتقدم فيه مع المتأخر . والجزء السابق من الزمان يمتنع أن يوجد مع الجزء المتأخر ، بل تقدم الأمس على اليوم كتقدم الجزء السابق من الحركة على الجزء اللاحق منها ، فإن كان هذا النوع من التقدم لا يتقرر إلا مع الزمان ، لزم أن يكون للزمان زمان آخر ، وإلا فجوزوا مثله فيها ذكرتم .
Second question: This argument that you have mentioned necessitates establishing a time for time. Its explanation: That some parts of time are prior to others, and it is apparent that that priority is not by causality nor by essence, because priority that is by causality and by essence allows the prior to exist with the posterior. But the prior part of time cannot exist with the posterior part. Rather, the priority of yesterday over today is like the priority of the prior part of motion over the subsequent part. If this type of priority is not established except with time, then it would necessitate that time has another time. Otherwise, allow the like of it in what you have mentioned.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the argument that priority and posteriority require time. The objector states that if we posit time, then some parts of time are prior to others. This priority is not causal or essential priority, because causal priority allows coexistence of prior and posterior, but temporal parts cannot coexist. The priority of yesterday over today is like the priority of an earlier part of motion over a later part. If this kind of priority can only be established by time, then we would need a time for time, leading to infinite regress. The objector challenges: either accept that this priority does not require time (thus undermining the argument) or allow the same objection against the original argument.

Key Terms
زمان (time)
سبق (priority)
علية (causality)
ذات (essence)
تسلسل (infinite regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of priority are the same and allow coexistence of prior and posterior.
Correction: Temporal priority is distinct: prior and posterior temporal parts cannot coexist, unlike causal or essential priority.

Checkpoint
What does the objector say about the priority of yesterday over today?
A) It is causal priority
B) It requires a time for time
C) It is like the priority of a cause over its effect
D) It allows coexistence

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that if temporal priority itself requires time, we get an infinite regress, challenging the assumption that all priority is grounded in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a certain way of thinking is absolutely necessary, only to find it leads to an endless chain of questions? How did that affect your faith and your trust in revelation?
?
This objection reveals that even our concept of time can be questioned. When you reflect on the limitations of human reason regarding time, does it humble you? How does that drive you to rely more on Allah's timeless knowledge?
?
The objector forces a choice: either accept a regress or reject the premise. In your own life, are there assumptions you cling to that actually lead to confusion or doubt? What would it cost you to let them go and submit to the teachings of the Quran and Sunnah?

Third Proof for the Existence of Time from Intuitive Divisions
Sanusi proves existence of time from intuitive divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that time must be a real existent entity because our intuitive divisions and rational judgments about time cannot apply to pure non-existence.

Core Idea
Time must exist because non-existence cannot be divided into parts or compared.

Sanusi presents a proof for the existence of time that starts with the most basic human experience: we divide time into years, months, days, and hours. This intuitive division, he argues, forces us to accept that time is real, because pure nothingness cannot be divided or have properties like longer and shorter. Let's walk through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the argument's flow from intuitive division to the rejection of non-existence.
The passage is from Sanusi's al-Matalib al-Aliyah.
2
Identify the intuitive premise
Find where Sanusi states that we know by necessity that there is something divisible into years, months, etc. This is the starting point.
He calls it 'the clearest of intuitive sciences'.
3
Understand the dichotomy and rejection of non-existence
Sanusi argues that this divisible thing is either non-existence or existent. Follow the reasoning why non-existence cannot be divided or have comparative properties.
4
See the conclusion from comparisons and past/future
Since we make comparisons (year longer than month) and speak of past and future, the thing must be real. Therefore time exists.
The proof is complete before the text cuts off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في إثبات الزمان : وهو دليل ، رتبناء للقائلين بإثبات الزمان . فنقول : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن مهنا شيئاً ينقسم إلى السنين تارة وإلى الشهور أخرى ، وإلى الأيام تارة ، وإلى الساعات أخرى ، والعلم بذلك أجلى العلوم البديهية وأظهرها ، ونعلم أن ذلك الشيء يعد بالسنين ، وأن السنة تعد بالشهور ، وتقدر بها [ وأن الشهر بعد بالأيام ويقدر بها . وأن اليوم يعد بالساعات ويقدر بها ] وكيف لا نقول ذلك. وكل من له سمة من العقل ، فإنه يعلم أن له من السنين كذا ؟ وأن الشهور المنقضية من هذه السنة التي نحن فيها كم هي ؟ [ وأن الشهور الباقية كم هي ؟ وأن الأيام من الشهر كم هي والباقية ( كم هي ؟ ] وأن الساعات المنقضية من هذا اليوم كم هي ؟ والباقية كم هي ؟ وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : قضت بدائه العقول بصحة هذه التقسيمات ، وكل من تشكك في صحتها ، قضى عليه بالجنون التام [ والعبه الشديد ] ثم نقول : هذا الذي حكم عليه العقل بكونه مورداً لهذه التقسيمات . إما أن يكون عدماً محضاً ، ونفياً صرفاً ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون موجوداً وثابتاً ومتحققاً ، والأول باطل ، لأن العدم المحض لا يمكن تقسيمه إلى الأجزاء والأبعاض [ ولا يمكن وصف تلك الأبعاض والأجزاء ] بأنه أزيد من غيره ، أو أنقص من غيره . ومعلوم أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن السنة أطول من الشهر ، والشهر أطول من اليوم، واليوم أطول من الساعة . وأيضاً : فصريح العقل يقضي على هذا الشيء بأن منه ما مضى، ومنه ما هو مستقبل . وصريح العقل يقضي بأن الماضي هو الذي كان حاضراً ، وقد انقضى . والمستقبل هـو الذي يتوقع حضوره ، ولم يحضر بعد . فلو لم يكن لهذا الشيء حضور وحصول البتة ، لامتنع أن يحكم العقل عليه بكونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً ، وحيث حكم صريح العقل عليه بذلك. علمنا أنه ليس عدماً محضاً [ ونفياً صرفاً ] ، بل لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً . ثم نقول : الموجودات على قسمين : منها ما هو قار
The third proof for the existence of time: It is an argument we have arranged for those who affirm the existence of time. So we say: We know by necessity that there is something that is sometimes divided into years, sometimes into months, sometimes into days, and sometimes into hours. Knowledge of this is the clearest and most manifest of intuitive sciences. And we know that that thing is counted by years, and the year is counted by months and measured by them, [and the month is then counted by days and measured by them, and the day is counted by hours and measured by them]. How could we not say that? And everyone who has a bit of intellect knows that he has so many years, and how many months have elapsed from this year we are in, [and how many months remain, and how many days of the month and how many remain], and how many hours have elapsed from this day and how many remain. And when this is established, we say: The innate dispositions of minds have judged the validity of these divisions, and anyone who doubts their validity is judged to be utterly insane [and severely foolish]. Then we say: This thing which the mind has judged to be subject to these divisions is either pure non-existence and absolute nothingness, or it is not like that, but rather it is existent, established, and realized. The first is false, because pure non-existence cannot be divided into parts and portions, [nor can those portions and parts be described] as being more or less than others. And it is known that clear reason judges that the year is longer than the month, the month is longer than the day, and the day is longer than the hour. Also, clear reason judges regarding this thing that some of it has passed and some is future. And clear reason judges that the past is what was present and has elapsed, and the future is what is expected to become present but has not yet become present. So if this thing had no presence or occurrence at all, it would be impossible for the mind to judge it as past and future. Since clear reason has judged it thus, we know that it is not pure non-existence [and absolute nothingness], but rather it must be existent. Then we say: Existents are of two types: some are permanent...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the existence of time, structured as an argument from everyday experience. The author starts by stating that we intuitively know there is something divisible into years, months, days, and hours. This is self-evident. He then argues that the mind accepts these divisions, and anyone who denies them is insane. Next, he presents a dichotomy: this divisible thing is either pure non-existence or it is existent. Non-existence cannot be divided into parts, nor can parts be compared as longer or shorter. Since we make such comparisons (year longer than month, etc.), and we speak of past and future, the thing must have a reality. Therefore, time exists. The proof concludes by noting that existents are either permanent (qarr) or something else (the text cuts off).

Key Terms
زمان (time)
تقسيم (division)
سنين (years)
شهور (months)
أيام (days)
ساعات (hours)
عدم (non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is an illusion or merely a mental construct.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the intuitive divisions of time and our rational judgments about them prove time is a real existent entity, not an illusion.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say pure non-existence cannot be divided?
A) Because it has no parts
B) Because it cannot be described as longer or shorter
C) Both A and B
D) Because it is not subject to reason

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's third proof shows that time must be real because non-existence cannot admit the divisions and comparisons we intuitively make.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is real and not an illusion, then every moment you live has real weight and will be accounted for. Are you spending your time as if each hour is a real, countable entity from Allah? What would change if you truly believed that non-existence cannot be divided, so your time is a real gift? Will you take a concrete step today to evaluate how you use the hours of this day as real portions of your life?
?
Sanusi says that anyone who doubts the divisions of time is considered utterly insane. Yet we often act as if time is something we can waste without consequence. Do you live as if the past is truly gone and the future is coming? Will you repent for moments wasted and resolve to use the remaining hours of this day for His sake?
?
The argument proves that time must exist because non-existence cannot have past and future. In light of this, how seriously do you take the fact that your past deeds are irrevocable and your future is real but unknown? Will you commit to making the present moment count for the akhirah?

Proof that Time Exists from the Possibility of Its Division
Al-Razi proves the existence of time through its division into years, months, days, and hours.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's rational proof for the existence of time based on the intuitive divisions of years, months, days, and hours.

Core Idea
Time exists because we meaningfully divide duration and compare lengths.

Al-Razi begins with an everyday observation: we count years, months, days, and hours without hesitation. This division is so natural that anyone who doubts it is considered irrational. He then builds a logical argument: the thing being divided must be real because nothingness cannot be partitioned or measured. By following his reasoning, you will see how intuitive practices lead to a proof of time's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation carefully. Notice the strong claim about the self-evidence of temporal divisions.
2
Identify the intuitive premise
Al-Razi starts by asserting that dividing time into years, months, etc., is clear and intuitive. Reflect on why he calls this the 'clearest of intuitive sciences'.
3
Follow the disjunctive syllogism
The core argument: The subject of these divisions is either pure non-existence or something existent. Trace how al-Razi eliminates the first option.
He uses two arguments: impossibility of division/comparison and reason's judgment of past/future.
4
Understand why non-existence fails
Non-existence cannot be divided into parts, nor can parts be compared as longer or shorter. Also, reason cannot attribute past or future to nothing. Grasp these points.
5
Conclude the necessity of time's existence
Since the first option is absurd, the second must be true: the entity that undergoes temporal divisions is real. That entity is time.
This proof does not rely on scripture; it is purely rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشهور أخرى ، وإلى الأيام تارة ، وإلى الساعات أخرى ، والعلم بذلك أجلى العلوم البديهية وأظهرها ، ونعلم أن ذلك الشيء يعد بالسنين ، وأن السنة تعد بالشهور ، وتقدر بها [ وأن الشهر بعد بالأيام ويقدر بها . وأن اليوم يعد بالساعات ويقدر بها ] وكيف لا نقول ذلك. وكل من له سمة من العقل ، فإنه يعلم أن له من السنين كذا ؟ وأن الشهور المنقضية من هذه السنة التي نحن فيها كم هي ؟ [ وأن الشهور الباقية كم هي ؟ وأن الأيام من الشهر كم هي والباقية كم هي ؟ ] وأن الساعات المنقضية من هذا اليوم كم هي ؟ والباقية كم هي ؟
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : قضت بدائه العقول بصحة هذه التقسيمات ، وكل من تشكك في صحتها ، قضى عليه بالجنون التام [ والعبه الشديد ] ثم نقول : هذا الذي حكم عليه العقل بكونه مورداً لهذه التقسيمات . إما أن يكون عدماً محضاً ، ونفياً صرفاً ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون موجوداً وثابتاً ومتحققاً ، والأول باطل ، لأن العدم المحض لا يمكن تقسيمه إلى الأجزاء والأبعاض [ ولا يمكن وصف تلك الأبعاض والأجزاء ] بأنه أزيد من غيره ، أو أنقص من غيره . ومعلوم أن صريح العقل يحكم بأن السنة أطول من الشهر ، والشهر أطول من اليوم، واليوم أطول من الساعة . وأيضاً : فصريح العقل يقضي على هذا الشيء بأن منه ما مضى، ومنه ما هو مستقبل . وصريح العقل يقضي بأن الماضي هو الذي كان حاضراً ، وقد انقضى . والمستقبل هـو الذي يتوقع حضوره ، ولم يحضر بعد . فلو لم يكن لهذا الشيء حضور وحصول البتة ، لامتنع أن يحكم العقل عليه بكونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً ، وحيث حكم صريح العقل عليه بذلك. علمنا أنه ليس عدماً محضاً [ ونفياً صرفاً ] ، بل لا بد وأن يكون موجوداً .
And to months another [division], and to days sometimes, and to hours another [division]. And knowledge of that is the clearest and most apparent of the intuitive sciences. And we know that that thing is counted by years, and the year is counted by months and is measured by them. [And the month is counted by days and is measured by them. And the day is counted by hours and is measured by them.] And how could we not say that? And everyone who has a mark of reason knows that he has such-and-such years? And how many are the months that have passed from this year in which we are? [And how many are the remaining months? And how many are the days of the month that have passed and how many are the remaining?] And how many are the hours that have passed from this day and how many are the remaining? 
And when this is established, we say: The intuitions of minds have judged the correctness of these divisions. And whoever doubts their correctness is judged to be in complete insanity [and intense foolishness]. Then we say: This thing that the intellect has judged to be the subject of these divisions is either pure non-existence and absolute negation, or it is not such, but rather is existent, established, and realized. The first is false, because pure non-existence cannot be divided into parts and fragments, [and those fragments and parts cannot be described] as being more than another or less than another. And it is known that clear reason judges that the year is longer than the month, and the month is longer than the day, and the day is longer than the hour. Also, clear reason decides concerning this thing that part of it is past and part of it is future. And clear reason decides that the past is that which was present and has elapsed, and the future is that which is expected to become present and has not become present yet. So if this thing had no presence and occurrence at all, it would be impossible for reason to judge it as being past and future. And since clear reason has so judged it, we know that it is not pure non-existence [and absolute negation], but must be existent.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the existence of time. It begins by noting the common human practice of dividing duration into years, months, days, and hours. This division is self-evidently valid; anyone who denies it is considered irrational. The author then presents a disjunctive syllogism: the entity that undergoes these divisions is either pure nothingness or something existent. The first option is impossible because nothingness cannot be divided into parts, nor can its parts be compared as longer or shorter—yet we clearly judge that a year is longer than a month, etc. Moreover, reason distinguishes past and future in this entity: the past is what was present and has gone, the future is what will become present. If the entity had no presence at all, reason could not apply past/future to it. Since reason does apply these categories, the entity must be real, not a mere nothing. Thus time exists.

Key Terms
زمان
مدة
عدمحض
بديهة العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes time exists and then divides it.
Correction: The argument does not assume existence; it proves it from the meaningfulness of division and comparison, which are impossible for nothing.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi reject the possibility that the thing divided into years, months, days, and hours is pure non-existence?
A) Because non-existence cannot be described as having past and future parts.
B) Because non-existence cannot be divided into parts or compared as longer or shorter.
C) Because reason judges that a year is longer than a month, which implies existence.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Time must be a real entity because we can divide it into parts, compare lengths, and distinguish past and future—all impossible for pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You divide your life into days, years, and hours without a second thought. But if time were not real—if it were just an illusion you imposed on events—would your regret over a wasted year still cut as deep? Al-Razi says time must be real because you can compare lengths and mark past and future. Right now, is there a 'past' hour you gave to something meaningless? Let that prove time's reality—and let it push you to reclaim the next hour for what truly matters before it slips into the past.
?
When you think about your own death, does the reality of time become more urgent? This lesson argues that time is not an empty fiction but a real thing that passes. If you live as though time were infinite, you are denying its nature. How would you live differently today if you truly believed each hour is a real piece of your existence—something that either builds or erodes your standing before Allah? Do not let another day pass without asking: is my time being spent on what will survive the test of reality?
?
Al-Razi insists that anyone who doubts the reality of time is insane. But your actions often suggest you doubt it: you postpone repentance, you delay a kind word, you assume there will be more opportunities. Is that not a practical denial of time's reality? If time is real and its hours are numbered, then your procrastination is not just laziness—it is a betrayal of reason. Look at the coming week. Name one thing you will do to treat time as the precious, real entity it is, not as a cheap resource.

Time as a Non-Stable Existent
Al-Razi classifies existents and proves time is non-stable and distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's classification of existents into stable and non-stable, and the proof that time is a non-stable existent distinct from bodies and their accidents.

Core Idea
Time's parts never coexist – this separates it from all stable beings.

Al-Razi begins by introducing a fundamental division among all existents: those whose parts are present together (stable) and those whose parts are not (non-stable). He then applies this to time, showing that its sequential nature makes it non-stable, and uses this to prove that time is neither a body nor any accident of a body. This lesson presents that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two categories
Read the passage and identify the two types of existents: stable (qār al-wujūd) and non-stable (ghayr qār al-wujūd). Note the definition: stable means its parts exist together; non-stable means they do not.
This classification is the foundation of the argument.
2
See why time is non-stable
Follow the reasoning: time can be divided into years, months, days, hours. No two of these parts ever exist together; one is always prior. Therefore, time is non-stable. The author says this is known intuitively with reflection.
This is the crucial premise.
3
Connect the conclusion
The passage then states that bodies, quantities, qualities, places, positions, relations, and all categories are stable. Since time is non-stable, it cannot belong to any of these. Hence time is a distinct entity.
This establishes the uniqueness of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : الموجودات على قسمين : منها ما هو قار الوجود ، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك ، والمراد من قولنا : قار الوجود : هو الذي تكون أجزاؤه حاصلة معاً . والمراد من قولنا : غير قار الوجود : ما لا يكون كذلك . فنقول : هذا الشيء الذي يمكن تقسيمه إلى السنين ، والشهور والأيام والساعات ليس من الموجودات التي تكون قارة الوجود ، بدليل أن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأنه لا يوجد جزءان منه [ معاً ] فلا يمكن حصول سنتين معاً ولا [ حصول ] شهرين ، ولا حصول يومين ، ولا حصول ساعتين معاً . ولا يمكن أيضاً أن يجتمع في الوجود منها جزءان ، وإن كانا أصغر الأجزاء ، بل كل اثنين يفرضان ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون أحدهما متقدماً على الآخر ، وإنه لا بد عند حصول أحدهما ، أن لا يكون الآخر حاصلاً ، وكل ذلك معلوم في البديهة ، بشرط [ حصول ] التأمل الصافي، واستحضار مفهومات هذه الألفاظ في الذهن. فيثبت : أن هذا الشيء المسمى بالمدة والزمان موجود ليس قار الوجود، لكن ثبت أن الجسم وكمه وكيفه وأينه ووضعه ومضافه وسائر مقولاته ، أمور قارة ، فوجب القطع بأن هذا الشيء المسمى بالمدة والزمان أمر مغاير لهذه الأشياء بأسرها.
Then we say: Existents are of two kinds: some have stable existence, and some are not such. By 'stable existence' we mean that whose parts are present together. By 'non-stable existence' we mean that which is not such. We say: This thing that can be divided into years, months, days, and hours is not among the existents that have stable existence, because the intuition of reason judges that no two parts of it exist [together]. So it is impossible for two years to exist together, nor [for] two months, nor for two days, nor for two hours together. Also, it is impossible for two parts of it to coexist in existence, even if they are the smallest parts. Rather, whenever two are assumed, one must be prior to the other, and when one is present, the other must not be present. All that is known intuitively, on condition of [attaining] pure reflection and bringing the concepts of these terms to mind. Thus it is established that this thing called duration and time is existent but not stable in existence. But it is established that the body, its quantity, its quality, its place, its position, its relation, and all its categories are stable things. So it must be decisively concluded that this thing called duration and time is something different from all these things.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a proof. It begins by dividing all existents into two categories: 'qarār al-wujūd' (stable existence), where parts coexist simultaneously, and 'ghayr qār al-wujūd' (non-stable existence), where parts do not coexist. Time clearly belongs to the second category: its parts (years, months, days, hours) never exist together; one is always prior to another. This is self-evident upon reflection. In contrast, bodies and their accidents (quantity, quality, place, position, relation, and the other Aristotelian categories) are stable—their parts coexist. Hence time cannot be a body or any of its accidents; it is a distinct entity. This argument establishes both the existence of time (already proved in the previous lesson) and its unique nature as a non-stable being, separate from the physical world.

Correction: Al-Razi insists time is truly existent, but its parts do not persist together; it is real but non-stable.

?

Proof that Time is not Motion and Motion Depends on Time
Al-Razi refutes the identification of time with motion and shows motion's dependence on time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that time is distinct from motion and that motion is dependent on time, not vice versa.

Core Idea
Motion requires time, but time does not require motion.

Al-Razi continues his investigation into the nature of time by addressing the common identification of time with motion. After dismissing other possibilities, he presents a fresh argument: the very definition of motion involves succession in time, making motion dependent on time. Moreover, we can conceive of time without motion, proving time's independence. This passage solidifies the distinction between time and motion and establishes time as a fundamental reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Pay attention to the logical flow from possibilities to definition to conclusion.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Notice that Al-Razi presents three possibilities for what time could be: motion itself, a property of motion, or something else. He quickly dismisses the first two.
The dismissal of the first two is based on previous arguments; this lesson focuses on the new argument.
3
Analyze the definition of motion
Read Al-Razi's definition: motion is 'successive occurrences in successive contiguous places'. Consider why succession implies time.
Succession means one after another, which requires a temporal framework.
4
Follow the argument of dependence
The passage argues that the relation of succession to time is posterior to the times themselves, so motion's essence depends on time. Try to rephrase this reasoning.
This shows motion cannot be the same as time.
5
Grasp the conclusion of time's independence
Finally, note that time can be conceived without motion (a period devoid of motion), so time is independent. This contrasts with motion's dependence.
This completes the proof that time is not motion nor a property of motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : هذا الشيء إما أن يكون هو الحركة أو صفة من صفات الحركة ، أو شيء مغاير للحركة ولجميع صفات الحركة . والأول باطل للوجوه الكثيرة التي شرحناها وقررناها في الفصل المتقدم .
والذي نزيده الآن أن نقول : لا معنى للحركة إلا حصولات متعاقبة في أحياز متعاقبة متلاصقة ، ولا معنى لتعاقب تلك الحصولات ، إلا حصول بعضها عقيب البعض في الأزمنة المتوالية ، لكن تواليها في تلك الأزمنة إضافة بينها وبين تلك الأزمنة [ والإضافة بينها وبين الأزمنة متأخرة عن تقرر حصول تلك الأزمنة ] فثبت : أن الحركة مفتقرة في تحقق ماهيتها إلى الزمان ، وأما الزمان فإنه غني في ذاته عن الحركة ، لأنه يمكننا فرض مدة خالية عن جميع الحركات
Then we say: This thing is either motion or a property of motion, or something different from motion and all its properties. The first is false due to the many aspects that we have explained and established in the preceding chapter. And what we add now is to say: Motion means nothing but successive occurrences in successive contiguous places, and the succession of those occurrences means nothing but the occurrence of some after others in successive times. But their succession in those times is a relation between them and those times, [and the relation between them and the times is posterior to the establishment of the occurrence of those times.] So it is established that motion, in the realization of its essence, is dependent on time. As for time, it is independent in its essence from motion, because we can conceive a period devoid of all motions.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and a proof. Al-Razi first presents three possibilities for what time could be: motion itself, a property of motion, or something entirely different. He dismisses the first two (already argued in a previous chapter) and here provides an additional argument. He defines motion as a series of successive occurrences in contiguous places. The very notion of succession implies a sequence in time—one occurrence after another in successive times. That temporal succession is a relation between the motion-instances and the times, and this relation is logically posterior to the times themselves. Therefore, motion in its essence requires time; time is more fundamental. Moreover, time can be conceived without motion—we can imagine a duration empty of all motion. Hence time is not motion nor a property of it; it is an independent, more basic reality. This argument also reinforces that time is not any kind of body or accident, as already shown in the previous lesson.

Correction: Al-Razi argues the opposite: motion depends on time because its definition involves temporal succession. Time can exist without motion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

What change in your daily routine would reflect this understanding?
