The Twelfth Argument: Time is Not Motion
Al-Razi presents a reductio ad absurdum to show time is not motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum to prove that time cannot be identical to the motion of the celestial sphere.

Core Idea
If time were motion, saying the sphere was non-existent before a time would be self-contradictory.

Al-Razi presents his twelfth argument against the view that time is the motion of the celestial sphere. He begins with an intuitively acceptable statement: it is not obviously absurd to say that the sphere was non-existent a million years ago. He then shows that if time were motion, this statement would lead to a blatant contradiction, proving that time is something distinct from motion. This argument relies on rational intuition and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing yet. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
The passage is a single, compact reductio argument.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice that Al-Razi starts by stating it is not impossible or obviously false to conceive the sphere having been non-existent a million years ago. This premise is crucial.
He uses 'badīhat al-ʿaql' (primary intuition of the intellect).
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
Al-Razi then says: if time were the sphere's motion, then the statement would mean the sphere's motion was non-existent when it existed, a clear contradiction. Trace this step.
This is the heart of the reductio.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since the original statement is not obviously false, but the implication if time=motion is obviously false, time must be something other than motion and its magnitude.
The conclusion is 'time is other than motion' (mugheyr).
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how Al-Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: assume the opponent's view, derive a contradiction, and thus prove the view false.
This is a standard method in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر: لا شك أنه غير ممتنع في بديهة العقل، أن يتصور أن الفلك كان معدوماً قبل هذا الوقت، بألف ألف سنة. ولا شك أن هذا الحكم ليس بديهي البطلان والفساد. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: معنى قولنا: إن الفلك كان معدوماً قبل هذا الوقت بألف ألف سنة: هو أن الوقت المتقدم على هذا الوقت الحاضر، بمقدار ألف ألف سنة، كان الفلك معدوماً فيه. وهذا يقتضي إضافة عدم الفلك، وإضافة عدم حركة الفلك إلى ذلك الوقت المعين. فلو كان الزمان عبارة عن حركة الفلك، لصار معنى هذا الكلام: أن حركة الفلك كانت معدومة، عندما كانت حركة الفلك موجودة. ومعلوم أن هذا الكلام أولى بالفساد، وبديهي البطلان. فلما كان قولنا: الحركة الفلكية كانت معدومة قبل هذا الوقت بألف ألف سنة ليس بديهي البطلان، وكانت إضافة عدم الحركة إلى نفس وجودها، وإلى نفس وجود مقدارها بديهي البطلان، علمنا: أن الزمان شيء مغاير للحركة الفلكية والمقدارها. وذلك هو المطلوب.
The twelfth argument: There is no doubt that it is not impossible in the primary intuition of the intellect (badīhat al-ʿaql) to conceive that the sphere (al-falak) was non-existent before this time by a thousand thousand years. And there is no doubt that this judgment is not obviously false and corrupt. And when this is established, we say: The meaning of our statement 'the sphere was non-existent before this time by a thousand thousand years' is that the time preceding this present time by a thousand thousand years was the time in which the sphere was non-existent. And this requires the attribution of non-existence of the sphere and the attribution of non-existence of the sphere's motion to that specific time. For if time were the motion of the sphere, the meaning of this statement would become: the motion of the sphere was non-existent when the motion of the sphere was existing. And it is known that this statement is more deserving of corruption and is obviously false. Therefore, since our statement 'the celestial motion was non-existent before this time by a thousand thousand years' is not obviously false, while the attribution of non-existence of motion to its own existence and to the existence of its magnitude is obviously false, we know that time is something other than the celestial motion (al-ḥaraka al-falakiyya) and its magnitude. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is the twelfth argument, a reductio ad absurdum. Al-Razi starts from an intuitively acceptable proposition: it is not obviously absurd to say that the celestial sphere (al-falak) was non-existent a million years ago. This presupposes a time before the sphere's existence. If time were identical to the sphere's motion, then saying 'the sphere was non-existent at a prior time' would mean 'the sphere's motion was non-existent when the sphere's motion existed'—a blatant contradiction. Since the former statement is not obviously absurd while the latter is, time cannot be the same as motion or the magnitude of motion. The argument relies on the idea that we can meaningfully attribute non-existence of motion to a time that is independent of that motion. The conclusion is that time is something else (maghyār).

Key Terms
بديهة العقل
الفلك
الحركة الفلكية
مقدار
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is nothing but the motion of the celestial sphere.
Correction: Al-Razi shows this leads to a self-contradiction when we speak of the sphere's non-existence before a time, so time must be something independent of motion.

Checkpoint
What does the twelfth argument assume about the statement 'the sphere was non-existent before this time by a million years'?
A) It is obviously false and corrupt.
B) It is impossible to conceive.
C) It is not obviously false.
D) It implies time is the sphere's motion.

Key Takeaway
The intuitive conceivability of the sphere's non-existence before a time proves that time cannot be identical to the sphere's motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi says it is not self-evidently false to imagine the sphere non-existent a million years ago. Have you ever truly considered the possibility that the entire universe might not have existed? ?
The argument shows that time is not just the motions we measure. Does your life treat time as a mere sequence of actions and events, or do you see it as a created reality that carries divine signs? What would change if you began each day by acknowledging that time itself is a gift from Allah, separate from what you do within it? Make a plan to pause at least once today to reflect on the nature of time.

Al-Razi relies on rational intuition to reject a false belief. When have you ignored your own God-given intuition about a matter of faith or life, only to later realize it was leading you to contradiction? The Thirteenth Argument: Time Is Eternal, Motion Is Not
Al-Razi presents the Platonic argument with two sub-proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Platonic argument that time is eternal and motion cannot be eternal, and how this proves they are distinct realities.

Core Idea
Time's eternity and motion's non-eternity show they are fundamentally distinct.

Al-Razi presents the thirteenth argument, attributed to the followers of Plato, which uses two separate demonstrations to prove that time is not identical to motion. This argument relies on essential definitions: time cannot have a temporal beginning, while motion, by its nature, requires a prior state. Understanding these proofs clarifies a key distinction in Islamic cosmology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the overall argument structure.
The passage contains two sub-proofs: one for time, one for motion.
2
Analyze the proof for time's eternity
Focus on the first part: why time must be eternal. Note the logical contradiction if time were temporal.
Al-Razi uses a reductio ad absurdum: if time had a beginning, its non-existence would precede its existence temporally, implying a contradiction.
3
Analyze the proof for motion's non-eternity
Focus on the second part: why motion cannot be eternal. Understand the definition of motion as transition and why that precludes eternity.
The essence of motion (intiqal) requires a prior state, which is incompatible with the definition of eternity (al-azaliyya).
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine the two results: time is eternal, motion is not, therefore time is other than motion. Reflect on why this matters for theology.
The conclusion directly opposes those who identify time with motion or its measure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة عشر: قال أصحاب أفلاطون: دل البرهان على أن الزمان قديم، ودل البرهان على أن الحركة يمتنع أن تكون قديمة. وهذا يدل على أن الزمان مغاير للحركة، ومغاير لجميع صفات الحركة. أما بيان أن الزمان قديم: فلأنه لو كان مسبوقاً بالعدم، لكان عدمه سابقاً على وجوده سبقاً بالزمان، وذلك يوجب أن يكون كونها موجوداً حال كونه معدوماً، وهو محال. وأما بيان أن الحركة يمتنع أن تكون قديمة: فذلك لأن الحركة انتقال من حال إلى حال، فحقيقة الحركة تقتضي كونها مسبوقة بالغير، وحقيقة الأزلية والقدم تنافي المسبوقية بالغير، والجمع بينهما محال. فثبت: أن الزمان قديم. وثبت: أن الحركة يمتنع كونها قديمة، فيلزم أن يقال: الزمان غير الحركة، وغير مقدار الحركة، وهو المطلوب.
The thirteenth argument: The followers of Plato said: Demonstration indicates that time is eternal (qadīm), and demonstration indicates that motion cannot be eternal. And this indicates that time is other than motion, and other than all attributes of motion. As for the explanation that time is eternal: because if it were preceded by non-existence, then its non-existence would precede its existence with a temporal precedence (sabqan bi-l-zamān), and that would necessitate that it exist while it is non-existent, which is impossible. And as for the explanation that motion cannot be eternal: that is because motion is a transition (intiqāl) from state to state, so the essence of motion requires that it be preceded by something else (al-ghayr), and the essence of eternity (al-azaliyya) and primacy (al-qidam) are incompatible with being preceded by something else, and combining them is impossible. So it is established that time is eternal. And it is established that motion cannot be eternal. Consequently, it must be said: time is other than motion, and other than the magnitude of motion. And that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is the thirteenth argument, attributed to the followers of Plato (Aṣḥāb Aflāṭūn). It consists of two sub-proofs. First, time is shown to be eternal: if time were temporal (i.e., came into existence after non-existence), then its non-existence would be temporally prior to its existence. But that would mean time exists while it does not exist—a contradiction. Therefore time must be eternal. Second, motion cannot be eternal: motion is essentially a transition from one state to another; thus by definition it is preceded by a previous state (mashūqa bi-l-ghayr). Eternity (al-azaliyya) and primacy (al-qidam) imply no prior cause or state. Since these are incompatible, motion cannot be eternal. Given that time is eternal and motion is not, they are distinct (ghayr) in reality: time is other than motion and its magnitude.

Key Terms
قديم
الحركة
ازلية
قدم
مسبوقة بالغير
انتقال
أصحاب أفلاطون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion can be eternal if it is circular or continuous.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that regardless of the type, motion is inherently a transition from state to state, so it requires a prior state and thus cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint

A) Because motion is a substance.
B) Because motion is a transition from state to state, and thus requires being preceded by something else.
C) Because the philosophers reject it.
D) Because time is not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time is eternal and motion is not, therefore they are distinct realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof that Time is Not Motion or Its Measure
Proof that time is not motion via circularity argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof that time is neither identical to motion nor a measure of motion, based on the impossibility of circularity.

Core Idea
If time were motion, it would be both the container and the contained – an impossibility.

This passage presents a concise syllogism to refute the idea that time is identical to motion or its quantitative measure. The argument relies on the impossibility of circularity, a key logical principle. Let's examine how the reasoning unfolds step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you analyze.
2
Identify the key logical move
Notice that the argument begins by stating the impossibility of circularity. Why is circularity impossible? Understand that this is the foundation of the proof.
Circularity here means something containing itself or being its own measure.
3
Trace the syllogism
The passage then presents a syllogism: All motions occur in time; no motion occurs in itself or in its own measure; therefore, time is not motion nor a measure of motion. Write out the two premises and the conclusion.
The first premise is implied but stated clearly: 'Every motion is obtained in time.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion denies both that time is motion and that time is a measure of motion. Ask yourself: why would someone think time is a measure of motion? The argument refutes both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاصل فيه أيضاً محال، لامتناع الدور، وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع كون الحركة حاصلة في نفسها، ولا في مقدار نفسها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: كل حركة فهي حاصلة في الزمان، ولا شيء من الحركات حاصلة في نفسها ولا في مقدار نفسها، ينتج: فلا شيء من الزمان بحركة، ولا بمقدار الحركة. وهو المطلوب.
That which is obtained in it also is impossible, because of the impossibility of circularity. And if it is so, then it is impossible for motion to be obtained in itself, nor in its own measure. And when this is established we say: Every motion is obtained in time, and none of the motions are obtained in themselves nor in their own measure. It follows: None of time is motion, nor is it a measure of motion. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that time is not motion nor a measure of motion. It begins by stating that something obtained in time being also impossible due to circularity. Then it asserts that if that is the case, then motion cannot be obtained in itself or in its own measure. Then the main syllogism: All motions occur in time; no motion occurs in itself or in its own measure; therefore, time is not motion nor a measure of motion. The conclusion is stated: 'And that is what was sought.'

Key Terms
زمان (time)
حركة (motion)
مقدار (measure)
دور (circularity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is identical to motion or a measurable property of motion.
Correction: The argument shows that if time were motion, it would lead to the impossible situation of motion being in itself. Therefore, time must be distinct from motion and not a measure of motion.

Checkpoint
What does this proof conclude about time?
A) Time is motion
B) Time is a measure of motion
C) Time is neither motion nor a measure of motion
D) Time is a substance

Key Takeaway
Time is distinct from motion and its measure; circularity makes identity impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that time is not your actions, nor their duration. If time is a separate gift from Allah, how does that change the urgency you feel about every passing moment? Will you today treat time as something you own, or as something that owns you?
?
The argument shows that circular reasoning is impossible. Are you caught in a circular life—working to earn, to keep working—without stepping back to consider the container (time) that holds it all? What one specific habit will you change to align your use of time with the truth that it is not your motion?
?
If time is not a measure of your deeds, then your worth is not measured by how much you do. Will you stop measuring yourself by busyness? What space will you create today to simply be in time, not consumed by motion?

The Fifteenth Proof: Time is Not a Measure of Motion
The fifteenth proof using a syllogism to argue time is not a measure of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifteenth proof that time is not a measure of motion, based on a syllogism about inseparability.

Core Idea
Time does not vanish when motion stops, so it cannot be a measure of motion.

In this lesson, we examine the fifteenth proof, which uses a simple logical argument to show that time cannot be the measure of motion. The proof relies on the observation that time persists even when motion ceases, unlike a true measure which would cease. We will break down the syllogism and see why it works.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure of a formal syllogism with two premises and a conclusion.
The proof is very concise; each part is essential.
2
Identify the two premises and conclusion
The major premise states that time does not cease with motion. The minor premise states that every measure of motion does cease with motion. The conclusion follows logically.
This is a classic categorical syllogism.
3
Understand why the conclusion follows
If something (time) lacks a property that all measures of motion have, then it cannot be a measure of motion.
This is a valid deductive argument.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some think time stops when motion stops. This proof shows otherwise, reinforcing that time is not dependent on motion.
The author emphasizes this suffices for the topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة عشر. الزمان لا يرتفع بارتفاع الحركة، وكل ما كان مقداراً للحركة، فإنه يرتفع بارتفاع الحركة، ينتج: فلا شيء من الزمان بمقدار الحركة. ولنكتف بهذا القدر من الدلائل في هذا المطلوب
The fifteenth proof. Time does not cease with the cessation of motion, and everything that is a measure of motion ceases with the cessation of motion. It follows: None of time is a measure of motion. And we suffice with this amount of proofs for this sought matter.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is the fifteenth proof. It presents a simple syllogism: Time does not disappear when motion disappears (major premise); every measure of motion disappears when motion disappears (minor premise); therefore, time is not a measure of motion. The author then states that this suffices for the proofs on this topic.

Key Terms
حجة (proof)
ارتفاع (cessation)
مقدار (measure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If motion stops, time also stops.
Correction: The proof shows time does not cease when motion ceases, so time is not a measure of motion. Time continues independently.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise of the fifteenth proof?
A) Time does not cease with cessation of motion
B) Every measure of motion ceases with cessation of motion
C) Motion is eternal
D) Time is eternal

Key Takeaway
Through a simple syllogism, the author demonstrates that time is not a measure of motion because it does not vanish when motion does.

Time as Measure of Motion: The Second Argument and Its Refutation
Second argument for time as motion's measure and its partial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second argument for time being a measure of motion and the initial response that challenges its key premise.

Core Idea
Not everything that admits equality and difference is a quantity by itself.

This passage presents a detailed argument from the opponents claiming time is merely a measure of motion, followed by the beginning of the refutation. The argument builds from the idea that time admits equality and difference, making it a quantity, then narrows it down to a non-stationary quantity ultimately identified with motion. The refutation strikes at the foundation: being susceptible to equality and difference does not automatically make something a quantity by its own essence. We'll walk through each step and see how the commentator begins to dismantle the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument premises
The opponents present a chain of reasoning: time admits equality/difference → time is a quantity → time is non-stationary → time has a matter → that matter cannot be the body or the moving object → hence time must be a measure of motion. Read the Arabic and translation to follow the logic.
Notice how each step relies on the previous one; the argument is tightly packed.
2
Identify the crucial premise
The key step is that everything which admits equality and difference is a quantity. Ask yourself: Is that always true? The refutation will question this.
This premise is what allows the argument to proceed.
3
Examine the refutation's opening move
The commentator says: We do not accept that everything susceptible to equality and difference is a quantity by itself. Only things that are so by their essence are quantities. The opponents have not proven that time is a quantity by essence.
The refutation is incomplete, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on the significance
This lesson shows how a seemingly tight argument can be undone by challenging an unstated assumption. Time could have an independent reality, and the burden of proof is on those who claim otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية لهم : قالوا إنا قد بينا في دليل الإمكانات : أن الزمان قابل للمساواة، والمفاوتة ثم نقول : وكل ما كان كذلك فهو كم. ينتج : فالزمان كم. ثم نقول : وكل كم إما أن يكون قار الذات أو لا يكون، والأول باطل، وإلا كان الوقت الحاضر عين الماضي وعين المستقبل. هذا خلف فثبت : أنه كم غير قار الذات، وإذا كان كذلك وجب أن تكون أجزاؤه على التقضي والمرور، وكل ما كان كذلك فله مادة، يكون هو صفة من صفاتها، وحالة من أحوالها بناء على أنه لا بد لكل محدث من مادة. فالزمان لا بد له من مادة، ومادته إما مادة الجسم، وإما أن تكون مادته صفة من صفات الجسم والأول باطل. لأن الزمان إن جعل مقداراً لمادة المسافة فهو محال، لأن المختلفين في هذا المقدار قد يستويان في المسافة، وإن جعل مقداراً لمادة المتحرك فهو أيضاً محال، وإلا لكان كلما كان الزمان أعظم، كان المتحرك أعظم. هذا خلف. فثبت : أن الزمان مقدار لهيئة حالة في الجسم وتلك الهيئة إما قارة وإما غير قارة. والأول باطل لأن مقدار الهيئة القارة يجب أن يكون قاراً، وهذا المقدار غير قار، فبقي أن الزمان مقدار لهيئة غير قارة، وهي الحركة فثبت : أن الزمان مقدار الحركة. والجواب : لا نسلم أن كل ما كان قابلاً للمساواة والمفاوتة كان كما بالذات . بل كل ما قبل المساواة والمفاوتة لذاته، لا لاجل غيره ، كان كماً بالذات . وأنتم ما أقمتم الدلالة على أن الزمان قابل للمساواة والمفاوتة لذاته فإنه ليس يمتنع أن يقال : الزمان له في ذاته حقيقة مستقلة بنفسها قائمة بذاتها ،
Their second argument: They said: We have shown in the proof of possibilities that time is susceptible to equality and difference. Then we say: Everything that is so is a quantity. It follows: Time is a quantity. Then we say: Every quantity is either stationary in essence or not. The first is false, otherwise the present time would be identical to the past and identical to the future. This is a contradiction. So it is established that it is a non-stationary quantity. And if so, its parts must be in a state of passing away and passing. And everything that is so has a matter (madda), being one of its attributes and a state of its states, based on the fact that every originated thing (muhdath) must have a matter. Therefore, time must have a matter. Its matter is either the matter of the body or it is an attribute of the body. The first is false because if time is made a measure of the matter of distance, it is impossible, since two differing in this measure may be equal in distance. And if it is made a measure of the matter of the moving object, it is also impossible, otherwise whenever time is greater, the moving object would be greater. This is a contradiction. So it is established that time is a measure of a state (hay'a) subsisting in the body. And that state is either stationary or non-stationary. The first is false because the measure of a stationary state must be stationary, but this measure is non-stationary. So it remains that time is a measure of a non-stationary state, which is motion. Thus it is established that time is a measure of motion. The answer: We do not accept that everything that is susceptible to equality and difference is a quantity by itself. Rather, everything that is susceptible to equality and difference by its essence, not due to something else, is a quantity by itself. And you have not provided evidence that time is susceptible to equality and difference by its essence, for it is not impossible to say: Time in its essence has an independent reality subsisting by itself...
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is the second argument from the opponents and the beginning of its refutation. The opponents argue: (1) Time is susceptible to equality and difference (i.e., can be measured). (2) Anything that admits equality and difference is a quantity. So time is a quantity. (3) All quantities are either stationary or non-stationary. Stationary is impossible for time, otherwise present would equal past and future. So time is a non-stationary quantity. (4) Non-stationary quantities have a matter which is a substrate. Time must have a matter. (5) Its matter cannot be the matter of the body (distance), because differing times can have same distance; nor can it be the matter of the moving object, because then greater time would imply greater size. So time must be a measure of a state in the body. (6) That state cannot be stationary (since its measure would be stationary), so it must be a non-stationary state, i.e., motion. Therefore time is a measure of motion. The refutation begins by challenging the premise: Not everything that admits equality and difference is intrinsically a quantity; only things that do so by their own essence are quantities. The opponents have not proven that time does so by its essence; time may have its own independent reality. The answer is incomplete at the page end.

Key Terms
كم (quantity)
قار الذات (stationary in essence)
مادة (matter)
هيئة (state)
مساواة (equality)
مفاوتة (difference)
مقدار (measure)
حركة (motion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time must have a material substrate because it is a non-stationary quantity.
Correction: The refutation questions whether time is truly a quantity by itself; it may have an independent reality without needing a material substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the refutation of the second argument?
A) Time is not a quantity
B) Time is not susceptible to equality and difference
C) Not everything susceptible to equality and difference is a quantity by itself
D) Time is stationary

Key Takeaway
A seemingly solid argument can be undermined by challenging an unproven premise about what constitutes a quantity by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals how a seemingly logical argument collapses when a premise is not proven to be intrinsic. In your own life, have you built a belief about Allah or His creation on an assumption you never questioned? What would it take for you to test that assumption against what the Quran and Sunnah actually say?
?
The opponents here assumed that because time can be measured, it must be a quantity by itself. How can you move from feeling to certainty?

Time is something you experience every moment, yet its true nature is debated. Proof that time is a continuous quantity
Presentation of a proof that time must be continuous, not composed of successive instants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that time must be a continuous quantity, not composed of successive instants.

Core Idea
Time is continuous because assuming it is made of instants leads to a contradiction with the divisibility of space.

In this passage, the author presents a proof by contradiction to show that time cannot be a discrete quantity composed of successive instants. Using a motion across a divisible distance, he derives a contradiction that forces the conclusion that time must be continuous. This argument relies on the philosophical consensus that space is infinitely divisible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure of the proof.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The proof begins by assuming the opposite of what it wants to prove: that time is composed of successive instants. Find this in the text.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the argument: motion to a midpoint leads to division of motion and instant, resulting in a contradiction. See how the author derives the contradiction.
Pay attention to the phrase 'هذا خلف' (this is a contradiction).
4
Understand the conclusion
The contradiction forces the conclusion that time cannot be discrete, so it must be continuous. Confirm the final sentence.
The conclusion is 'فوجب أن يكون كماً متصلاً'.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters in Islamic philosophy: it establishes the nature of time as a continuous quantity, which has implications for physics and metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان [ منقسماً (۱) ] . فحينئذ يلزم أن تكون الحركة إلى نصفه متقدمة في الوجود على الحركة من نصفه إلى آخره، فحينئذ تنقسم الحركة ، وينقسم ذلك الآن . وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك. هذا خلف . فيلزم : أن يكون ذلك القدر من المسافة غير منقسم ، فيثبت : أن الزمان لو كان مركباً من الآنات المتعاقبة ، والدفعات المتتالية ، لزم أن تكون المسافة مركبة من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ ، إلا أن هذا مشهور البطلان عند الحكماء ، فيلزم أن لا يكون الزمان مركباً من الآنات المتتالية ، والدفعات المتعاقبة. وإذا ثبت هذا وجب أن لا يكون الزمان كماً منفصلاً ، فوجب أن يكون كماً متصلاً . وهو المطلوب . هذا تقرير كلام القوم
It was [divisible (1)]. Then it would necessarily follow that motion to its half precedes in existence motion from its half to its end, then motion would be divided, and that instant would be divided. But we assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction. So it is necessary that that amount of distance is indivisible. Thus it is established: If time were composed of successive instants and consecutive moments, then distance would necessarily be composed of indivisible parts, except that this is well-known to be false among the philosophers. So it is necessary that time is not composed of consecutive instants and successive moments. And when this is established, it is necessary that time is not a discrete quantity, so it is necessary that it is a continuous quantity. That is what was sought. This is the formulation of the statement of the people.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that time must be a continuous quantity (kamm muttasil) and not a discrete one. It begins by assuming the opposite: if time were composed of successive instants, then any motion over a distance would require that the motion to the midpoint occurs before the motion from the midpoint to the end, which would divide both the motion and the instant. Since this contradicts the original assumption (that the motion occurs in a single instant), it leads to a contradiction. Therefore, the distance itself must be indivisible, and thus time cannot be composed of instants. The argument uses the philosophical consensus that space is infinitely divisible, so time must be continuous.

Key Terms
الزمان (zaman, time)
الآنات (ānāt, instants)
كم متصل (kamm muttasil, continuous quantity)
كم منفصل (kamm munfasil, discrete quantity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the proof conclude about the nature of time?
A) Time is composed of indivisible instants.
B) Time is a continuous quantity.
C) Time is a discrete quantity.
D) Time cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
Time is a continuous quantity because the assumption that it is composed of successive instants leads to a contradiction with the divisibility of space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that time is continuous, not a series of separate instants. How does this affect your perception of your own life? Do you see your days as a continuous journey toward Allah, or as isolated moments of obedience and disobedience? What would change if you truly lived in the reality of continuous time?
?
If time were discrete, every 'now' would be disconnected from the next. The argument shows that is false. In your spiritual life, do you treat repentance as a one-time event (an instant) or as a continuous state of turning back? How can you make your repentance continuous rather than sporadic?
?
The proof relies on the idea that contradiction reveals truth. Think of a contradiction in your own life between what you believe and how you act. What does that contradiction reveal about the true state of your heart? Will you resolve it by changing your belief or your action?

The Indivisible Present Moment
First argument that time cannot be continuous, based on indivisibility of the present moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the continuity of time, based on the indivisibility of the present moment, and how it leads to the conclusion that time is composed of successive instants.

Core Idea
If the present moment were divisible, it wouldn't be present.

In this passage, Sanusi presents the first of several objections to the idea that time is a continuous quantity. He argues that the present moment must be indivisible, and from that he deduces that time consists of a succession of discrete instants. This argument is part of a dialectical process where the author presents objections before answering them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the logical flow: what is the claim about the present moment?
Notice the footnotes indicating variant readings.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument asserts that the present moment cannot be divided into two sequential parts. Why? Because if it were, one part would exist while the other would not, contradicting its 'presence.'
3
Understand the contrapositive
The reasoning uses a contrapositive: everything present is indivisible. Make sure you see how this follows from the premise.
This is a logical conversion step.
4
Follow the conclusion about time
If the present moment is indivisible, its ceasing must be instantaneous, leading to an immediate next moment. Repeating this yields a succession of instants, showing time is composed of such instants.
The argument does not prove time is actually discrete; it is an objection to be answered later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا نذكر وجوهاً في بيان أنه يمتنع أن يكون الزمان كما متصلاً ثم نجيب عن حجتهم فنقول : الذي يدل على أنه يمتنع كون الزمان كماً متصلاً وجوه : الحجة الأولى : أن نقول : هذا الآن الحاضر الذي هو نهاية الماضي وبداية المستقبل ، يمتنع أن يكون قابلا للانقسام إلى جزءين يكون أحدهما سابقاً على الآخر ، إذ لو كان الأمر كذلك ، لكان عند حضور النصف الأول منه ، لا يكون النصف الثاني حاصلاً ، وعند مجيء النصف الثاني منه يكون النصف الأول منه فائتاً [ زائلا (۲) ] فثبت : أن كل ما كان قابلاً للانقسام على وجه يكون أحد قسميه واجب التقدم على الآخر ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون حاضراً ، وهذا ينعكس انعكاس النقيض : أن كل ما كان حاضراً ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون منقسماً ، فيثبت : أن هذا الآن الحاضر ، غير قابل للقسمة على الوجه المذكور . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : آن (۳) عدمه يجب أن يكون دفعة ، إذ لو كان على التدريج ، لكان منقسماً . لكنا بينا أنه غير منقسم ، وإذا كان عدمه دفعة ، كان الآن الذي هو أول عدمه يكون متصلاً بوجوده بوجوده ، فقد تتالى هذان الآنان (۱) ثم الكلام في الآن الثاني ، كما في الأول ، وهذا يوجب القطع بتتالي الآنات ، وهو المطلوب
And know that we will mention aspects showing that it is impossible for time to be a continuous quantity, then we will answer their proof. We say: That which indicates that it is impossible for time to be a continuous quantity are aspects: The first proof: We say: This present moment, which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, cannot be divisible into two parts such that one of them precedes the other. For if it were so, then at the presence of its first half, the second half would not exist, and at the coming of its second half, its first half would have passed [ceased (2)]. So it is established: everything that is capable of division in a manner where one of its two parts necessarily precedes the other cannot be present. And this is contrapositive conversion: everything that is present cannot be divisible. So it is established that this present moment is not divisible in the aforementioned manner. When this is established, we say: Its ceasing must be instantaneous, for if it were gradual, it would be divisible. But we have shown that it is indivisible. And if its ceasing is instantaneous, then the moment which is the first of its ceasing is contiguous with its existence. So these two moments are consecutive. Then the discussion about the second moment is like that about the first. This necessitates the succession of moments, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the continuity of time, arguing that time must be composed of successive instants. It begins by asserting that the present moment (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) cannot be divided into parts that are sequential, because if it were, one part would exist while the other would not, contradicting its 'presence.' Therefore, the present moment is indivisible. Consequently, its ceasing (adam) must occur instantaneously, not gradually. If it ceases instantaneously, then the moment that is the first of its ceasing immediately follows the moment of its existence, resulting in two consecutive instants. By repeating this reasoning, an infinite succession of instants is established, which means time is composed of such instants.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر (al-ān al-ḥāḍir, the present moment)
الانقسام (al-inqisām, division)
العدم (al-‘adam, ceasing/non-existence)
دفعة (daf‘atan, instantaneously)
تتالي الآنات (tatālī al-ānāt, succession of instants)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this argument definitively proves that time is discrete.
Correction: The author is presenting this as an objection to be answered later; it is part of a dialectical process, not a final conclusion.

Checkpoint
What is the key claim about the present moment in this argument?
A) It is infinitely divisible.
B) It cannot be divided into sequential parts.
C) It is the same as the past moment.
D) It lasts for a duration.

Key Takeaway
The present moment is indivisible, and from that we can deduce that time consists of a succession of instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the present moment is truly indivisible and instantly becomes past, what does that demand of your intention (niyyah) in your next prayer? Will you commit to a single, undivided focus on Allah? What is that focus?
?
The proof shows that the present cannot hold division. Consider your heart when you recite Qur'an: is it divided among worldly concerns? This very second, you have a chance to unify it. What specific worry will you release before your next sujood?
?
The argument ends with a succession of instants, each gone before you grasp it. Tomorrow, you will not have this same moment. What is one act of obedience you will perform today, right now, because you see its urgency?

Rebuttal: answering the objection about the present moment
Objection and answer about the first ceasing of the moment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical structure of the objection about the moment's first ceasing and how the author defends the necessity of a first moment of non-existence.

Core Idea
The moment's ceasing must have a first instant; otherwise, change is impossible.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated objection to the argument for the succession of instants. The objector attempts to show that the conclusion does not follow by proposing alternative meanings of 'first ceasing.' The author's response clarifies the nature of change and defends the need for a definite first moment of non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection and the author's initial response. Notice how the objector tries to introduce a third option for the ceasing of the moment.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'first ceasing'
The objector distinguishes two interpretations: the first ceasing being the moment's existence itself, or a first moment of non-existence. See how each leads to different consequences.
3
Follow the author's proof
The author argues that change from existence to non-existence necessitates a first moment of non-existence. Trace this reasoning step by step.
This is the core of the rebuttal.

فإن قيل : قولكم عدم الآن ، إما أن يقع دفعة ، أو على التدريج . قلنا : ههنا قسم ثالث وهو أن يقع عدم الآن في جميع الزمان الذي بعده . لا يقال : ليس كلامنا في عدم الآن ، بل كلامنا في أول عدمه ، ومن المعلوم أن أول عدمه لا يحصل في جميع الزمان الذي بعده، فإن أول عدمه لا يكون حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن أول عدمه ، إما أن يحصل دفعة ، وإما أن يحصل على التدريج، وحينئذ يعود الكلام المذكور . لأنا نقول : ما المراد بأول عدم الآن ؟ إن أردتم به أن لعدمه أولاً ، فهذا مسلم . لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أول عدمه هو وجوده ، فحصول ذلك الآن وثبوته هو بعينه أول الزمان عدمه، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم تتالي الآنات . وإن أردتم به أن لعدمه أولاً يكون هو فيه معدوماً [ فهذا ممنوع . فيا الدليل على أن لعدمه أولاً ، هو فيه معدوماً ؟ ] (٢) وذلك [ باطل (۳) ] لأن هذا الكلام إنما يصح على القول بتتالي الآنات ، فلو أثبتنا تتالي الآنات بناء على هذه المقدمة ، لزم الدور ، وأنه باطل . فهذا أقصى ما يمكن إيراده من السؤال على الحجة [ التي ذكرناها على إثبات تتالي الآنات (1) ] . والجواب : إنا ندعى أن عدم الآن ، لا بد وأن يحصل له [ أول يكون ذلك الآن معدوماً فيه والدليل عليه : أن هذا الآن كان موجوداً ثم صار معدوماً. فعدمه حدث بعد أن لم يكن () ] فهذا العدم له أول لا محالة، وذلك العدم يكون حاصلاً في ذلك الأول لا محالة. وإلا فهو بعد لم يتبدل من الوجود إلى العدم . لكنا فرضنا أنه حصل هذا التبدل ، فيثبت أن الآن الموجود إذا عدم فلا بد وأن يكون له أول عدم يكون معدوماً فيه. وهذا هو المطلوب.
For if it is said: Your statement that the ceasing of the moment either occurs instantaneously or gradually. We say: Here is a third division: that the ceasing of the moment occurs in all the time after it. It cannot be said: Our discussion is not about the ceasing of the moment, but rather our discussion is about its first ceasing, and it is known that its first ceasing does not occur in all the time after it; indeed its first ceasing does not occur in all the time after it. And when this division becomes invalid, it is established that its first ceasing either occurs instantaneously or occurs gradually, and then the mentioned discussion returns. For we say: What is meant by the first ceasing of the moment? If you mean by it that its ceasing has a first, then this is conceded. But why is it not permissible to say: Its first ceasing is its existence, so the occurrence of that moment and its subsistence is precisely the first time of its ceasing? On this assumption, the succession of moments does not follow. And if you mean by it that its ceasing has a first in which it is non-existent, then this is denied. So what is the proof that its ceasing has a first in which it is non-existent? And that is invalid, because this statement is only sound on the doctrine of the succession of moments; if we establish the succession of moments based on this premise, circularity would follow, and it is invalid. This is the utmost that can be raised as an objection to the proof [that we mentioned on establishing the succession of moments (1)]. And the answer: We claim that the ceasing of the moment must have a first in which that moment is non-existent. The proof of that: This moment existed and then became non-existent. So its ceasing occurred after it was not. So this ceasing has a first without fail, and that ceasing occurs in that first without fail. Otherwise, it would not have changed from existence to non-existence. But we assumed that this change occurred. So it is established that the existing moment, when it ceases, must have a first ceasing in which it is non-existent. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It begins with an objector raising a third possibility: the ceasing of the moment could occur over all subsequent time, not instantaneously or gradually. The author immediately clarifies that the discussion is about the first ceasing, which cannot occur over all subsequent time. Thus the third option is invalid, returning to the dichotomy. Then the objector questions the meaning of 'first ceasing': if it means the moment's existence itself is its first ceasing, then the succession of instants does not follow. If it means a first moment of non-existence, that would presuppose the succession and cause circularity. The author's answer is that the moment must have a first ceasing because it changes from existence to non-existence; otherwise, no change occurred. This establishes a first moment of non-existence, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
أول العدم (awwal al-‘adam)
دور (dawr)
تتالي الآنات (tatālī al-ānāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objection successfully refutes the succession of instants.
Correction: The answer shows that the objection either concedes the point (if first ceasing is existence) or leads to circularity (if first ceasing is non-existence), and the author's independent proof establishes the first ceasing.

Checkpoint
What is the key objection raised against the first argument?
A) The moment's ceasing could occur gradually.
B) The first ceasing might be the moment's existence itself.
C) The moment is divisible.
D) The moment never ceases.

Key Takeaway
The objection about the first ceasing either concedes the premise or leads to circularity; the author's proof establishes a first instant of non-existence as necessary for change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last experience a real change from a bad habit to repentance? Was there a definite first moment you stopped that sin, or did you just hope it would fade away? If there was no first moment, did the change really happen?
?
The objector tried to avoid the clear conclusion by redefining terms. Where in your own life do you intellectually dodge the clear conclusion about your need to change, by redefining what repentance or obedience means? What would a definite first step of genuine transformation look like for you today?
?
The passage argues that change necessitates a first moment of non-existence. Think of a sin you still struggle with. If you are serious about leaving it, what is stopping you from setting a specific moment after which it will be non-existent in your life? Will you set that moment now?

The Objection to the First Proof for Succession of Instants
Objection to the first proof for succession of instants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's challenge to the first proof for the succession of instants, including the three possible ways the non-existence of an instant could occur and the refinement about the first non-existence.

Core Idea
Objectors question the assumption that non-existence must be either instantaneous or gradual.

In this passage, the author presents an objection against his own first proof. The objector challenges the exhaustive division of the non-existence of an instant into either all at once or gradually, proposing a third possibility. This forces a deeper analysis of what 'first non-existence' means.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. The objector first suggests two options, then a third. Notice how the author responds.
The third division is that non-existence occurs in all subsequent time.
2
Understand the refinement about 'first non-existence'
The objector refines the discussion to the first non-existence of the instant. See how this eliminates the third division and returns to the original two options.
The objector argues that the first non-existence cannot be in all subsequent time.
3
Analyze the two interpretations of 'first non-existence'
The objector then questions what is meant by 'first non-existence': either a first moment of non-existence (which could be the existence itself) or a moment in which it is non-existent. The latter leads to circularity.
The objector tries to prove that the second interpretation leads to a vicious circle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قولكم عدم الآن ، إما أن يقع دفعة ، أو على التدريج . قلنا : ههنا قسم ثالث وهو أن يقع عدم الآن في جميع الزمان الذي بعده . لا يقال : ليس كلامنا في عدم الآن ، بل كلامنا في أول عدمه ، ومن المعلوم أن أول عدمه لا يحصل في جميع الزمان الذي بعده، فإن أول عدمه لا يكون حاصلا في جميع الزمان الذي بعده ولما بطل هذا القسم ثبت أن أول عدمه ، إما أن يحصل دفعة ، وإما أن يحصل على التدريج، وحينئذ يعود الكلام المذكور . لأنا نقول : ما المراد بأول عدم الآن ؟ إن أردتم به أن لعدمه أولاً ، فهذا مسلم . لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن أول عدمه هو وجوده ، فحصول ذلك الآن وثبوته هو بعينه أول الزمان عدمه، وعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم تتالي الآنات . وإن أردتم به أن لعدمه أولاً يكون هو فيه معدوماً فهذا ممنوع . فالدليل على أن لعدمه أولاً هو فيه معدوماً؟ وذلك باطل لأن هذا الكلام إنما يصح على القول بتتالي الآنات ، فلو أثبتنا تتالي الآنات بناء على هذه المقدمة ، لزم الدور ، وأنه باطل . فهذا أقصى ما يمكن إيراده من السؤال على الحجة التي ذكرناها على إثبات تتالي الآنات . والجواب : إنا ندعى أن عدم الآن ، لا بد وأن يحصل له أول يكون ذلك الآن معدوماً فيه والدليل عليه : أن هذا الآن كان موجوداً ثم صار معدوماً. فعدمه حدث بعد أن لم يكن فهذا العدم له أول لا محالة، وذلك العدم يكون حاصلاً في ذلك الأول لا محالة. وإلا فهو بعد لم يتبدل من الوجود إلى العدم . لكنا فرضنا أنه حصل هذا التبدل ، فيثبت أن الآن الموجود إذا عدم فلا بد وأن يكون لعدمه أول ، يكون هو فيه معدوماً ، وإذا ثبت هذا المذكور ، وظهر لزوم الحجة المذكورة ، بحيث لا تقبل الدفع . اندفع السؤال والله أعلم
If it is said: Your statement 'the non-existence of an instant' either occurs all at once or gradually. We say: There is a third division, which is that the non-existence of the instant occurs in all of the time after it. It is not said: Our speech is not about the non-existence of the instant, but our speech is about its first non-existence, and it is known that its first non-existence does not obtain in all of the time after it, for its first non-existence does not occur in all of the time after it. When this division is invalidated, it is established that its first non-existence either occurs all at once or occurs gradually, and then the aforementioned speech returns. For we say: What is meant by 'first non-existence of an instant'? If you mean that it has a first non-existence, this is conceded. But why is it not permissible to say that its first non-existence is its existence? So the occurrence and subsistence of that instant is precisely the first time of its non-existence, and on this estimation the succession of instants does not follow. And if you mean that it has a first [moment] in which it is non-existent, this is prevented. So what is the proof that it has a first [moment] in which it is non-existent? That is invalid because this speech is only correct on the doctrine of the succession of instants. So if we establish the succession of instants based on this premise, circularity would follow, and it is invalid. So this is the utmost that can be advanced as an objection against the proof which we have mentioned for establishing the succession of instants. And the answer: We claim that the non-existence of an instant must have a first [moment] in which that instant is non-existent. The proof of it is that this instant existed and then became non-existent. So its non-existence occurred after it was not, so this non-existence necessarily has a first, and that non-existence necessarily occurs in that first. Otherwise, it has not yet changed from existence to non-existence. But we have assumed that this change occurred. So it is established that when an existing instant ceases, it must have a first [moment] of its non-existence in which it is non-existent. And when this is established, it appeared the necessity of the aforementioned proof such that it cannot be repelled. The objection is repelled. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The author first presents an objection against the first proof for the succession of instants. The objector challenges the claim that the non-existence of an instant must occur either at once or gradually, suggesting a third possibility: that the non-existence occurs in all of the subsequent time. The author responds by distinguishing between the first moment of non-existence and the whole period; the objector then argues that perhaps the 'first non-existence' is actually the existence itself, so that the instant's existence and its first non-existence coincide, avoiding the need for another instant. The author's answer reaffirms that non-existence is a new occurrence, so it must have a first moment in which the instant is non-existent, otherwise the change from existence to non-existence did not happen. The author concludes that the objection is repelled and the proof stands.

Key Terms
تتالي الآنات
العدم
أول العدم
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector's third division (non-existence in all subsequent time) is a valid alternative.
Correction: The first non-existence cannot be spread over all subsequent time; that would mean the instant never truly ceases. The discussion is about the first moment of non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's third division regarding the non-existence of an instant?
A) It occurs all at once.
B) It occurs gradually.
C) It occurs in all of the subsequent time.
D) It occurs in another instant.

Key Takeaway
The objector forces a precise definition of 'first non-existence', but their own argument leads to a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a truth that challenges your comfort or convenience, do you find yourself inventing 'third options' to escape the conclusion, like the objector did? What would it cost you to accept the truth instead?
?
Is there a belief you hold dear that you have never examined with precise definitions? Are you afraid that if you defined your terms clearly, your belief might collapse? What are you protecting?
?
In your own life, can you identify a time when you used circular reasoning to defend a position—assuming what you were trying to prove? How did that feel, and what would it take to break that cycle?

The Author's Answer and Rebuttal
Author's answer proving first moment of non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that the non-existence of an instant must have a first moment, and why this defeats the objection.

Core Idea
Non-existence is a change; change necessitates a first moment of non-existence.

Having presented the objection, the author now provides a definitive answer. He argues that the non-existence of an instant is a change from existence to non-existence, and such a change necessarily has a first moment of non-existence. This reaffirms the original proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's claim and proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The author asserts that non-existence must have a first moment. Follow the proof carefully.
The proof is based on the fact of change.
2
Understand the necessity of a first moment
The author argues that if there were no first moment of non-existence, the change from existence to non-existence would not have occurred. This is a logical necessity.
Without a first moment, the instant would still exist.
3
See how this repels the objection
The author concludes that the original proof is sound and the objection is repelled. Reflect on why the objection fails.
The objector's alternative interpretations cannot account for the necessity of a first moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every instant of your life—every moment you are alive—is a gift that will have a first moment of non-existence. Are you living in such a way that you truly appreciate the fleeting nature of each now, or do you take it for granted?
?
The author proves that non-existence is a real event with a definite beginning. How does this truth confront the way you hold onto things: your time, relationships, or youth? Are you treating them as permanent or as fleeting instants?
?
If the first moment of non-existence is inevitable for every existing instant, then your current 'now' is already heading toward its end. What will you do with this moment to ensure it is not wasted?

Second Proof for Succession of Instants
Second proof for succession based on impossibility of time ending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof for the succession of instants, based on the impossibility of time ending, and how it differs from the first proof.

Core Idea
After an instant ends, something must follow—or time would vanish.

We have already established that the present instant is indivisible. Now, consider what happens when it passes. Does something else arise or not? This simple question leads to a powerful proof for the succession of instants. The author presents a clear disjunction and shows that only one outcome is rationally possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation carefully. Note the logical structure of the argument.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the disjunction
The key move is the disjunctive question: after the present instant ceases, does something else occur or not? Identify both branches and their consequences.
3
Understand the second branch's absurdity
The second branch (nothing follows) leads to the claim that time ends, which is deemed false. Why is it false? Reflect on the concept of time and its continuity.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Contrast with the first proof
The author explicitly distinguishes this proof from the previous one. Note the difference: the first proof focused on how the instant ceases, while this proof does not.
Understanding the difference deepens your grasp of both proofs.
5
Summarize the argument in your own words
Write a brief summary of the proof, ensuring you capture the disjunction and the conclusion. Check that your summary aligns with the text.

الحجة الثانية على أن القول بتالي الآنات هو الحق : أن نقول : قد دللنا على أن الآن الحاضر لا يقبل القسمة . فنقول : إذا عدم ذلك الآن ، فهل حصل عقيبه شيء آخر يكون هو أيضاً حاضراً أو لم يحصل ؟ فإن كان الأول فالذي حصل عقيبه يكون هو أيضاً حاضراً ، فيكون غير منقسم ، فيلزم تتالي الآنات وهو المطلوب. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ لم يحصل عقيب ذلك الآن شيء آخر البتة ، فكان هذا قولاً بانقطاع الزمان وانتهائه إلى العدم ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فالزمان غير منقطع . واعلم أن الفرق بين هذا الدليل وبين ما قبله : أنا في الدليل الأول قلنا : عدم الآن الأول لا بد وأن يقع في آن آخر ملاصق للآن الأول. وفي هذا الدليل ما تعرضنا لكيفية عدم الآن الأول ، بل ادعينا أن عقيب انقضاء الآن الأول ، لا بد وأن يحضر أن آخر من غير حاجة إلى بيان أن الأول كيف عدم .
The second proof that the doctrine of the succession of instants is the truth: We say: We have demonstrated that the present instant is indivisible. So we say: When that instant ceases, does something else occur after it which is also present or not? If the first, then what occurs after it is also present, so it is indivisible, so the succession of instants follows, and it is the desired conclusion. If the second, then nothing at all occurs after that instant, so this would be a statement of the cessation of time and its termination into non-existence, and it is known that that is false. So time is not broken. And know that the difference between this proof and the previous one is that in the first proof we said: The non-existence of the first instant must occur in another instant adjacent to the first instant. In this proof we did not address how the first instant ceases, but we asserted that after the passing of the first instant, another must appear without needing to explain how the first ceases.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the succession of instants. It begins with a premise established earlier: the present instant is indivisible. The argument proceeds by disjunction: after the present instant ceases, either something else follows or it does not. If something else follows, that thing must also be an indivisible present instant, leading to succession. If nothing follows, time would end, which is absurd. Therefore succession is necessary. The author then clarifies the difference between this proof and the first: the first proof argued that the non-existence of an instant must occur in an adjacent instant, whereas this proof does not address how the instant ceases but simply argues that something must follow its cessation, regardless of the manner of its ceasing.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر
القسمة
انقطاع الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof assumes what it tries to prove, but it actually starts from the premise of indivisibility of the present instant.
Correction: The proof does not assume succession; it only assumes indivisibility and then shows that succession must follow logically from the disjunction.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if after the present instant nothing occurs, what would follow?
A) The succession of instants.
B) The cessation of time.
C) The existence of an adjacent instant.
D) The indivisibility of time.

Key Takeaway
The succession of instants is necessary because time cannot end; after each instant, another must follow, or time would cease to exist, which is impossible.

The Third Proof for Succession of Instants
Third proof against continuous time based on non-existence of past and future.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the succession of instants, which shows that time cannot be continuous because the past and future are non-existent.

Core Idea
The present instant cannot connect non-existent past and future; time is a series of successive instants.

This proof addresses a key problem: if time were continuous, the present moment would have to bridge the past (non-existent) and the future (non-existent). But connecting two non-existents is absurd. The solution is that time is composed of discrete instants. Read the passage to see how the author presents this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Pay attention to the key terms: الماضي, المستقبل, معدومان.
2
Identify the two divisions of time
The author divides time into past and future. Note that both are described as non-existent.
3
Understand the absurd consequence
If the present instant connects these two non-existents, then one non-existent is connected to another by an existent, which is rationally absurd.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Examine the alternative
The author then offers the resolution: time is composed of successive instants, so the present instant simply follows the previous one without needing to connect non-existents.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why the succession model avoids the absurdity: each instant is independent and related only by sequence, not by connection to non-existence.
This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن أحد قسمي الزمان هو الماضي . والقسم الثاني هو المستقبل . ولا شك في أن الماضي والمستقبل معدومان . فلو قلنا : إن هذا الآن الحاضر يوجب اتصال أحد قسمي الزمان بالقسم الآخر ، لكان هذا القول معناه أحد المعدومين متصل بالمعدوم الثاني بطرف موجود . وهذا الكلام بعيد عن العقل . أما إذا قلنا : الزمان مركب من آنات متتالية ، ودفعات متعاقبة ، فالإشكال زائل . لأن الذي وجد لا تعلق له بما عدم إلا بمعنى كونه حاصلا بعد زوال الأول وفنائه . وذلك محال استبعاد فيه والله أعلم
The third proof: One of the two divisions of time is the past, and the second division is the future. There is no doubt that the past and future are non-existent. If we say that this present instant necessitates the connection of one division of time with the other division, this statement would mean that one of the two non-existents is connected to the second non-existent by an existent extremity. This speech is far from reason. But if we say that time is composed of successive instants and consecutive moments, the problem vanishes. Because that which exists has no relation to what is non-existent except in the sense of its occurring after the removal and passing of the first. And that is impossible and involves absurdity. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof based on the nature of past and future. The author notes that time has two parts: past (non-existent) and future (non-existent). The present instant is existent. If we claim that the present instant connects these two non-existent parts, then we would have a connection between two non-existents by an existent, which is rationally absurd. The alternative is that time is composed of successive instants, so that the connection is simply the fact that one instant occurs after another. This resolves the absurdity, because the present instant is not required to connect non-existents; rather, it is just one of a series.

Key Terms
الماضي
المستقبل
معدومان
الاتصال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The present instant can connect past and future into a continuous flow.
Correction: The author shows that past and future are non-existent, so connecting them via the present is absurd; time is not continuous but composed of successive instants.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea that the present instant connects past and future?
A) Because the present instant is divisible.
B) Because past and future are non-existent.
C) Because time is infinite.
D) Because the present instant is not part of time.

Key Takeaway
The third proof demonstrates that time cannot be continuous because the present instant cannot connect the non-existent past and future; instead, time must be a series of successive instants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own mind instinctively imagines time as a river flowing from past to future. But this lesson reveals that the past and future are non-existent — only the present instant is real. Are you willing to let go of your deeply held intuition of time as continuous? What would that mean for how you view your past sins and future plans?
?
If the present instant has no real connection to the past except succession, then your past is truly gone — it cannot affect your present except by your choice to remember it. How much of your present peace is stolen by a non-existent past? Will you decide now to stop letting what has perished rule what exists?
?
The proof argues that the present cannot connect non-existents. So between you and your previous bad habit lies only the removal of that instant — no chain, no inevitability. What non-existent past event have you been treating as an unbreakable link, when in fact it is simply gone? Can you now see that the present instant is free to follow only what you choose?

Razi's Proof of Essential Difference of Time Parts
Razi argues that temporal parts must be essentially different due to implications of priority and posteriority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that temporal parts must differ in essence, and why priority and posteriority cannot be accidental.

Core Idea
Time parts are essentially distinct, otherwise we fall into infinite regress.

Razi presents a crisp logical proof that the parts of time cannot be identical in essence. He argues that the priority of one temporal part over another must be an essential property, not an accidental one, to avoid logical absurdities. This establishes that time is composed of discrete, successively ordered moments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the compressed logical style.
2
Identify key premises
Find the rejection of accidental priority and the assertion that priority and posteriority must be essential.
3
Trace the logical inference
Follow how essential priority leads to unique necessary attributes and then to difference in essences.
4
Understand the conclusion
See that the argument concludes time parts are discrete successive nows.
Contrast with a continuous view of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون متقدماً على الآخر، ولم لا يجوز أن يكون تقدم الجزء المتقدم على الجزء المتأخر أمراً حصل له بسبب غيره، وإلا لزم التسلسل أو الدور. بل لا بد وأن يكون الجزء المحكوم عليه بكونه متقدماً يكون متقدماً لذاته. والجزء المحكوم عليه بكونه متأخراً يكون متأخراً لذاته. وإذا كان كذلك كان كل جزءين يمكن فرضهما في الزمان، فإنه حصل لكل واحد منهما لازم يلزمه لذاته، وذلك اللازم يكون ممتنع الحصول في حق الجزء الآخر، لكن من المعلوم أن اختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف ماهيات الملزومات. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن تكون الأجزاء المفترضة في الزمان مختلفة في الحقائق والماهيات. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، لم يعقل من اتصالها إلا تواليها وتعاقبها بحيث يكون كل واحد منفصلاً في نفسه عن الآخر، ولا معنى لتتالى الآنات إلا ذلك.
It is prior over the other. Why is it not permissible that the priority of the prior part over the posterior part be something that occurs to it due to something else, otherwise infinite regress or circularity would follow. Rather it is necessary that the part judged to be prior be prior in its essence, and the part judged to be posterior be posterior in its essence. If that is so, then any two parts that can be assumed in time, each one of them necessarily has a necessary attribute that accompanies it in its essence, and that necessary attribute is impossible to obtain with respect to the other part. But it is known that difference of necessary attributes indicates difference of the essences of the things they accompany. If the matter is so, then it is necessary that the parts assumed in time be different in realities and essences. If the matter is so, nothing intelligible from their connection except their succession and alternation such that each one is separate in itself from the other, and there is no meaning to the succession of nows except that.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof that temporal parts must be essentially different. It is an argument by contradiction: if the priority of one part over another were accidental (due to something else), that would lead to infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, priority and posteriority must be essential properties of the parts. Consequently, each part has an essential attribute that cannot belong to any other part, proving that the parts differ in essence. Thus, time parts are discrete and successive, establishing the concept of 'succession of nows' (tatali al-anat).

Key Terms
تقدم
تأخر
لازم
ماهية
تتالي الآنات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are merely accidental relations between temporal parts.
Correction: Razi shows that if they were accidental, it would lead to infinite regress or circularity; therefore they must be essential properties of the parts themselves.

Checkpoint
What would follow if the priority of one temporal part over another were due to something else?
A) Infinite regress or circularity
B) Time would be continuous
C) Parts would be identical
D) Nothing would change

Key Takeaway
Temporal parts must be essentially different to avoid logical absurdities, making time a discrete succession of moments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your life is essentially distinct from the next, then who are 'you' that continues? Are you a different person at each instant? How does that affect your sense of responsibility for your past actions before Allah? Write down one sin you committed years ago—do you still bear its burden even though the 'you' of that moment is essentially gone?
?
The proof shows that priority and posteriority cannot be accidental but must be essential. Reflect on your own priorities: Is your faith an accidental part of your life or essential to who you are? If it were accidental, what would that mean for your accountability? Commit to one change today that makes your faith more essential.
?
Razi argues that time is a succession of discrete nows, not a continuous flow. Think about your prayer: Do you rush through it as a blur of continuous movement, or do you pause at each moment, aware that each 'now' is separate and significant before Allah? Starting with your next prayer, try to experience each moment of it as a distinct, essential act of worship.
