The Fifth Proof: Time is Composed of Indivisible Nows
Razi gives a deductive proof that past and future time must be composed of indivisible presents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's deductive argument that past and future time must be composed of indivisible present moments (nows).

Core Idea
Past and future time are made of present moments, each an indivisible now.

In this proof, Razi builds on the premise that only present existence is real. He argues that for something to be called 'past,' it must have been present. Since the past as a whole cannot be present simultaneously, each part must have been individually present. This leads to the conclusion that time is composed of indivisible nows. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical connectors: 'we say', 'because', 'but', 'therefore'.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument rests on the premise that anything past must have been present. Find where Razi states this and why he says it cannot be pure non-existence.
The term 'pure non-existence' (عد محض) is crucial.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the deduction: Past/future time must have been/will be present. It cannot be present in its entirety (all at once), so each part must be present individually. Each present moment is an indivisible now. Therefore time is composed of indivisible nows.
Try to restate each step in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة في إثبات تتالي الآنات: أن نقول: إنا قد دللنا على أن كل ما صدق عليه حكم العقل بأنه ماضي أو مستقبل، فإنه لا بد وأن يصدق حكم العقل على مجموعه، أو على كل واحد من أجزاء ذلك المجموع بأنه كان حاضراً لأن الشيء الذي لم يكن حاضراً لا بمجموعه ولا بأجزاء مجموعه كان ذلك عدماً محضاً. والباقي على العدم المحض لا يمكن أن يحكم عليه بكونه ماضياً، فالزمان الماضي والمستقبل لا بد وأن يصدق عليه حكم العقل بأنه كان حاضراً، أو سيصير حاضراً، إما بمجموعه، أو بكل واحد من أجزائه، لكن كون الزمان حاضراً بحسب مجموعه محال. فلم يبق إلا أن يكون حاضراً بحسب كل واحد من أجزائه، وكل ما كان حاضراً فهو الآن الذي لا ينقسم، ينتج: أن الزمان الماضي والمستقبل مركب من حضورات غير منقسمة، وتلك الحضورات آنات لا تنقسم، فالزمان مركب من آنات لا تنقسم. وهو المطلوب.
The fifth proof for establishing the succession of nows: we say: We have demonstrated that everything to which the judgment of reason applies as past or future, it is necessary that the judgment of reason applies to its entirety, or to every one of the parts of that entirety, as having been present. Because that which has not been present, neither in its entirety nor in its parts, is pure non-existence. And what remains pure non-existence cannot be judged as being past. So past and future time must be judged by reason as having been present or as going to be present, either in its entirety or in each one of its parts. But for time to be present in its entirety is impossible. Therefore it remains that it is present with respect to each one of its parts. And everything that is present is the indivisible now. So the conclusion is that past and future time are composed of indivisible presences, and those presences are indivisible nows. Thus time is composed of indivisible nows. That is what was sought.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage presents a deductive proof: Past and future time are judged by reason. For something to be past, it must have been present. If time were not composed of present parts, it would be non-existent. The entire past or future cannot be present at once. Hence, each part must have been present individually. Each present moment is an indivisible now. Therefore, time is composed of indivisible nows. This proof relies on the premise that only present existence is real, and the past and future derive reality from past present moments.

Key Terms
ماضي
مستقبل
حاضر
عد محض
آن لا ينقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Past and future have a continuous, independent existence.
Correction: The proof shows that past and future derive their reality from having been present in discrete nows; they are not independently existing continua.

Checkpoint
Why can't time be present in its entirety?
A) Because past and future are not present together
B) Because time is infinite
C) Because the mind cannot grasp it
D) Because time is subjective

Key Takeaway
Time is composed of indivisible nows because every moment of past and future must have been present at its own time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each moment of your past only exists because it was a present moment that is now gone, how does that reshape your view of the sins you committed? Are you clinging to them as if they still exist, or have you let them go as pure non-existence, relying on Allah's forgiveness?
?
This proof teaches that only the present is real, while the past and future are just collections of present moments. When you worry about the future or regret the past, are you treating those as realities that exist now? How can you train yourself to focus on the only moment that actually is: this current now?
?
Razi says that what hasn't been present in any of its parts is pure non-existence. When you think about your actions in the past, do you realize they were real only in their own now? How does that understanding motivate you to make the most of each passing now for the sake of Allah?

Three Rational Proofs for the Existence of the Present Moment
Three proofs for the existence of the present moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three rational proofs that establish the existence of the present moment as a necessary reality.

Core Idea
The present moment is real—without it, nothing exists and nothing can be known.

In this passage, the author presents three distinct arguments to prove that the present moment (al-aan) must exist. Each proof approaches the same conclusion from a different angle: the impossibility of total non-existence, the conceptual dependency of past and future on a present, and the immediate evidence of perception and inner experience. The author also notes that the existence of the present is actually self-evident, but he provides these proofs for thoroughness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's structure become familiar.
Notice the three 'وجوه' (proofs) clearly marked with 'الأول' and 'الثاني' and 'الثالث'.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that denying the present moment leads to the absurd conclusion that nothing exists at all. Follow the logic: past and future are non-existent; if the present were also non-existent, then existence would be impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof is based on the concepts of past and future. These concepts only make sense if there is a present moment that was or will be present. If the present were impossible, then nothing could be past or future.
This proof shows the conceptual necessity of the present.
4
Understand the third proof
The third proof appeals to direct perception and inner experiences such as pains and pleasures. These are perceived only in the present, not in the past or future. Therefore, the present must exist.
This is an empirical argument from common experience.
5
Reflect on the author's concluding remark
The author states that the existence of the present is actually self-evident, but he provides proofs for emphasis. Consider why a theologian might still give proofs for something obvious.
This shows the kalam method of supporting even evident truths with rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود شيء يكون حاضراً في الحال ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الماضي والمستقبل كل واحد منهما عدم محض ، فلو كان الحاضر أيضاً عدماً محضاً، لما تقرر الحصول والوجود أصلا (۱) فكان هذا قولاً بأنه لا وجود لشيء من الأشياء ، ولا حصول [ لأمر من الأمور (٣) ] وذلك جهالة . الثاني : إنه لا شك في حصول أمور يحكم العقل عليها بكونها ماضية وبكونها مستقبلة ، والمعقول من الماضي هو الذي كان حاضراً ثم مضى ، والمعقول من المستقبل هو الذي يتوقع حضوره ، ولكنه لم يحضر . فلو كان ممتنعاً ، لكانت صيرورته ماضياً ومستقبلاً أيضاً ممتنعاً . ولما لم يكن حضوره كذلك ، علمنا أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بالحال الحاضر. الثالث : إنه لا نزاع في أنا نشاهد أشياء ، مشاهدة حقيقية . ونجد من أنفسنا آلاماً ولذات . فهذا الذي نشاهده إنما نشاهده في الحال ، لأن الذي مضى ولم يبق تمتنع مشـاهـدتـه ، والذي هو مستقبل ولم يحضر تمتنع أيضاً مشاهدته. فعلمنا أن هذه الوجدانيات والمشاهدات إنما حصلت في الحال ، لا في الماضي ولا في المستقبل . واعلم : أن القول بأنه لا بد من الاعتراف يكون [ الحال (۱) ] الحاضر موجوداً ، أظهر وأجلى من أن يفتقر فيه إلى بيان وبرهان . وإنما ذكرنا هذه الوجوه مبالغة في البيان .
Know that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of something that is present in the current moment. And there are proofs for it: First: The past and future, each one of them is pure non-existence. So if the present were also pure non-existence, then occurrence and existence would not be established at all, and this would be a statement that there is no existence for anything, nor occurrence for any matter, and that is ignorance. Second: There is no doubt that there occur matters which the intellect judges as past and future. What is understood by past is that which was present and then passed, and what is understood by future is that which is expected to be present but has not yet been present. So if it (the present) were impossible, then its becoming past and future would also be impossible. Since its being present is not like that, we know that it is necessary to acknowledge the present moment. Third: There is no dispute that we truly perceive things and find in ourselves pains and pleasures. That which we perceive is only perceived in the present moment, because that which has passed and remained no longer is impossible to perceive, and that which is future and has not yet come is also impossible to perceive. So we know that these internal experiences and perceptions only occur in the present, not in the past or future. And know: The statement that it is necessary to acknowledge the present moment as existent is more apparent and clearer than to need explanation and proof. We have only mentioned these proofs for emphasis in explanation.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of the present moment (al-aan). The author begins by stating that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of something present. He then provides three proofs. The first proof argues from the non-existence of past and future: if the present were also non-existent, nothing would exist at all, which is absurd. The second proof uses the fact that we have concepts of past and future, which logically imply a prior present moment — if the present were impossible, then its becoming past or future would also be impossible. The third proof appeals to direct perception and internal experiences (pains, pleasures) which are experienced only in the present. After the proofs, the author remarks that the existence of the present moment is actually self-evident, and he provided these proofs only for thoroughness. This shows a typical kalam approach: providing rational proofs even for self-evident truths.

Key Terms
الحال
الآن
الماضي
المستقبل
العدم
الوجدانيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The proofs show that without a real present moment, nothing would exist, no event could occur, and perception would be impossible. The present must be a real entity.

Two Types of Division and the Indivisibility of the Present Moment
The author distinguishes two types of division and asserts the present moment is indivisible in the second type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of division (simultaneous and sequential) and why the present moment is indivisible in the second type.

Core Idea
Division has two faces: parts together or parts apart — the present moment is the second type's limit.

Before proving that the present moment cannot be divided, the author establishes a crucial premise: there are two fundamentally different ways a thing can be divided. This distinction is essential for understanding the nature of time, motion, and the instant we call 'now'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the examples of body and time.
2
Identify the two types of division
The passage clearly lists two ways a thing can be divided. Find the definitions and notice how they differ.
One type has parts coexisting; the other does not.
3
Analyze the examples
The author uses a body as an example of the first type and time as an example of the second. Understand why each fits its category.
Think about why a body's parts can exist together but time's parts cannot.
4
Connect to the present moment
The conclusion states the present moment is not divisible in the second way. Reflect on why that follows from the premise.
The present moment is like a point in time—no duration, so no parts.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can apply the distinction between the two types of division.
This will reinforce the key concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : في بيان أن الآن الحاضر لا يقبل القسمة : وقبل الخوض في تقرير المطلوب ، لا بد من تقديم مقدمة . وهي في بيان أن كون الشيء منقسماً [ يقع (۲) ] على وجهين : الأول : أن ينقسم الشيء بحيث يحصل لجميع أجزائه [ وأقسامه (۳) ] وجود معاً . وذلك مثل انقسام الجسم، فإنه حال انقسامه ، يكون كل واحد من أجزائه حاصلاً مع حصول الجزء الآخر المعند. والثاني : أن ينقسم الشيء بحيث يمتنع أن يحصل بعض أجزائـه حـال حصول الجزء الآخر . وهذا مثل انقسام الزمان والحركة . وذلك لأن الزمان أول اليوم إلى آخره يقبل القسمة إلى الأجزاء . ولكن أي جزء فرض ، فإنه [ يمتنع حصوله ، حال حصول الجزء الآخر منه (٤) ] والعلم بذلك ضروري بعد التأمل واستحضار تصورات هذه الالفاظ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الآن الحاضر غير قابل للقسمة بالوجه الثاني
The second issue: On explaining that the present moment is indivisible. Before delving into the exposition of what is sought, it is necessary to present a premise. And it is in explaining that the division of a thing occurs in two ways: First: That a thing is divided such that all its parts have existence together. That is like the division of a body, for when it is divided, each one of its parts occurs simultaneously with the occurrence of the other part. Second: That a thing is divided such that it is impossible for some of its parts to occur while the other part occurs. That is like the division of time and motion. That is because time from the beginning of the day to its end is divisible into parts. But whichever part is assumed, it is impossible for it to occur while the other part occurs. And knowledge of that is necessary after reflection and bringing to mind the concepts of these words. If you have understood this, we say: The present moment is not divisible in the second way.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces the second issue concerning the present moment (al-aan). Before proving the claim, the author lays out a premise about two types of division. The first type is simultaneous division, where parts exist together, exemplified by the division of a body. The second type is sequential division, where parts cannot coexist, exemplified by time and motion. The author explains that time is divisible into parts that cannot occur simultaneously — e.g., the first part of the day cannot coexist with the second part. After this premise, the author states the conclusion: the present moment is not divisible in the second way (i.e., it is not like time). The proof for this conclusion is not provided here; the passage serves as the conceptual foundation for the argument that follows.

Key Terms
القسمة
الانقسام
الجسم
الزمان
الحركة
الآن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The present moment has duration and can be divided into smaller moments.
Correction: The author shows that the present moment is not like time; it is a point-like instant that does not admit sequential division.

Checkpoint
According to the premise, which type of division applies to time?
A) The first type, where parts exist together.
B) The second type, where parts cannot coexist.
C) Both types equally.
D) Neither type.

Key Takeaway
The present moment is an indivisible instant that does not admit sequential division, unlike time which has successive parts.

The Third Proof: Observation of Pains and Pleasures Establishes the Present Moment
Sanusi gives a third argument that observed pains and pleasures are in the present moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the direct experience of pain and pleasure to prove the reality of the present moment.

Core Idea
What you feel now proves the 'now' exists.

Sanusi now turns to a very direct proof: our own experience. We all feel pains and pleasures, and we know they happen in the present. This passage shows that this self-evident truth is actually a powerful argument for the existence of the present moment, even though it is so obvious it hardly needs proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Sanusi starts with an undeniable fact (we observe things) and builds his argument.
The argument is simple but foundational.
2
Understand the logic: only the present can be observed
Sanusi says we observe things in the present because past is gone and future is not here. Identify this reasoning in the text.
This is a basic principle of perception.
3
Recognize the self-evident nature
Sanusi then says this truth is so clear it needs no proof. Why does he still mention it? Reflect on the pedagogical purpose.
He is being thorough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إنه لا نزاع في أنا نشاهد أشياء ، مشاهدة حقيقية . ونجد من أنفسنا آلاماً ولذات . فهذا الذي نشاهده إنما نشاهده في الحال ، لأن الذي مضى ولم يبق تمتنع مشاهدته ، والذي هو مستقبل ولم يحضر تمتنع أيضاً مشاهدته . فعلمنا أن هذه الوجدانيات والمشاهدات إنما حصلت في الحال ، لا في الماضي ولا في المستقبل . واعلم : أن القول بأنه لا بد من الاعتراف يكون الحال الحاضر موجوداً ، أظهر وأجلى من أن يفتقر فيه إلى بيان وبرهان . وإنما ذكرنا هذه الوجوه مبالغة في البيان .
The third: There is no dispute that we observe things, a true observation. And we find in ourselves pains and pleasures. So that which we observe, we only observe it in the present (al-ḥāl), because that which has passed and no longer remains, its observation is impossible, and that which is future and has not yet arrived, its observation is also impossible. So we know that these found things (wijdāniyyāt) and observations are only obtained in the present, not in the past nor in the future. And know: that the statement that it is necessary to acknowledge the present (al-ḥāl al-ḥāḍir) as existent is more apparent and clearer than to need explanation and proof. And we only mentioned these points in excess of clarification.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a third argument (third point) proving that the present moment (al-ḥāl) exists as a reality. Sanusi begins by stating that there is no dispute that we genuinely observe things and experience pains and pleasures. These observations and feelings are experienced only in the present moment, because the past is gone and cannot be observed, and the future has not yet come. Thus, these found experiences (wijdāniyyāt) are necessarily present occurrences. Sanusi then asserts that the need to acknowledge the existence of the present moment is so self-evident that it requires no demonstration; he only raises these points to emphasize clarity.

Key Terms
الحال (al-ḥāl, the present moment)
مشاهدة (mushāhada, observation)
وجدانيات (wijdāniyyāt, found things/experiences)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving the existence of the present moment requires a complex philosophical argument.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the present moment is self-evident through our direct experience of pain and pleasure, which can only be felt in the present.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Our direct experience of pains and pleasures in the present moment is an undeniable proof of the existence of the present.

This lesson proves that such experiences are signposts of the present's reality. The Present Moment is Indivisible: Two Types of Division
Sanusi shows the present moment cannot be divided in the second type of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the present moment cannot be divided according to the second type of division, and the distinction between simultaneous and sequential division.

Core Idea
The present moment is an indivisible point; sequential division would destroy its existence.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that 'divisibility' can mean two very different things: parts that exist together or parts that cannot coexist. This distinction is crucial before he can argue that the present moment—the now—is not divisible in the way time is. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Classify the two types of division
Read the preliminary classification carefully. Notice that the first type (simultaneous) applies to bodies, while the second (sequential) applies to time and motion.
Sanusi says knowledge of the second type is necessary after reflection.
2
Understand the second type with time
Time from start to end of day is divisible into parts, but parts cannot coexist. This is key to the argument.
3
Apply to the present moment
If the now were divided in the second way, its halves would never exist together, so the whole now would never exist. Therefore, it cannot be divided that way.
The argument uses the property of the second type: one part is absent when the other is present.
4
Grasp the contrapositive conclusion
Sanusi concludes that anything whose aggregate exists cannot be divided in the second way. This is the contrapositive of the previous statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : في بيان أن الآن الحاضر لا يقبل القسمة : وقبل الخوض في تقرير المطلوب ، لا بد من تقديم مقدمة . وهي في بيان أن كون الشيء منقسماً يقع على وجهين : الأول : أن ينقسم الشيء بحيث يحصل لجميع أجزائه وجود معاً . وذلك مثل انقسام الجسم، فإنه حال انقسامه ، يكون كل واحد من أجزائه حاصلاً مع حصول الجزء الآخر . والثاني : أن ينقسم الشيء بحيث يمتنع أن يحصل بعض أجزائه حال حصول الجزء الآخر . وهذا مثل انقسام الزمان والحركة . وذلك لأن الزمان أول اليوم إلى آخره يقبل القسمة إلى الأجزاء . ولكن أي جزء فرض ، فإنه يمتنع حصوله، حال حصول الجزء الآخر منه . والعلم بذلك ضروري بعد التأمل واستحضار تصورات هذه الألفاظ . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الآن الحاضر غير قابل للقسمة بالوجه الثاني . وذلك لأن خاصية القسمة بالوجه الثاني هو أن لا يكون أحد الجزءين موجوداً عند وجود الجزء الآخر منه ، فلو كان الآن الحاضر منقسماً على هذا الوجه ، لكان عند دخول نصفه الأول في الوجود ، لم يكن النصف الثاني منه موجوداً ، وعند دخول النصف الثاني منه في الوجود يكون النصف الأول قد فات وزال . فعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون لذلك المجموع وجود البتة . فيثبت : أن كل ما كان منقسماً على هذا الوجه لم يكن لمجموعه وجود البتة . وهذا ينعكس انعكاس النقيض، أن كل ما كان لمجموعه وجود ، فإنه لا يكون منقسماً على هذا الوجه . والله أعلم .
The second issue: On demonstrating that the present moment (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) is not divisible. Before delving into establishing the desired point, it is necessary to present a preliminary. It is on demonstrating that a thing's being divisible occurs in two ways: The first: that the thing is divided such that all its parts obtain existence together. An example of that is the division of a body, for when it is divided, each one of its parts is present along with the obtaining of the other part. The second: that the thing is divided such that it is impossible for some of its parts to obtain at the time of obtaining the other part. An example of that is the division of time and motion. That is because time from the beginning of the day to its end is divisible into parts. But whichever part is assumed, it is impossible for it to obtain at the time of obtaining the other part of it. Knowledge of that is necessary after reflection and calling to mind the conceptions of these expressions. If you know this, we say: The present moment is not divisible in the second way. That is because the property of division in the second way is that one of the two parts is not existent at the existence of the other part of it. So if the present moment were divided in this way, then at the entry of its first half into existence, its second half would not exist, and at the entry of its second half into existence, the first half would have passed away and ceased. Thus, on this assumption, that aggregate would have no existence whatsoever. So it is established: that everything that is divided in this way does not have existence for its aggregate at all. And this is converted by contraposition: that everything that has existence for its aggregate is not divided in this way. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a demonstration that the present moment (al-ān al-ḥāḍir) cannot be divided. Sanusi first provides a preliminary classification of two types of division. The first type is simultaneous division, where all parts exist together, like the parts of a physical body. The second type is sequential division, where parts cannot coexist, like parts of time or motion; when one part is present, the others are not. Sanusi then applies this to the present moment. He argues that if the now were divisible in the second way, its halves could never exist together, so the whole would never exist. Therefore, the now, which does exist as a whole, cannot be divisible in that manner. He concludes with the contrapositive: any existent whole cannot be divided in the sequential way.

Key Terms
الآن الحاضر (al-ān al-ḥāḍir, the present moment)
القسمة (al-qisma, division)
الجزء (al-juz', part)
الوجود (al-wujūd, existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The present moment is divisible like time because it is part of time.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the present moment, if divided in the sequential way time is, cannot exist as a whole; thus it is indivisible.

Checkpoint
Which type of division applies to time according to the preliminary?
A) First type: simultaneous division
B) Second type: sequential division
C) Both types
D) Neither type

Key Takeaway
The present moment is indivisible in the sequential sense because if it had parts that do not coexist, it would never have existence as a whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the present moment is an indivisible point, what does that mean for your constant worrying about past regrets or future anxieties? How can you anchor yourself in this indivisible now that alone exists?
?
Sanusi argues that if the now had sequential parts, it would never exist as a whole. In what ways do you fragment your life by dwelling on what is gone or what is yet to come, thereby never fully existing in the present?
?
The existence of the now is a whole; it cannot be split. Are you living your life as a series of fragmented moments, or do you recognize the unity of the present? What would change if you truly believed in the indivisibility of the now?

The Now as Separator and Connector
Sanusi explains the dual role of the now in continuous time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the now (al-ān) functions both as a separator and a connector in continuous time, via the analogy of a point on a line.

Core Idea
The now is both a divider of time and a bridge between past and future.

This passage from Sanusi explains a subtle point about time: the 'now' is not just a barrier separating past from future, but also a connector that links them. Using the analogy of a point on a line, Sanusi shows how the same instant can be both a separator and a connector depending on how you look at it. This idea is crucial for understanding the nature of time in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to grasp the overall argument. Let the author's own words set the context.
Notice that the passage begins by referencing Aristotle's view.
2
Understand the definition of separation
Identify the phrase 'لا معنى للفصل إلا حصول طرف بالفعل' and reflect on what it means for a continuous quantity.
Separation here means having an actual extremity, not just conceptual division.
3
Consider the example of a point on a line
Sanusi uses the point as a concrete example. Observe how a point both separates the line into segments and connects them.
The point is both an end of one segment and a beginning of another.
4
Apply the analogy to time
Replace the point with the now and the line with time. The now separates past from future but also connects them as the present moment.
This dual role is key to understanding the nature of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة في أن الآن كيف يكون فاصلا باعتبار ، وواصلا باعتبار . اعلم : أنا ذكرنا أن مذهب أرسطاطاليس وأصحابه : أن الزمان كم متصل . وإذا كان كذلك فنقول : كل كم متصل فإنه يقبل الفصل . ولا معنى للفصل إلا حصول طرف بالفعل لذلك الشيء ، وهذا يقتضي أن الزمان شيء يمكن أن يحصل له طرف بالفعل . ثم إن هذا الطرف قد يكون واصلاً وقد يكون فاصلا . وهذا الكلام إنما ينكشف بالمثال . فنقول : كل نقطة تحدث في الخط ، فإنها تكون فاصلة ، ثم بعد ذلك قد تكون واصلة ، وقد لا .
The third issue: On how the now (al-ān) is a separator in one respect and a connector in another respect. Know: We mentioned that the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers is that time is a continuous quantity (kamm muttaṣil). And if that is so, we say: Every continuous quantity is capable of separation (faṣl). And there is no meaning of separation except the obtaining of an actual extremity (ṭaraf bil-fiʿl) for that thing, and this requires that time is a thing that can have an actual extremity. Then this extremity may be a connector and may be a separator. And this statement is only disclosed by example. We say: Every point that occurs on a line, it is a separator, but then after that it may be a connector, or it may not be.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how the now (al-ān) can function both as a separator and as a connector, depending on perspective. Sanusi first invokes Aristotle's view that time is a continuous quantity (kamm muttaṣil). Then he argues that any continuous quantity admits of separation (faṣl). Separation means having an actual extremity (ṭaraf bil-fiʿl). For time, this extremity is the now. The now can act as a connector, joining past and future, and as a separator, distinguishing past from future. Sanusi clarifies this with the analogy of a point on a line: a point can separate the line into segments, but it can also be considered the end of one segment and the beginning of another, thus connecting them. The passage cuts off before completing the analogy, but the idea is clear.

Key Terms
الآن (al-ān, the now)
كم متصل (kamm muttaṣil, continuous quantity)
الفصل (al-faṣl, separation)
الطرف (al-ṭaraf, extremity)
نقطة (nuqṭa, point)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the now is only a separator, dividing time into past and future.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the now also connects past and future, just as a point on a line both separates and connects line segments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does separation (faṣl) mean for a continuous quantity?
A) The division into parts
B) The obtaining of an actual extremity
C) The connection of two parts
D) The removal of a part

Key Takeaway
The now is both a separator and a connector in time, just as a point on a line both divides and joins segments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the now—this very moment—how do you use it as a separator from past sins and a connector to future obedience? Commit to one specific action today that turns your 'now' into a bridge to draw closer to Allah.
?
Think of a recent turning point in your life. Did you see it as a separator (cutting you off from the past) or a connector (linking you to a new path)? How does Sanusi's teaching challenge you to view every difficult now as a connector to Allah's mercy? Resolve to face the next one with that perspective.
?
Your actions are like points on the line of time. How can you ensure that your next 'now' is not only an end in itself but also a connector to the akhirah? Name one precise moment tomorrow when you will consciously make the now a step toward your ultimate goal.

The Implication of Division in the Second Manner for Existence
Argues that if a composite is divisible in the second manner, it has no existence, and vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a composite divisible in the second manner cannot exist, and its contrapositive.

Core Idea
Division in the second manner implies non-existence; existence implies no such division.

In this passage, the author examines a specific type of division—where parts do not coexist—and uses the example of the present moment to show that such a composite never has existence. From this, a general principle is derived and expressed as a contrapositive. Understanding this logical relationship is key to arguments about temporality and composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: definition, example, conclusion, contrapositive.
2
Identify the definition of division in the second manner
Locate the sentence that defines this type of division. Understand what makes it different from other divisions.
The key phrase is 'لا يكون أحد الجزءين موجوداً عند وجود الجزء الآخر'.
3
Follow the argument to the conclusion
See how the author applies the definition to the 'now' and then generalizes. Why does the composite never exist? How does the contrapositive follow?
The contrapositive is stated as 'انعكاس النقيض'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأن خاصية القسمة بالوجه الثاني هو أن لا يكون أحد الجزءين موجوداً عند وجود الجزء الآخر منه، فلو كان الآن الحاضر منقسماً على هذا الوجه، لكان عند دخول نصفه الأول في الوجود، لم يكن النصف الثاني منه موجوداً، وعند دخول النصف الثاني منه في الوجود يكون النصف الأول قد فات وزال. فعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون لذلك المجموع وجود البتة. فيثبت: أن كل ما كان منقسماً على هذا الوجه لم يكن لمجموعه وجود البتة. وهذا ينعكس انعكاس النقيض، أن كل ما كان لمجموعه وجود، فإنه لا يكون منقسماً على هذا الوجه. والله أعلم.
And that is because the property of division in the second manner is that one of the two parts does not exist when the other part exists. So if the present now were divided in this manner, then when its first half enters into existence, its second half would not exist, and when its second half enters into existence, the first half would have passed and ceased. According to this estimation, that composite would have no existence at all. So it is established: that everything that is divided in this manner does not have existence for its composite. And this contraposes, that everything that has existence for its composite is not divided in this manner. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of division called 'division in the second manner' (القسمة بالوجه الثاني). The characteristic of this division is that when one part exists, the other part does not exist. The author applies this to the concept of the 'now' (الآن الحاضر) to argue that if the present moment were divisible in this way, it could not have continuous existence. Because when the first half comes into existence, the second half is absent, and when the second half comes, the first half is gone. Therefore, the composite (the whole now) would never have existence. From this, the author draws a general conclusion: anything that is divisible in this manner has no existence for its composite. Then the contrapositive (انعكاس النقيض) is stated: anything whose composite has existence cannot be divisible in this manner. The passage ends with 'والله أعلم' (God knows best).

Key Terms

وجود
انعكاس النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All types of division imply non-existence.
Correction: Only a specific type of division—where parts do not coexist—leads to non-existence. Other divisions may allow coexistence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the property of 'division in the second manner'?
A) Both parts exist simultaneously.
B) One part exists only when the other does not.
C) The composite has existence despite division.
D) The parts are inseparable.

Key Takeaway
A composite that is divisible in the second manner (where parts do not coexist) has no existence, and anything that exists cannot be divisible in that way.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own sense of self: do you experience a continuous 'I' that remains present, or is it a series of fleeting moments? If your self is divided in the second manner, what does that mean for your accountability before Allah?
?
Your relationship with Allah today: is it a composite of isolated acts of worship that do not coexist, or is it a continuous presence? If the former, is it truly existent in the sight of Allah?
?
You claim to believe in the Hereafter, but does that belief coexist with your worldly attachments? If your faith is divided in the second manner—present only when you remember, absent when you forget—does it truly exist as a composite that will save you?

The Now as Both Separator and Connector
Explains how the 'now' functions as both a divider of past and future and a connector of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the 'now' functions both as a divider between past and future and as a connector that ensures the continuity of time.

Core Idea
The 'now' separates past from future, yet paradoxically connects them in the continuous flow of time.

This passage delves into a subtle but crucial aspect of time: the nature of the 'now'. The author uses a geometric analogy of a point on a line to explain how a single limit can both divide and unite. By understanding this, we gain insight into the continuous nature of time according to Aristotelian physics.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Aristotelian view of time
Read the opening lines where the author states that time is a continuous quantity. This is the foundation for the argument.
2
Study the geometric analogy
Focus on the example of a point on a line. Understand how a single point can be both a separator (by dividing the line) and a connector (by serving as endpoint and beginning). The author presents two cases: first where the point is both, second where it is only a separator.
3
Apply the analogy to the 'now'
Now apply the reasoning to time. The 'now' is like that point: it separates past from future. But unlike a point that can be only a separator, the 'now' must also be a connector, otherwise time would cease. The author argues that it's impossible for time to cease, so the 'now' is necessarily a connector.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة في أن الآن كيف يكون فاصلا باعتبار، وواصلا باعتبار
اعلم: أنا ذكرنا أن مذهب أرسطاطاليس وأصحابه: أن الزمان كم متصل. وإذا كان كذلك فنقول: كل كم متصل فإنه يقبل الفصل. ولا معنى للفصل إلا حصول طرف بالفعل لذلك الشيء، وهذا يقتضي أن الزمان شيء يمكن أن يحصل له طرف بالفعل. ثم إن هذا الطرف قد يكون واصلاً وقد يكون فاصلاً. وهذا الكلام إنما ينكشف بالمثال. فنقول: كل نقطة تحدث في الخط، فإنها تكون فاصلة، ثم بعد ذلك قد تكون واصلة، وقد لا تكون.
أما القسم الأول: فإذا حدثت نقطة في خط بحيث لا ينفصل بسببها أحد قسمي ذلك الخط عن القسم الآخر منه، فإنه ليس هناك إلا أنه حدثت نقطة بالفعل. وتلك النقطة كما أنها نهاية لأحد قسمي ذلك الخط، فهي بعينها بداية للقسم الآخر منه. فهذه النقطة تكون فاصلة لذلك الخط باعتبار، وواصلة باعتبار آخر. أما أنها فاصلة فذلك لأن ذلك الخط كان قبل حدوث هذه النقطة فيه: خطاً واحداً، وعند حدوث هذه النقطة فيه: انقسم بقسمين. وأما أنها واصلة فلأن تلك النقطة أوجبت اتصال أحد قسمي هذا الخط بالقسم الثاني منه، فهذا هو بيان كون النقطة المعينة الواحدة فاصلة باعتبار، وواصلة باعتبار آخر.
وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أن تكون النقطة فاصلة ولا تكون واصلة، فمثاله: إذا انقطع أحد نصفي الخط عن النصف الآخر. فإن تلك النقطة التي هي نهاية أحد القسمين لم توجب اتصال ذلك القسم بالقسم الآخر.
وإذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: قد دللنا على أن الآن الحاضر لا بد وأن يفصل الماضي عن المستقبل، لأنه نهاية للماضي وبداية للمستقبل، فيكون هذا الآن فاصلاً. ولكنك قد عرفت أن الطرف الفاصل قد يكون واصلا وقد لا يكون. فنقول: هذا الآن الفاصل يستحيل أن لا يكون واصلاً، لأنه إنما لا يكون واصلا لو انقطع الزمان ولم يكن بعده شيء آخر. وذلك محال. لأنه لو عدم الزمان لكان عدمه بعد وجوده: بعدية.
The third issue concerning how the now is a separator in one respect and a connector in another respect. Know: we have mentioned that the doctrine of Aristotle and his followers is that time is a continuous quantity. If that is so, we say: every continuous quantity admits of division. And the meaning of division is nothing but the occurrence of an actual limit for that thing. And this requires that time is something that can have an actual limit. Then this limit may be a connector or may be a separator. This statement is only clarified by example. We say: every point that occurs in a line, it is a separator, then after that it may be a connector or may not be. As for the first division: if a point occurs in a line such that by it one of the two parts of that line is not separated from the other part of it, then there is nothing except that a point actually occurred. And that point, just as it is an endpoint for one of the two parts of that line, it is itself a beginning for the other part of it. So this point is a separator for that line in one respect, and a connector in another respect. As for it being a separator, that is because that line before the occurrence of this point in it was one line, and upon the occurrence of this point in it, it split into two parts. As for it being a connector, it is because that point necessitated the connection of one part of this line with the second part of it. This is the clarification of the fact that one specific point is a separator in one respect and a connector in another respect. As for the second division: that the point is a separator and not a connector, its example is when one half of the line is cut off from the other half. Then that point which is the endpoint of one of the two parts did not cause connection of that part with the other part. And when you have understood this premise, we say: we have demonstrated that the present now must separate the past from the future, because it is an endpoint for the past and a beginning for the future. So this now is a separator. But you have understood that the separating limit may be a connector or may not be. We say: this separating now is impossible to not be a connector, because it would only not be a connector if time were cut off and there were nothing after it. And that is impossible. Because if time were to cease, its ceasing after its existence would be a posteriority.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the third issue concerning the nature of the 'now' (الآن). The author begins by stating the issue: how the 'now' can be a separator (فاصل) in one respect and a connector (واصل) in another. He then recalls that according to Aristotle and his followers, time is a continuous quantity. Since a continuous quantity admits of division, and division means the occurrence of an actual limit (طرف بالفعل), time must have an actual limit. This limit (the 'now') can be a connector or a separator. The author clarifies this through an example of a point on a line. He presents two cases: first, a point that is both a separator and connector—when a point is placed on a line such that the line is divided into two parts but the point serves as both the end of one part and the beginning of the other, thus both separating and connecting. Second, a point that is only a separator—when a line is cut, the endpoint of one part does not connect to the other. Applying this to time, the author argues that the present 'now' must separate the past from the future, as it is the end of the past and the beginning of the future. However, unlike a simple point, the 'now' cannot fail to be a connector because that would require time to cease after the 'now', which is impossible—since if time ceased, its ceasing would have a posteriority, implying time continues. Thus the 'now' is necessarily both a separator and connector.

نقطة
خط
كم متصل
طرف بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'now' only separates past from future.
Correction: The 'now' also connects them, as time is continuous and the 'now' is both an end and a beginning.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the present 'now' cannot fail to be a connector?
A) Because the 'now' is an endpoint of the past.
B) Because if it were not a connector, time would cease, which is impossible.
C) Because the 'now' is a point on a timeline.
D) Because the 'now' is both a beginning and an end.

Key Takeaway
The present 'now' necessarily both separates the past from the future and connects them, ensuring the continuity of time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life in a series of 'nows'. Do you experience each moment as merely a divider between what you did and what you will do, or as a connector that gives meaning to your past and direction to your future? How can you make the present moment a genuine connection between your past repentance and your future obedience?
?
The passage argues that the now must connect because time cannot cease. Reflect on your own mortality: you know your time will end. If the now were only a separator, what would that mean for the continuity of your deeds? How does the fact that each now connects past and future challenge you to act with purpose and consistency?
?
In your daily prayers, you stand at the 'now' between the past and future of your worship. Are you allowing the present moment of prayer to separate you from your previous distractions and connect you to a future of devotion? What would change in your prayer if you truly believed it is the connector that ties your entire day together?

Objection on Temporal Priority and Its Mental Resolution
An objection that temporal priority implies simultaneity, answered by mental consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why temporal priority is considered a mental relation rather than a real attribute in Islamic theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is not a real property but a mental consideration.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection from the opponents of temporal priority and the theologian’s answer. The objection claims that priority and simultaneity cannot coexist, so temporal priority is impossible. The resolution shifts the discussion from external reality to mental consideration, showing that priority is a judgment of the mind, not an actual property of things.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first part of the passage that presents the doubt. Understand the logical argument: if something necessitates simultaneity, it cannot also cause priority. Then the conclusion that temporal priority cannot be asserted.
Notice the use of 'موجباً للمعية' and 'موجباً للتقدم'.
2
Identify the core dilemma
The objection points out that for any two things, if one is prior in time, they cannot be simultaneous. But if the cause requires simultaneity, there's a contradiction.
This is a logical puzzle.
3
Read the answer
The answer says temporal priority only exists in the mind. Consider the example: when the son exists, the mind considers the father as prior.
This shifts from real to mental.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that attributes like temporal priority are not necessarily real in the external world but can be mental constructs.
Important for understanding kalam's view on time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
1 والذي يكون موجباً للمعية (۱) يمتنع أن يكون هو بعينه موجباً للتقدم، لأن المعية منافية للتقدم. فيثبت: أنه لم يحصل لهذا المتقدم إلا هذا الزمان، ويثبت: أن كل واحد منهما ينافي حصول التقدم، فوجب أن لا يصح الحكم على الشيء بكونه متقدماً على الغير بالزمان. فهذا هو تقرير هذا الشك. وجوابه: أن التقدم بالزمان لا حصول له إلا في الأذهان. وذلك لأن عند حصول الابن يعتبر الذهن أن الأب كان موجوداً قبله. فمن هذا الاعتبار يحصل التقدم الزماني.
1. And that which necessitates simultaneity (1) cannot itself necessitate priority, because simultaneity contradicts priority. Therefore it is established: that nothing has been obtained for this prior except this time, and it is established: that each of the two prevents the obtaining of priority, so it is necessary that judgment of a thing being temporally prior to another is not valid. So this is the exposition of this doubt. And its answer: that temporal priority has no existence except in the mind. That is because when the son obtains, the mind considers that the father was existent before him. From this consideration, temporal priority obtains.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the nature of temporal priority. The objection argues that if a cause necessitates simultaneity, it cannot also necessitate priority, since simultaneity and priority are mutually exclusive. Therefore, a thing cannot be genuinely temporally prior to another. The answer resolves this by stating that temporal priority is not a real attribute but only a mental consideration: when the son comes into existence, the mind considers the father as having existed before him, thus establishing priority in thought, not in external reality.

معية
ذهن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temporal priority is a real property of things in the external world.
Correction: Temporal priority exists only in the mind as a mental consideration, not as an attribute of external objects.

Checkpoint

A) In the external world
B) In the mind
C) In the objects themselves
D) In the past

Key Takeaway
Temporal priority is a mental judgment, not a real attribute in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection on Relational Priority and Posteriority and Its Solution
An objection that priority and posteriority are relata that would coexist, answered by distinguishing aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an apparent contradiction between priority and posteriority is resolved by distinguishing different aspects of relation.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority can coexist when considered from different relational aspects.

The objection suggests that priority and posteriority, being relative terms, must coexist, yet priority seems to exclude posteriority. How can both be true? Sanusi presents this objection and then resolves it by distinguishing between the essences involved and the relational aspects. This lesson unpacks that elegant solution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the logical flow of the objection. Then read the translation.
Notice the key terms: مضافان (relata), تقدم (priority), تأخر (posteriority).
2
Work through the logical steps of the objection
Identify the three premises: priority and posteriority are relata, relata are together, priority implies not being together. See how they lead to a contradiction.
The objector uses a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Study the answer and the distinction of aspects
Read Sanusi's answer. Note how he separates the essence of the father (ذات الأب) from the relation of being prior (كونه متقدماً). The relation is simultaneous with the son's posteriority.
The key phrase is تغاير في الاعتبار (difference in consideration).
4
Apply the concept to a new example
Think of another pair of relational opposites (e.g., cause and effect) and explain how they might coexist under different aspects.
This reinforces the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني: إن التقدم والتأخر مضافان، والمضافان معاً. فالتقدم مع التأخر والمعية ينافي التقدم. والحاصل أن كونه متقدماً على الغير، يوجب كونه ذلك الغير، وكونه معه (۲) ينافي كونه متقدماً عليه. ينتج أن كونه متقدماً على الغير، ينافي كونه متقدماً على الغير، وذلك باطل. والجواب: إن ذات الأب متقدمة على ذات الابن. أما كونه متقدماً على الابن فإنه مقارن لكون الابن متأخراً عنه. ولما حصل التغاير في الاعتبار، زال التنافي.
The second question: Priority and posteriority are relata, and relata are together. So priority together with posteriority and simultaneity contradicts priority. The summary: his being prior to another necessitates that he is that other, and his being with him (2) contradicts his being prior to him. It follows that his being prior to another contradicts his being prior to another, and that is false. The answer: The essence of the father is prior to the essence of the son. As for his being prior to the son, it is concomitant with the son's being posterior to him. When the difference in consideration occurs, the contradiction is removed.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage addresses a second objection concerning the mutual relations of priority and posteriority. The objection holds that since priority and posteriority are relative terms (relata), they must coexist, but priority also excludes posteriority, leading to a contradiction. The answer clarifies that the essence of the father is prior to the essence of the son in one respect, while the relation 'being prior to the son' is simultaneous with the relation 'being posterior to him' from the son's perspective. Since the considerations differ, the apparent contradiction disappears.

Key Terms
مضافان
تقدم
تأخر
تغاير في الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Priority and posteriority are mutually exclusive in every aspect.
Correction: They can coexist when considered under different aspects, as the father's essence is prior while the relation of priority coexists with the son's posteriority.

Checkpoint
How does the answer resolve the contradiction in the second objection?
A) By denying that priority and posteriority are relata
B) By showing they refer to different aspects of the same relation
C) By asserting that only one exists
D) By claiming the objection is based on false premises

Key Takeaway
Priority and posteriority are not mutually exclusive when considered from different relational aspects; the father is prior in essence while the relation of being prior coexists with the son's posteriority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a contradiction between your faith and your feelings, do you look for different aspects to reconcile them, or do you let the tension go unresolved? What specific aspect might you be conflating today?
?
The father-son example shows that priority in essence does not prevent simultaneity in relation. Is there a relationship in your life (with Allah, parents, or others) where you need to distinguish between essence and relation to avoid false conflict?
?
This lesson teaches that logical distinctions can resolve contradictions. Where have you been too quick to call two truths incompatible? Will you commit to finding the different considerations before dismissing a difficult belief?

Future and Past Attributes of Non-Existent Things
Objection and answer on attributes of non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why descriptions like 'will exist' and 'was existent' are not real attributes and how the objection of an existent attribute in a non-existent subject is resolved.

Core Idea
Future and past descriptions are mental constructs, not real attributes inhering in non-existents.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if a non-existent thing can be described as 'future' or 'past', those descriptions seem to be real attributes that come and go. But if they are real, they must inhere in something, yet the subject at the time is non-existent, leading to an impossibility. The author presents the objection and then offers an answer that reinterprets these descriptions as mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of objection and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection's two parts
Notice the objection uses two examples: futureness before existence and pastness after nonexistence. Both argue that an existent attribute would inhere in a non-existent subject.
Each example ends with 'محال' (impossible).
3
Analyze the proposed answer
The answer first mentions the view that the subject is a mental image (صورة ذهنية), which is existent. Then it critiques that such an image is always present, not past or future.
Look for the shift from 'من الناس من قال' to 'ولقائل أن يقول'.
4
Understand the final conclusion
The passage concludes that 'past' and 'future' are not existent attributes. This resolves the impossibility.
The phrase 'الأقرب أن يقال' signals the final position.
5
Reflect on the key insight
Consider why descriptions like 'future' are not real properties but mental constructs. How does this preserve the principle that attributes cannot inhere in non-existents?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث: المعدوم قبل دخوله في الوجود يصدق عليه أنه سيوجد، ثم إذا صار موجوداً، فإنه يزول عنه وصف أنه سيوجد، فهذا وصف قد عدم بعد حصوله، فيكون وصفاً موجوداً. لكنه إنما حصل قبل دخوله في الوجود، فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالموصوف المعدوم، وأنه محال. وأيضاً: أنه إذا وجد وحضر، ثم فني وعدم بعد ذلك، فإنه يحصل له وصف كونه ماضياً. وهذا الوصف مغاير لتلك الماهية المخصوصة. بدليل: أن الماهيات المختلفة قد تكون مشتركة في صيرورتها ماضية، والشيء الواحد يتوارد عليه وصف كونه مستقبلاً وحاضراً وماضياً. وكل ذلك يدل على أن وصف كونه ماضياً مغاير لتلك الذات المخصوصة. وهذا الوصف لا شك أنه وصف موجود بدليل أنه حصل بعد أن لم يكن حاصلاً، لكن هذا الوصف إنما يحصل للشيء بعد صيرورته معدوماً، فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم المحض، وهو محال. والجواب: من الناس من قال: المحكوم عليه بأنه سيوجد، أو بأنه كان موجوداً: هو الصورة الذهنية الحاضرة في العقل. وهي موجودة لا معدومة. ولقائل أن يقول: الصورة الذهنية حاضرة، والحاضر من حيث إنه حاضر لا يكون ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً. والحاصل: أن المحكوم عليه بأنه ماضي وبأنه مستقبل، إما أن يكون موجوداً حاضراً أو لا يكون. فإن كان حاضراً لم يكن ماضياً ولا مستقبلاً، وإن لم يكن حاضراً كان معدوماً في الحال. ووصف كونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً موجود في الحال. فيلزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم المحض والنفي الصرف. وهو محال. والأقرب أن يقال: كونه ماضياً ومستقبلاً ليسا صفتين موجودتين والله أعلم.
The third question: The non-existent before entering into existence is truly described as that it will exist, then when it becomes existent, the description that it will exist disappears from it, so this is a description that has become non-existent after its occurrence, so it is an existent description. But it occurred before entering into existence, so it necessitates the existence of an existent attribute in a non-existent subject, and that is impossible. Also: that if it exists and is present, then perishes and becomes non-existent after that, then it acquires the description of being past. This description is different from that specific essence. Evidence: that different essences may share in becoming past, and one thing undergoes the descriptions of being future, present, and past. All of that indicates that the description of being past is different from that specific essence. This description is undoubtedly an existent description because it occurred after it was not occurring, but this description only occurs to the thing after it becomes non-existent, so it necessitates the existence of an existent attribute in pure non-existence, and that is impossible. The answer: Some people said: The subject of judgment that it will exist, or that it was existent, is the mental image present in the mind. And it is existent, not non-existent. But it can be said: The mental image is present, and the present insofar as it is present is neither past nor future. The summary: The subject of judgment that it is past and that it is future, either it is existent and present or it is not. If it is present, it is not past nor future, and if it is not present, it is non-existent in the present. And the description of being past and future is existent in the present. So it necessitates the existence of an existent attribute in pure non-existence and pure negation. And that is impossible. And the closest thing to say is: Being past and future are not two existent attributes, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection concerning attributes of non-existent things, specifically the descriptions 'will exist' (future) and 'was existent' (past). The objection argues that such descriptions are real and existent, yet they apply to things that are non-existent at the time of description, leading to the impossible case of an existent attribute inhering in a non-existent subject. The answer first mentions a view that the subject of judgment is the mental image, which is existent. However, a counterpoint notes that the mental image is present and thus cannot be past or future. The conclusion is that such descriptions are not real attributes but mental considerations, and the closest position is that 'past' and 'future' are not existent attributes. This resolves the paradox.

Key Terms
معدوم
صفة موجودة
صورة ذهنية
ماضٍ
مستقبل
حال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'past' and 'future' are real properties of objects rather than mental constructs.
Correction: The passage concludes that being past and future are not two existent attributes; they are mental considerations that do not necessitate inheritance in a non-existent subject.

Checkpoint
What is the final position given in the answer regarding the descriptions 'past' and 'future'?
A) They are real attributes existing in the mind
B) They are real attributes existing in external objects
C) They are not existent attributes
D) They are attributes of the soul

Key Takeaway
The descriptions 'past' and 'future' are not real attributes but mental constructs, so no impossibility arises from their application to non-existent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that 'past' and 'future' are not real attributes but mental constructs. Think of a past regret that still pains you. Can you see how the pain is attached to a mental construct rather than a real property of something that no longer exists? Will you consciously remind yourself of this when the regret surfaces again?
?
You often worry about what will happen tomorrow, treating 'will exist' as a real attribute of something that doesn't exist yet. How many of your current anxieties are built on this mental construct? What would change if you truly internalized that the future is not a real attribute inhering in anything?

Context: Philosophers on the Eternity of Time
Classification of philosophers and their epistemic approaches.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two groups of philosophers who affirm the eternity of time and the basis of their claim.

Core Idea
Philosophers are split: some claim self-evidence, others rely on argument.

This passage sets the stage by showing that while all philosophers agree time is eternal, they differ on how we know this. One group claims it is self-evident, another proves it rationally. This classification helps us see the structure of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus
Notice the first sentence: philosophers agree on eternity, theologians on temporality. This frames the whole discussion.
The agreement is broad but not universal.
2
Distinguish the two factions
Read the description of each faction: one relies on self-evident knowledge, the other on reasoned proofs.
The first faction is the focus of the following arguments.
3
Understand the claim of self-evidence
The first faction says the intellect cannot conceive time's non-existence. That is their starting point.
They will give three reasons to support this claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفق جمهور الفلاسفة على قدم الدهر والمدة . واتفق المتكلمون على حدوثه . أما الأولون فهم فريقان : منهم من زعم أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنه لا يقبل العدم البتة ، ولا يتمكن العقل من تصور رفعه وعدمه . ومنهم من أثبت قدمه بالحجة والدليل أما الطائفة الأولى : فقالوا : إنما قلنا : إن العلم الضروري حاصل بامتناع عدمه [ وارتفاعه (١) ] لوجوه : الأول : إنا متى اعتقدنا أنه كان معدوماً ثم حدث ، فلا بد وأن نعتقد عدماً مستمراً من الأزل إلى وقت حدوثه ، وتعقل الدوام والاستمرار لا يمكن إلا عند إثبات مدة مستمرة ، لأنه لا معنى لذات المدة إلا ذلك الدوام السيال المتحرك (۲) فيثبت : أنه لا يمكن تصور عدمه إلا مع فرض وجوده ، وما كان كذلك ، كان عدمه ممتنعاً في العقول لذاته الثاني : إنا متى تصورنا أنه كان معدوماً . فقد تصورنا أن عدمه سابق على وجوده ، وذلك السبق لا معنى له إلا المدة والزمان. فيثبت : أن العقل لا يتمكن من تصور عدمه الثالث : إنا متى قلنا : إنه كان معدوماً أو سيصير معدوماً . فلفظ كان ، وسيكون ، لا بد وأن يشير إلى مدة ماضية ، وإلى مدة [ لاحقة ](١) آتية . وقول من يقول : إن هذا لضيق العبارة كلام فاسد، بل هذا لأجل أن العقل لا يتمكن من فرض عدمه ، والفهم لا يمكنه التعبير عن تقدير عدمه . وكل ذلك لاجل ما ادعينا أن تقدير عدمه (۲) وفرض رفعه ، كما يأباه صريح العقل والفهم .
The majority of philosophers agreed upon the eternity of time (dahr) and duration (mudda). The theologians (mutakallimun) agreed upon its temporality (huduth). As for the first group (the philosophers), they are two factions: among them are those who claim that self-evident knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) is attained that it (time) does not accept non-existence at all, and the intellect cannot conceive of its removal and non-existence. Among them are those who proved its eternity by argument and proof. As for the first faction, they said: Indeed we say that self-evident knowledge is attained of the impossibility of its non-existence [and its removal] due to aspects: The first: When we believe that it was non-existent and then occurred, we must believe in a continuous non-existence from pre-eternity to the time of its occurrence, and conceiving of continuity and duration is possible only upon establishing a continuous duration, because the meaning of duration itself is nothing but that flowing moving continuity. So it is established that its non-existence cannot be conceived except with the assumption of its existence, and whatever is like that, its non-existence is impossible in the intellect by itself. The second: When we conceive that it was non-existent, we have conceived that its non-existence preceded its existence, and that precedence has no meaning except duration and time. So it is established that the intellect cannot conceive of its non-existence. The third: When we say that it was non-existent or will become non-existent, the words 'was' and 'will be' must refer to a past duration and a future duration. The statement of one who says that this is due to narrowness of expression is corrupt speech; rather, this is because the intellect cannot assume its non-existence, and understanding cannot express the estimation of its non-existence. And all of that is due to what we claimed that the estimation of its non-existence and assumption of its removal is rejected by the clear intellect and understanding.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage classifies the philosophers who affirm the eternity of time into two groups and then presents three arguments from the first group, which claims that the eternity of time is self-evident. The author begins by stating the consensus of philosophers on the eternity of time and the consensus of theologians on its temporality. Then he divides the philosophers into two: those who hold self-evident knowledge of its eternity and those who prove it by argument. For the first subgroup, he gives three proofs. The first proof argues that if we imagine time began to exist, we must posit a continuous duration before it, which is time itself, so its non-existence presupposes its existence, making non-existence impossible. The second proof argues that the notion of temporal priority requires a duration, so conceiving time as non-existent still implies time. The third proof argues that words like 'was' and 'will be' necessarily refer to past and future durations, and the attempt to dismiss this as linguistic limitation is rejected because the intellect simply cannot assume time's non-existence. The author concludes that these arguments show the impossibility of conceiving time's non-existence is self-evident.

Key Terms
قدم الدهر
المدة
العلم الضروري
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All philosophers argue the same way for time's eternity.
Correction: The passage shows two distinct methods: self-evidence and rational proof.

Checkpoint
How many factions of philosophers are mentioned in the passage?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Philosophers are divided into two groups on how we know time is eternal: by self-evidence or by proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers claimed that the intellect cannot conceive of time not existing. But can you truly imagine a moment 'before' time began? What does that struggle reveal about the limits of your own reasoning and your reliance on God's revelation?
?
Some philosophers asserted absolute certainty about time's eternity based on self-evidence. Is there anything in your own life you hold with such certainty? Have you ever examined whether that certainty is truly self-evident or just a comfortable assumption? How would you respond if that certainty were challenged?
?
The theologians rejected the eternity of time entirely, grounding their view in divine creation. Have you ever considered what is at stake if time is eternal? Does it affect your understanding of God as the Creator of everything, including time? How does this passage push you to think more deeply about the relationship between reason and faith?

First Argument: Non-existence Presupposes Existence
First self-evident argument for the eternity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first self-evident argument for time's eternity, which shows that conceiving time as non-existent requires positing a prior duration.

Core Idea
To imagine time as non-existent, you must assume a duration, which is time itself.

The first argument uses a clever twist: if you try to think of time as beginning to exist, you must imagine a period of non-existence before it. But that period is itself a duration, which is precisely what time is. Thus, you cannot conceive time's non-existence without already assuming its existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional
Read the first sentence: if we believe time was non-existent and then occurred, we must believe in a continuous non-existence from eternity.
This is the starting premise.
2
Analyze the concept of duration
The passage says that conceiving continuity requires a continuous duration, and that duration is nothing but flowing movement.
Duration (mudda) is identified with time.
3
Reach the conclusion
Therefore, time's non-existence can only be conceived with the assumption of its existence, making its non-existence impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
You live your entire life inside time, never able to step out of it—not even in your imagination. Does this feeling of inescapability make you more aware of your absolute dependence on the One who is not bound by time? What would change if you lived today with that awareness?

Second Argument: Temporal Priority Implies Duration
Second self-evident argument for the eternity of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second self-evident argument, which shows that the very notion of 'prior non-existence' involves time.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are temporal concepts; they presuppose time.

The second argument focuses on the idea of 'prior non-existence'. If time were non-existent and then existed, then its non-existence is prior to its existence. But priority is a temporal relation—it only makes sense within time. Thus, the concept of non-existence being prior already involves time, making it impossible to conceive time as truly non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the concept of priority
Read: if we conceive time as non-existent before existence, we conceive that its non-existence preceded its existence.
This precedence is a temporal relation.
2
Identify what precedence means
The passage says precedence has no meaning except duration and time.
So any 'before' is within time.
3
Draw the conclusion
Thus, the intellect cannot conceive time's non-existence because doing so forces us to use temporal terms that presuppose time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

This passage classifies the philosophers who affirm the eternity of time into two groups and then presents three arguments from the first group, which claims that the eternity of time is self-evident. The author begins by stating the consensus of philosophers on the eternity of time and the consensus of theologians on its temporality. Then he divides the philosophers into two: those who hold self-evident knowledge of its eternity and those who prove it by argument. For the first subgroup, he gives three proofs. The first proof argues that if we imagine time began to exist, we must posit a continuous duration before it, which is time itself, so its non-existence presupposes its existence, making non-existence impossible. The second proof argues that the notion of temporal priority requires a duration, so conceiving time as non-existent still implies time. The third proof argues that words like 'was' and 'will be' necessarily refer to past and future durations, and the attempt to dismiss this as linguistic limitation is rejected because the intellect simply cannot assume time's non-existence. The author concludes that these arguments show the impossibility of conceiving time's non-existence is self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, what does 'precedence' (al-sabq) imply?
A) Priority in essence
B) Duration and time
C) A gap in existence

Key Takeaway
The concept of 'before' already requires time, so time's non-existence cannot be consistently conceived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that the very concept of 'before' forces you to admit time. Now ask yourself: When you try to imagine a moment before time existed, what does your mind actually picture? Are you smuggling in a timeline? How does that reveal your own dependence on time as a framework for thought?
?
If the intellect cannot genuinely conceive of time's non-existence, what does that mean for your attempts to think about Allah's existence 'before' creation? Does this argument challenge you to reconsider how you conceptualize eternity? What must you guard against in your own thinking?
