Third Argument: Language Reveals the Impossibility
Third self-evident argument for the eternity of time, based on language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third self-evident argument, which shows that even our language about time's non-existence presupposes its reality.

Core Idea
Words like 'was' and 'will be' necessarily reference past and future durations.

The third argument examines language. When we say 'time was non-existent' or 'will become non-existent', the words 'was' and 'will be' refer to past and future time. Some might object that this is just a limitation of language, but the passage rebuts that: it is not a linguistic quirk but a reflection of the intellect's inability to truly assume time's non-existence. The conclusion is that time's removal is rejected by clear reason and understanding.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the linguistic claim
Read: if we say time was non-existent, the word 'was' refers to a past duration.
Similarly 'will be' refers to a future duration.
2
Consider the objection
Some might say this is due to narrowness of expression. The passage rejects that as corrupt.
3
Understand the deeper reason
The passage argues it is because the intellect cannot assume non-existence, not because language is deficient.
4
Note the conclusion
Therefore, the estimation of time's non-existence is rejected by clear intellect and understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اتفق جمهور الفلاسفة على قدم الدهر والمدة . واتفق المتكلمون على حدوثه . أما الأولون فهم فريقان : منهم من زعم أن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنه لا يقبل العدم البتة ، ولا يتمكن العقل من تصور رفعه وعدمه . ومنهم من أثبت قدمه بالحجة والدليل أما الطائفة الأولى : فقالوا : إنما قلنا : إن العلم الضروري حاصل بامتناع عدمه [ وارتفاعه (١) ] لوجوه : الأول : إنا متى اعتقدنا أنه كان معدوماً ثم حدث ، فلا بد وأن نعتقد عدماً مستمراً من الأزل إلى وقت حدوثه ، وتعقل الدوام والاستمرار لا يمكن إلا عند إثبات مدة مستمرة ، لأنه لا معنى لذات المدة إلا ذلك الدوام السيال المتحرك (۲) فيثبت : أنه لا يمكن تصور عدمه إلا مع فرض وجوده ، وما كان كذلك ، كان عدمه ممتنعاً في العقول لذاته الثاني : إنا متى تصورنا أنه كان معدوماً . فقد تصورنا أن عدمه سابق على وجوده ، وذلك السبق لا معنى له إلا المدة والزمان. فيثبت : أن العقل لا يتمكن من تصور عدمه الثالث : إنا متى قلنا : إنه كان معدوماً أو سيصير معدوماً . فلفظ كان ، وسيكون ، لا بد وأن يشير إلى مدة ماضية ، وإلى مدة [ لاحقة ](١) آتية . وقول من يقول : إن هذا لضيق العبارة كلام فاسد، بل هذا لأجل أن العقل لا يتمكن من فرض عدمه ، والفهم لا يمكنه التعبير عن تقدير عدمه . وكل ذلك لاجل ما ادعينا أن تقدير عدمه (۲) وفرض رفعه ، كما يأباه صريح العقل والفهم .
The majority of philosophers agreed upon the eternity of time (dahr) and duration (mudda). The theologians (mutakallimun) agreed upon its temporality (huduth). As for the first group (the philosophers), they are two factions: among them are those who claim that self-evident knowledge (al-ilm al-daruri) is attained that it (time) does not accept non-existence at all, and the intellect cannot conceive of its removal and non-existence. Among them are those who proved its eternity by argument and proof. As for the first faction, they said: Indeed we say that self-evident knowledge is attained of the impossibility of its non-existence [and its removal] due to aspects: The first: When we believe that it was non-existent and then occurred, we must believe in a continuous non-existence from pre-eternity to the time of its occurrence, and conceiving of continuity and duration is possible only upon establishing a continuous duration, because the meaning of duration itself is nothing but that flowing moving continuity. So it is established that its non-existence cannot be conceived except with the assumption of its existence, and whatever is like that, its non-existence is impossible in the intellect by itself. The second: When we conceive that it was non-existent, we have conceived that its non-existence preceded its existence, and that precedence has no meaning except duration and time. So it is established that the intellect cannot conceive of its non-existence. The third: When we say that it was non-existent or will become non-existent, the words 'was' and 'will be' must refer to a past duration and a future duration. The statement of one who says that this is due to narrowness of expression is corrupt speech; rather, this is because the intellect cannot assume its non-existence, and understanding cannot express the estimation of its non-existence. And all of that is due to what we claimed that the estimation of its non-existence and assumption of its removal is rejected by the clear intellect and understanding.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage classifies the philosophers who affirm the eternity of time into two groups and then presents three arguments from the first group, which claims that the eternity of time is self-evident. The author begins by stating the consensus of philosophers on the eternity of time and the consensus of theologians on its temporality. Then he divides the philosophers into two: those who hold self-evident knowledge of its eternity and those who prove it by argument. For the first subgroup, he gives three proofs. The first proof argues that if we imagine time began to exist, we must posit a continuous duration before it, which is time itself, so its non-existence presupposes its existence, making non-existence impossible. The second proof argues that the notion of temporal priority requires a duration, so conceiving time as non-existent still implies time. The third proof argues that words like 'was' and 'will be' necessarily refer to past and future durations, and the attempt to dismiss this as linguistic limitation is rejected because the intellect simply cannot assume time's non-existence. The author concludes that these arguments show the impossibility of conceiving time's non-existence is self-evident.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely a linguistic trick.
Correction: The passage insists it reflects a genuine rational impossibility, not just a grammatical one.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, why does saying 'time was non-existent' not prove its non-existence?
A) Because language is misleading
B) Because the word 'was' itself implies a duration
C) Because theologians disagree
D) Because time is a substance

Key Takeaway
Our language about time's non-existence actually confirms time's reality, because temporal words require duration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself dismissing a difficult truth as 'just semantics,' what are you really protecting yourself from? Can you think of a specific belief about your own life that you have avoided examining by hiding behind linguistic skepticism?
?
This lesson argues that even the attempt to deny time's reality proves its existence. Where in your daily life do you act as if time is unreal—procrastinating, ignoring death, or living as if moments don't matter? What would it mean today to truly honor the past and future that your own speech constantly references?
?
The author calls the objection 'corrupt speech.' When have you used 'it's just language' to avoid an uncomfortable truth? Would you be willing to ask someone you trust to point out such evasions in your own words?
